Two Definitions of Dryness and Their Flaws
Two definitions of dryness and their objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of dryness and the objections raised against them.

Core Idea
Each definition of dryness either creates an intermediate state or conflates with solidity.

The commentator presents two more definitions of dryness: one based on difficulty of adhesion and separation, and another based on difficulty of shaping. Both face serious objections. Let's examine these definitions and understand why they fail to capture the essence of dryness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic for the fourth and fifth comments.
2
Analyze the first definition
The first definition defines dryness as difficulty of adhesion and separation. The objection is that this would allow an intermediate state where something is difficult in one aspect and easy in another, thus neither wet nor dry.
3
Analyze the second definition
The second definition defines dryness as difficulty of shaping and leaving that shape. The objection is that this applies to solidity as well, unless one can prove that solidity entails dryness.
4
Consider the implications
Both definitions fail to uniquely capture dryness, showing the difficulty of precise definition in natural philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فاليبوسة حينئذ هي الكيفية إلخ ) على هذا لا تكون اليبوسة من الملموسات ولا يكون الحجر يابساً ويكون النار رطباً، لأنه وإن كانت سهلة التفرق لكنها ليست عسيرة الاجتماع أو يكون واسطة ولعل هذه اليبوسة بمعنى الجفاف. فإن الجسم المبتل إذا أثر فيه الرطوبة الغريبة يصعب تفرقه ويسهل اجتماعه عما كان قبله . وإذا جف صار الأمر بالعكس.
قوله : ( في التقسيم المنسوب إلخ) لكن ذكره الإمام في فصل بيان الهشاشة واللزوجة .
قوله: (والمذكور إلخ) يعني اكتفى في تفسيرها بسهولة الانفراك وعدمها وجعل مقابل الهش واليابس، الصلب ولا يخفى أنه ليس مقابلا لهما .
قوله : ( فهي إما عسر الالتصاق والانفصال إلخ) قيل : فعلى هذا يكون بينهما واسطة إذ ما يعسر به واحد منها، ويسهل الآخر فهو لا رطب ولا يابس ولهذا قال الإمام : هذا التعريف بالصلابة أجدر .
قوله : ( أو عسر التشكل وتركه يرد على هذا التعريف بأنه صادق على الصلابة الموجودة عند الفلاسفة اللهم إلا أن يثبت استلزام الصلابة لليبوسة وإن ذلك العسر في الجسم الصلب لأجل يبوسته لا لأجل صلابته وأنى ذلك الإثبات ؟
His saying: (So dryness then is the quality etc.) According to this, dryness is not among the tangible things, and stone would not be dry, and fire would be wet, because although it is easily divisible, it is not difficult to unite, or there would be an intermediate. Perhaps this dryness means aridity. For a moist body, when affected by extraneous wetness, becomes difficult to separate and easy to unite compared to its previous state. And when it dries, the opposite occurs. His saying: (In the division attributed etc.) But the Imam mentioned it in the chapter on explaining crumbliness and viscosity. His saying: (And it is mentioned etc.) means he sufficed in its interpretation with ease of crumbling and its absence, and made the opposite of crumbly and dry [to be] solid, and it is not hidden that it is not opposite to them. His saying: (So it is either difficulty of adhesion and separation etc.) It is said: thus there would be an intermediate between them, because what is difficult in one of them and easy in the other is neither wet nor dry. For this reason the Imam said: this definition is more worthy of solidity. His saying: (Or difficulty of shaping and leaving it) This definition is objected to because it applies to solidity as it exists among the philosophers, unless the entailment of solidity for dryness is established, and that the difficulty in the solid body is due to its dryness and not due to its solidity; and how can that be proven?
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage provides a series of comments (qāla) that refine and critique the definitions of dryness. The first comment points out a problem: if dryness is defined as easy separation and difficult cohesion (as per al-Razi), then dryness would not be a tactile quality (since we feel hardness, not dryness per se), stone would not be dry (because stone is not easily separated), and fire would be wet (since fire parts easily separate but do not readily reunite). The commentator suggests that perhaps what is meant here is 'aridity' (jafāf) rather than the philosophical quality. The second comment clarifies that the Imam (al-Razi) mentioned the viscous in a different chapter. The third comment notes that in al-Mulakhkhas, the author sufficed with ease of crumbling and its opposite (solidity), but this is problematic because solidity is not the true opposite of both crumbly and dry. The fourth comment addresses the first definition (difficulty of adhesion and separation): if dryness is defined that way, there would be an intermediate state where one is difficult and the other easy, leading to something neither wet nor dry. Hence the Imam considered that the definition is more appropriate for solidity. The fifth comment critiques the second definition (difficulty of shaping): it would apply to solidity as well, unless one proves that solidity entails dryness and that the difficulty in a solid body is due to dryness, not solidity—something very hard to prove.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that dryness is simply the opposite of wetness defined by adhesion.
Correction: The passage shows that defining dryness by difficulty of adhesion alone is insufficient because it creates intermediates.

Checkpoint
What is the objection to defining dryness as difficulty of adhesion and separation?
A) It makes everything either wet or dry
B) It creates an intermediate state
C) It is the same as solidity
D) It cannot be proven

Key Takeaway
Defining dryness by a single property such as adhesion or shaping is problematic; precise definitions require avoiding intermediates and conflations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new theological term, do you automatically assume its definition is complete? How does this lesson's exposure of flawed definitions challenge you to be more critical? Identify one concept in your aqeedah that you might have defined too simplistically, and commit to studying it more deeply.
?
This lesson shows that a definition can accidentally apply to something else. In your own life, is there a quality (like patience, trust, or gratitude) that you might be confusing with a similar but different quality (like passivity, laziness, or mere optimism)? How can you distinguish them using the method of checking for counterexamples or intermediate cases?

Classification of Bodies: Solid, Crumbly, Dry, and Viscous
Classifies bodies into solid, crumbly, dry, and viscous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how continuous bodies are classified into solid, crumbly, dry, and viscous, and why viscosity is a temperamental mixture rather than a simple quality.

Core Idea
Viscosity is a temperamental mixture of wet and dry, not a simple quality.

This passage begins by sorting continuous bodies based on whether they crumble easily. From there, it distinguishes three main types—solid, crumbly, and dry—and then introduces viscosity as a composite quality. Understanding these basic categories is essential for grasping later discussions about physical substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the author moves from the broadest division to specific types.
2
Identify the four body types
Find the terms for solid, crumbly, dry, and viscous. Note that solid is the non-crumbling type, while crumbly and dry are subtypes of crumbling.
Crumbly is composed of solid parts with weak welds; dry is itself like those welds.
3
Understand viscosity as a mixture
Focus on the definition of viscosity: easy to shape, hard to separate, composed of wet and dry. Contrast it with the crumbly (hard to shape, easy to separate).
The retention comes from the dry, yielding from the wet.
4
Reflect on the opposite relationship
The crumbly is opposite to the viscous due to predominance of dry and weak mixing. This shows how qualities result from proportions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأجسام المتصلة ما ينفرك بسهولة ومنها ما ليس كذلك. والثاني هو الصلب والأول على قسمين، أحدهما أن يكون الجسم مركباً من أجزاء صغار لا يقوى الحس على إدراك كل واحد منها منفرداً، ويكون كل واحد منها صلباً عسر الانفراك، ولكنها متصلة بلحامات سهلة الانفراك وهو الهش وثانيهما أن يكون الجسم في طبيعة تلك اللحامات وهو اليابس، واعلم أن اللزوجة كيفية مزاجية لا بسيطة فإنَّ اللزج هو الذي يسهل تشكيله بأي شكل أريد ويعسر تفريقه، بل يمتد متصلاً فاللزج مركب من رطب ويابس شديدي الالتحام والامتزاج جداً، فاستمساكه من اليابس وإذعانه من الرطب والهش يقابل اللزج فهو الذي يصعب تشكيله ويسهل تفريقه وذلك بسبب غلبة اليابس فيه وقلة الرطب مع ضعف الامتزاج وهاهنا أبحاث تناسب ما نحن فيه. الأول في بيان البلة والجفاف. فنقول : إنَّ لنا جسماً رطباً ومبتلاً ومنتقعاً، فالرطب هو الذي تكون صورته النوعية مقتضية لكيفية الرطوبة المفسرة بما تقدم، والمبتل هو الذي التصق بظاهره ذلك الجسم الرطب والمنتقع هو الذي نفذ ذلك الرطب في عمقه، وأفاده ليناً . فالبلة هو الجسم الرطب الجوهر إذا أجري على ظاهر جسم آخر، والجفاف عدم البلة عن شيء هي من شأنه. وقد يطابق كل واحد من الرطوبة والبلة الآخر. الثاني أنَّ اللطافة تطلق بالاشتراك على معان أربعة . الأول رقة القوام وهي المقتضية لسهولة قبول الأشكال وتركها . الثاني قبول الانقسام إلى أجزاء صغيرة جداً.
قوله : ( واعلم أنَّ اللزوجة) هذا هو المذكور في الشفاء، ولعل هذا الاختلاف مبني على الاختلاف في تفسير الرطوبة. إذ لا بد فيها من الرطوبة فما ذكر في المتن بناء على تفسيرها بسهولة الالتصاق وما في الشفاء على تفسيرها بسهولة قبول الأشكال.
قوله : ( هو الذي يكون إلخ) سواء كان بسيطاً كالماء أو مركباً كالغصن العاري.
قوله : (هو الذي التصق إلخ) ويقال على ما يشمل المنتقع وهو المترطب بالرطوبة الغريبة على ما في الشفاء.
قوله : ( وهي المقتضية إلخ فيه أنها لو كانت مقتضية لكانت هي الرطوبة ولكانت النار أرطب من الماء والهواء، فالواجب إسقاطه كما في الشفاء.
قوله : ( وذلك بسبب غلبة اليابس) أما إذا كان الهش مركباً من يابس كثير ورطب قليل وقد تقدم أن اليابس سهل الانفراك بجميع أجزائه فمعنى ما مر من أن سهولة الانفراك في الهش لأجل لحامات سهلة الانفراك بين أجزاء صلب عسير الانفراك، فليتأمل .
قوله : ( والمبتل هو الذي التصق بظاهره ذلك الجسم الرطب وقد يقال : المبتل أيضاً لما نفذ في عمقه ذلك الجسم الرطب كما يقال له المنتقع صرح به في المباحث المشرقية .
Among continuous bodies are those that crumble easily and those that do not. The second is the solid. The first is of two kinds: one is that the body is composed of small parts that the sense cannot perceive each one individually, and each one of them is solid and difficult to crumble, but they are connected by welds that are easy to crumble, and that is the crumbly. The second is that the body is in the nature of those welds, and that is the dry. Know that viscosity is a temperamental quality, not simple. For the viscous is that which is easy to shape into any desired form and difficult to separate, rather it stretches continuously. So the viscous is composed of wet and dry that are very strongly joined and mixed. Its retention is from the dry and its yielding is from the wet. The crumbly is opposite to the viscous: it is that which is difficult to shape and easy to separate, due to the predominance of the dry in it and the scarcity of the wet with weak mixing. Here are discussions appropriate to our topic. The first: on clarifying moisture and aridity. We say: we have a wet body, a moistened body, and a soaked body. The wet is that whose specific form necessitates the quality of wetness interpreted as above. The moistened is that to whose surface that wet body adheres. The soaked is that into whose depth that wetness penetrates and renders it soft. So moisture (balla) is the wet substance when it is run over the surface of another body. Aridity (jafāf) is the absence of moisture from something that is capable of it. Each of wetness and moisture may correspond to the other. The second: that subtlety (laṭāfa) is applied by homonymy to four meanings. The first: thinness of consistency, which necessitates ease of accepting shapes and leaving them. The second: acceptance of division into very small parts. His saying: (Know that viscosity) This is what is mentioned in al-Shifā', and perhaps this difference is based on the difference in interpreting wetness. For it must involve wetness; so what is mentioned in the text is based on interpreting it as ease of adhesion, and what is in al-Shifā' is based on interpreting it as ease of accepting shapes. His saying: (He who is etc.) whether it is simple like water or composite like a bare branch. His saying: (He who adhered etc.) and it is said to include the soaked, which is moistened with extraneous wetness according to al-Shifā'. His saying: (And it is necessitating etc.) In it, that if it were necessitating, it would be wetness, and fire would be wetter than water and air; so it must be dropped as in al-Shifā'. His saying: (And that is due to predominance of the dry) As for if the crumbly is composed of much dry and little wet, and it has preceded that the dry is easy to crumble in all its parts, so the meaning of what passed that the ease of crumbling in the crumbly is due to welds that are easy to crumble between solid parts that are difficult to crumble, so let it be considered. His saying: (And the moistened is that to whose surface that wet body adheres, and it may be said: the moistened also for that into whose depth that wet body penetrates, as it is said for the soaked, as stated in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya.)
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage begins by classifying continuous bodies into those that crumble easily and those that do not. The non-crumbling are solid (ṣulb). The crumbling are of two types: the crumbly (hashsh), composed of small solid parts connected by weak welds, and the dry (yābis), which is itself like those welds. Viscosity (luzūja) is then explained as a temperamental quality—a strong mixture of wet and dry, making it easy to shape and hard to separate. The crumbly is the opposite: hard to shape and easy to separate due to predominance of dry. The passage then turns to 'moisture' (balla) and 'aridity' (jafāf). Wet (raṭb) is defined by essence; moistened (mubtall) by surface adhesion; soaked (muntaqiʿ) by deep penetration. Moisture (balla) is the wet substance applied to another, and aridity is its absence. The second topic is 'subtlety' (laṭāfa), which has four homonymous meanings: thinness of consistency (ease of shaping), and acceptance of division into very small parts (the second meaning introduced here). The commentary clarifies that the definition of viscosity in al-Shifāʾ differs due to different interpretations of wetness. It also notes that 'moistened' can include the soaked. The commentary flags a problem with the first meaning of subtlety: if thinness necessitates ease of shaping, then fire would be wetter than water, so that meaning must be dropped. Finally, the commentator reflects on the composition of the crumbly: if it has much dry, but dry is defined as easy to crumble, then the earlier explanation (that crumbliness comes from weak welds between solid parts) needs careful consideration.

Key Terms
الأجسام المتصلة
الصلب
الهش
اليابس
اللزوجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Viscosity is a simple quality like others.
Correction: Viscosity is a temperamental quality resulting from a strong mixture of wet and dry.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the opposite of the viscous (lazj)?
A) Solid
B) Dry
C) Crumbly (hashsh)
D) Wet

Key Takeaway
Continuous bodies are classified into solid, crumbly, dry, and viscous, with viscosity understood as a temperamental mixture of wet and dry.

What Is Kalam?

Definitions of Moisture, Aridity, and Subtlety
Defines moisture, aridity, and subtlety with their subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinctions between wet, moistened, and soaked bodies, and the two meanings of subtlety (laṭāfa) as thinness of consistency and divisibility.

Core Idea
Wetness differs by depth: surface adhesion (moistened) vs. deep penetration (soaked).

This passage continues the discussion of bodies by clarifying the concepts of moisture and aridity, then turns to subtlety. The author distinguishes three degrees of wetness—wet by essence, moistened by surface contact, and soaked by penetration. Subtlety is introduced as a homonym with four meanings, of which the first two are given.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three terms for wetness: raṭb, mubtall, muntaqiʿ.
2
Distinguish the three types of wet bodies
Wet (raṭb) is wet by essence; moistened (mubtall) has wetness on its surface; soaked (muntaqiʿ) has wetness penetrating inside.
These are not synonyms but distinct categories.
3
Define moisture and aridity
Moisture (balla) is the wet substance applied to another’s surface; aridity (jafāf) is the absence of such moisture from something capable of it.
Wetness and moisture may sometimes correspond.
4
Understand subtlety’s first two meanings
Subtlety (laṭāfa) is a homonym with four meanings. The first is thinness of consistency (easy to shape and leave shapes). The second is acceptance of division into very small parts.
Only two meanings are mentioned here; the rest are left for another context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأجسام المتصلة ما ينفرك بسهولة ومنها ما ليس كذلك. والثاني هو الصلب والأول على قسمين، أحدهما أن يكون الجسم مركباً من أجزاء صغار لا يقوى الحس على إدراك كل واحد منها منفرداً، ويكون كل واحد منها صلباً عسر الانفراك، ولكنها متصلة بلحامات سهلة الانفراك وهو الهش وثانيهما أن يكون الجسم في طبيعة تلك اللحامات وهو اليابس، واعلم أن اللزوجة كيفية مزاجية لا بسيطة فإنَّ اللزج هو الذي يسهل تشكيله بأي شكل أريد ويعسر تفريقه، بل يمتد متصلاً فاللزج مركب من رطب ويابس شديدي الالتحام والامتزاج جداً، فاستمساكه من اليابس وإذعانه من الرطب والهش يقابل اللزج فهو الذي يصعب تشكيله ويسهل تفريقه وذلك بسبب غلبة اليابس فيه وقلة الرطب مع ضعف الامتزاج وهاهنا أبحاث تناسب ما نحن فيه. الأول في بيان البلة والجفاف. فنقول : إنَّ لنا جسماً رطباً ومبتلاً ومنتقعاً، فالرطب هو الذي تكون صورته النوعية مقتضية لكيفية الرطوبة المفسرة بما تقدم، والمبتل هو الذي التصق بظاهره ذلك الجسم الرطب والمنتقع هو الذي نفذ ذلك الرطب في عمقه، وأفاده ليناً . فالبلة هو الجسم الرطب الجوهر إذا أجري على ظاهر جسم آخر، والجفاف عدم البلة عن شيء هي من شأنه. وقد يطابق كل واحد من الرطوبة والبلة الآخر. الثاني أنَّ اللطافة تطلق بالاشتراك على معان أربعة . الأول رقة القوام وهي المقتضية لسهولة قبول الأشكال وتركها . الثاني قبول الانقسام إلى أجزاء صغيرة جداً.
قوله : ( واعلم أنَّ اللزوجة) هذا هو المذكور في الشفاء، ولعل هذا الاختلاف مبني على الاختلاف في تفسير الرطوبة. إذ لا بد فيها من الرطوبة فما ذكر في المتن بناء على تفسيرها بسهولة الالتصاق وما في الشفاء على تفسيرها بسهولة قبول الأشكال.
قوله : ( هو الذي يكون إلخ) سواء كان بسيطاً كالماء أو مركباً كالغصن العاري.
قوله : (هو الذي التصق إلخ) ويقال على ما يشمل المنتقع وهو المترطب بالرطوبة الغريبة على ما في الشفاء.
قوله : ( وهي المقتضية إلخ فيه أنها لو كانت مقتضية لكانت هي الرطوبة ولكانت النار أرطب من الماء والهواء، فالواجب إسقاطه كما في الشفاء.
قوله : ( وذلك بسبب غلبة اليابس) أما إذا كان الهش مركباً من يابس كثير ورطب قليل وقد تقدم أن اليابس سهل الانفراك بجميع أجزائه فمعنى ما مر من أن سهولة الانفراك في الهش لأجل لحامات سهلة الانفراك بين أجزاء صلب عسير الانفراك، فليتأمل .
قوله : ( والمبتل هو الذي التصق بظاهره ذلك الجسم الرطب وقد يقال : المبتل أيضاً لما نفذ في عمقه ذلك الجسم الرطب كما يقال له المنتقع صرح به في المباحث المشرقية .
Among continuous bodies are those that crumble easily and those that do not. The second is the solid. The first is of two kinds: one is that the body is composed of small parts that the sense cannot perceive each one individually, and each one of them is solid and difficult to crumble, but they are connected by welds that are easy to crumble, and that is the crumbly. The second is that the body is in the nature of those welds, and that is the dry. Know that viscosity is a temperamental quality, not simple. For the viscous is that which is easy to shape into any desired form and difficult to separate, rather it stretches continuously. So the viscous is composed of wet and dry that are very strongly joined and mixed. Its retention is from the dry and its yielding is from the wet. The crumbly is opposite to the viscous: it is that which is difficult to shape and easy to separate, due to the predominance of the dry in it and the scarcity of the wet with weak mixing. Here are discussions appropriate to our topic. The first: on clarifying moisture and aridity. We say: we have a wet body, a moistened body, and a soaked body. The wet is that whose specific form necessitates the quality of wetness interpreted as above. The moistened is that to whose surface that wet body adheres. The soaked is that into whose depth that wetness penetrates and renders it soft. So moisture (balla) is the wet substance when it is run over the surface of another body. Aridity (jafāf) is the absence of moisture from something that is capable of it. Each of wetness and moisture may correspond to the other. The second: that subtlety (laṭāfa) is applied by homonymy to four meanings. The first: thinness of consistency, which necessitates ease of accepting shapes and leaving them. The second: acceptance of division into very small parts. His saying: (Know that viscosity) This is what is mentioned in al-Shifā', and perhaps this difference is based on the difference in interpreting wetness. For it must involve wetness; so what is mentioned in the text is based on interpreting it as ease of adhesion, and what is in al-Shifā' is based on interpreting it as ease of accepting shapes. His saying: (He who is etc.) whether it is simple like water or composite like a bare branch. His saying: (He who adhered etc.) and it is said to include the soaked, which is moistened with extraneous wetness according to al-Shifā'. His saying: (And it is necessitating etc.) In it, that if it were necessitating, it would be wetness, and fire would be wetter than water and air; so it must be dropped as in al-Shifā'. His saying: (And that is due to predominance of the dry) As for if the crumbly is composed of much dry and little wet, and it has preceded that the dry is easy to crumble in all its parts, so the meaning of what passed that the ease of crumbling in the crumbly is due to welds that are easy to crumble between solid parts that are difficult to crumble, so let it be considered. His saying: (And the moistened is that to whose surface that wet body adheres, and it may be said: the moistened also for that into whose depth that wet body penetrates, as it is said for the soaked, as stated in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya.)
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage begins by classifying continuous bodies into those that crumble easily and those that do not. The non-crumbling are solid (ṣulb). The crumbling are of two types: the crumbly (hashsh), composed of small solid parts connected by weak welds, and the dry (yābis), which is itself like those welds. Viscosity (luzūja) is then explained as a temperamental quality—a strong mixture of wet and dry, making it easy to shape and hard to separate. The crumbly is the opposite: hard to shape and easy to separate due to predominance of dry. The passage then turns to 'moisture' (balla) and 'aridity' (jafāf). Wet (raṭb) is defined by essence; moistened (mubtall) by surface adhesion; soaked (muntaqiʿ) by deep penetration. Moisture (balla) is the wet substance applied to another, and aridity is its absence. The second topic is 'subtlety' (laṭāfa), which has four homonymous meanings: thinness of consistency (ease of shaping), and acceptance of division into very small parts (the second meaning introduced here). The commentary clarifies that the definition of viscosity in al-Shifāʾ differs due to different interpretations of wetness. It also notes that 'moistened' can include the soaked. The commentary flags a problem with the first meaning of subtlety: if thinness necessitates ease of shaping, then fire would be wetter than water, so that meaning must be dropped. Finally, the commentator reflects on the composition of the crumbly: if it has much dry, but dry is defined as easy to crumble, then the earlier explanation (that crumbliness comes from weak welds between solid parts) needs careful consideration.

Key Terms
البلة
الجفاف
الرطب
المبتل
المنتقع
اللطافة
رقة القوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moistened and soaked are the same thing.
Correction: Moistened refers to surface adhesion only, while soaked involves deep penetration and softening.

Checkpoint
What is aridity (jafāf) defined as in the passage?
A) Absence of wetness
B) Absence of moisture from something that is capable of it
C) Dryness of the surface
D) Lack of any liquid

Key Takeaway
Wetness ranges from essential wetness (raṭb) to surface moisture (mubtall) to deep soaking (muntaqiʿ), and subtlety has two meanings: thinness of consistency and divisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you merely 'moistened' with faith—having it only on your surface—or has it penetrated your depths to 'soak' your heart and render it soft and living?
?
The passage defines subtlety as an acceptance of division into very small parts. In what ways is your own soul divided into fragments—distracted, pulled in many directions—preventing you from being a single, unified servant of Allah?
?
Aridity is the absence of moisture from something capable of it. What is the 'moisture' of your spiritual life that has become absent? What one concrete act will you do today to bring it back?

Defining Wetness, Moisture, Saturation, and Dryness
Defines key terms for wetness and dryness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of wet, moistened, saturated, wetness, and dryness as distinguished by the author.

Core Idea
Wetness is a quality; moisture and saturation are modes of contact.

This passage establishes the technical vocabulary for discussing physical properties of bodies in kalam. The author distinguishes between the internal quality of wetness and the external states of being moistened (surface contact) or saturated (depth penetration). Understanding these distinctions is essential for later arguments about bodies and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Focus on the sequence of definitions.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms رطب (wet), مبتل (moistened), منتقع (saturated), بلة (wetness), and جفاف (dryness) in the text.
Note that each term has a distinct definition.
3
Understand 'wet' vs 'moistened'
The wet body has wetness as a quality of its form, while the moistened body has that wet body adhered to its surface only.
4
Understand 'saturated'
Saturation means the wetness penetrates into the depth and gives softness, unlike mere surface contact.
5
Define wetness and dryness
Wetness is the wet substance applied to another surface; dryness is the absence of wetness from a thing that normally has it.
Note that wetness and moisture sometimes correspond.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول في بيان البلة والجفاف. فنقول : إنَّ لنا جسماً رطباً ومبتلاً ومنتقعاً، فالرطب هو الذي يكون صورته النوعية مقتضية لكيفية الرطوبة المفسرة بما تقدم، والمبتل هو الذي التصق بظاهره ذلك الجسم الرطب والمنتقع هو الذي نفذ ذلك الرطب في عمقه، وأفاده ليناً . فالبلة هو الجسم الرطب الجوهر إذا أجري على ظاهر جسم آخر، والجفاف عدم البلة عن شيء هي من شأنه. وقد يطابق كل واحد من الرطوبة والبلة الآخر.
The first [research] is on explaining wetness and dryness. So we say: Indeed we have a wet body, a moistened body, and a saturated body. The wet is that whose specific form necessitates the quality of wetness interpreted as previously mentioned. The moistened is that to whose surface that wet body has adhered. The saturated is that into whose depth that wetness has penetrated and given it softness. So wetness is the wet substance when it is applied to the surface of another body, and dryness is the absence of wetness from a thing that is naturally disposed to it. And sometimes each of wetness and moisture corresponds to the other.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a definitional passage. The author defines four key terms: wet (رطب), moistened (مبتل), saturated (منتقع), wetness (بلة), and dryness (جفاف). He begins by stating the topic: explaining wetness and dryness. Then he defines 'wet' as a body whose specific form (صورة نوعية) necessitates the quality of wetness, referring to a prior definition. 'Moistened' means that a wet body has adhered to the surface of another body. 'Saturated' means that the wetness has penetrated into the depth of the body, giving it softness. Then he defines 'wetness' itself as the wet substance when it is applied to the surface of another body. 'Dryness' is the absence of wetness from something that is naturally disposed to have it. Finally, he notes that sometimes 'wetness' and 'moisture' correspond to each other, implying they can be used interchangeably in some contexts. The definitions are precise and grounded in physical interaction.

Key Terms
رطب
مبتل
منتقع
بلة
جفاف
صورة نوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'wet' and 'moistened' are synonyms.
Correction: The author distinguishes them: 'wet' refers to the internal quality of a body, while 'moistened' refers to a surface contact with a wet body.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between a 'moistened' body (مبتل) and a 'saturated' body (منتقع)?
A) They are synonyms with no difference.
B) Moistened means wetness has penetrated the depth; saturated means wetness is only on the surface.
C) Moistened means wetness is on the surface; saturated means wetness has penetrated the depth and given softness.
D) Moistened means the body is wet by nature; saturated means wetness is accidental.

Key Takeaway
Wetness is an internal quality; moistened and saturated describe different degrees of contact with that wetness.

The Four Meanings of Subtlety and Density
Enumerates the four meanings of subtlety and their opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct meanings of the term 'latafah' (subtlety) and how 'kathafah' (density) is its opposite.

Core Idea
Subtlety is not one concept but four—each with a clear opposite.

In physical discussions, the term 'subtlety' (لطافة) can refer to different properties. The author carefully distinguishes four meanings, each with a specific opposite in density (كثافة). This lesson breaks down these meanings so you can recognize which nuance is intended in various contexts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage aloud or silently. Notice the structure: it begins by stating that latafah has four homonymous meanings, then lists them, and concludes with kathafah.
The term 'بالاشتراك' indicates polysemy.
2
Identify the four meanings
List the four meanings: (1) thinness of consistency, (2) divisibility into very small parts, (3) rapid susceptibility from a contactant, (4) transparency.
Each meaning is a separate concept.
3
Understand the opposites
Note that density (kathafah) is used for the opposites of these four meanings. For example, the opposite of thinness is thickness, of divisibility is indivisibility, etc.
The opposites are not spelled out individually but are implied.
4
Test your understanding
Try to explain each meaning in your own words without looking. Then check the checkpoint question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني أنَّ اللطافة تطلق بالاشتراك على معان أربعة . الأول رقة القوام وهي المقتضية لسهولة قبول الأشكال وتركها . الثاني قبول الانقسام إلى أجزاء صغيرة جداً. الثالث سرعة التأثر عن الملاقي. الرابع الشفافية والكثافة تطلق على مقابلات هذه المعاني .
The second [research]: That subtlety (al-latafah) is used homonymously for four meanings. The first: thinness of consistency, which necessitates ease of accepting forms and leaving them. The second: acceptance of division into very small parts. The third: rapid susceptibility from a contactant. The fourth: transparency. And density (al-kathafah) is used for the opposites of these meanings.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration. The author states that the term 'subtlety' (لطافة) is a polysemous term (بالاشتراك) with four distinct meanings. He lists them: (1) thinness of consistency, which entails ease of receiving and abandoning shapes; (2) being divisible into very small parts; (3) being quickly affected by something that contacts it; (4) transparency. Then he notes that 'density' (كثافة) is used for the opposites of these four meanings. The passage thus provides a clear taxonomy of how these terms are used in physical discussions.

Key Terms
لطافة
كثافة
رقة القوام
شفافية
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'latafah' has only one meaning.
Correction: The passage clarifies it is used for four distinct concepts.

Checkpoint
How many meanings does 'latafah' (subtlety) have according to this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Subtlety (latafah) has four distinct meanings, and density (kathafah) refers to their opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the four meanings of subtlety—flexibility, being analyzable, quick to be affected, or transparency—do you find most lacking in your own heart when it comes to receiving divine truth? Will you ask Allah to cultivate that quality in you today?
?
Think of a time when you were 'dense'—resistant to change, slow to be affected by good advice, or opaque about your faults. How can you use the four opposite meanings of subtlety to become more open to Allah's guidance starting this week?
?
If your soul were described by these four meanings, would it be subtle or dense? Commit to one specific action you will take today to increase your soul's 'transparency' before Allah and others.

Two Opinions on the Essence of Wetness
Presents two views on whether wetness' essence differs across substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that scholars debated whether the wetness of different substances is essentially the same or different, and that Imam al-Razi considered both views plausible.

Core Idea
The wetness of water, oil, and mercury may be essentially different or the same — both views are possible.

This passage from a classical theological text examines a subtle metaphysical question: is the wetness we perceive in water, oil, and mercury one and the same quality, or does it differ in essence across substances? Two opposing positions are presented, each with its own reasoning. Imam al-Razi's conclusion reminds us that some questions in kalam remain open.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read both the Arabic and English versions of the passage without analyzing deeply. Let the structure and key terms settle in your mind.
Notice the technical terms: رطوبة, ماهية, جنس, نوع.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find where the first opinion ends and the second begins. Pay attention to how they are introduced: 'زعم بعضهم' and 'وزعم آخرون'.
The first opinion claims essential difference; the second claims unity of essence.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each
The first opinion leads to wetness being a genus with species. The second opinion attributes differences to mixture of dry and wet. Compare the logic.
Imam al-Razi's statement is the third part.
4
Reflect on Imam al-Razi's conclusion
He says both opinions are possible (محتمل). Why might he leave the matter open? Consider the nature of such metaphysical debates in kalam.
This shows that not all questions have a settled answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : زعم بعضهم أن رطوبة الماء مخالفة بالماهية لرطوبة الدهن المخالفة لرطوبة الزئبق. فالرطوبة جنس تحتها أنواع وزعم آخرون أن ماهيتها واحدة بالنوع والاختلاف، بسبب اختلاط اليابس بالرطب . قال الإمام الرازي: كلا القولين محتمل .
The third [research]: Some claimed that the wetness of water is essentially different from the wetness of oil, which is different from the wetness of mercury. So wetness is a genus under which there are species. Others claimed that its essence is one in type, and the difference is due to the mixture of dry with wet. Imam al-Razi said: Both opinions are possible.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of two opposing views regarding the nature of wetness in different substances. The first view holds that the wetness of water, oil, and mercury are essentially different (مخالفة بالماهية), meaning that 'wetness' is a genus with multiple species, each specific to the substance. The second view holds that wetness has one and the same essence in all substances, and the observed differences arise from the admixture of dry elements with the wet. The author then cites Imam Razi's judgment that both opinions are plausible (محتمل), indicating that the matter is not settled definitively.

Key Terms
رطوبة
ماهية
جنس
نوع
اختلاط
محتمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wetness is a single uniform quality that is the same in all substances.
Correction: The passage shows that scholars debated whether wetness differs in essence across substances; it is not a settled fact.

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion mentioned, what causes the difference in wetness between water, oil, and mercury?
A) Their wetness is essentially different by species.
B) The mixture of dry and wet.
C) The amount of heat in each.
D) Their transparency.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi reminds us that some metaphysical questions in kalam remain open, with both views being plausible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a matter where respected scholars differ, do you feel you must pick a side, or can you humbly accept both as possible? What drives that feeling?
?
Are there areas in your faith where you insist on a single answer, even when more knowledgeable people say both views are plausible? Could that insistence be a form of arrogance?
?
Imam al-Razi saw no harm in leaving this question open. Does that unsettle you? What does that reveal about your need for certainty versus your trust in divine wisdom?

Is There an Intermediate Quality Between Wetness and Dryness?
Raises the question of an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness, like red between black and white.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness is considered epistemically uncertain in classical kalam.

Core Idea
Not every conceptual middle has a real counterpart—some gaps remain unknown.

This passage raises a subtle question in the physics of qualities: can there be a quality that is intermediate between wetness and dryness, similar to how redness lies between black and white? The author presents the problem and then gives a cautious answer: it is unknown, and even its possibility is doubtful. This lesson explores the reasoning behind that epistemic reserve.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the question structure: it presents two opposing possibilities and then gives the answer.
The passage is very concise, so every word matters.
2
Understand the analogy with redness
The author compares the potential intermediate quality between wetness and dryness to redness between blackness and whiteness. Reflect on why redness is considered an intermediate quality in color theory.
Colors have clear intermediates, but wetness and dryness may not.
3
Grasp the epistemic conclusion
The author says 'the truth is that it is unknown, and that the possibility of its existence is doubtful.' This is not a denial of existence but an admission of ignorance. Consider why a theologian would choose such a cautious position.
Kalam often limits claims to what is rationally certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: هل توجد كيفية متوسطة بين الرطوبة واليبوسة تنافيهما كالحمرة بين السواد والبياض أو لا توجد الحق أنه غير معلوم ، وأنَّ إمكان وجودها مشكوك فيه .
The fourth [research]: Is there an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness that opposes both, like redness between blackness and whiteness, or not? The truth is that it is unknown, and that the possibility of its existence is doubtful.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a problem-posing passage. The author asks whether there exists a quality (كيفية) that is intermediate between wetness and dryness, analogous to redness which is intermediate between black and white. The answer is given: the truth is that this is not known (غير معلوم), and even the possibility of its existence is doubtful (مشكوك فيه). The passage does not attempt to resolve the question but rather states the epistemic uncertainty surrounding it.

Key Terms
كيفية متوسطة
رطوبة
يبوسة
حمرة
سواد
بياض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that just as there are intermediate colors, there must be intermediate tactile qualities.
Correction: The author states that the existence of such an intermediate quality is unknown and its possibility doubtful, so it is not a necessary truth.

Checkpoint
What is the final ruling on the existence of an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness?
A) It definitely exists.
B) It definitely does not exist.
C) It is unknown and its possibility is doubtful.
D) It exists but only in certain substances.

Key Takeaway
The existence of an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness is not known, and even its possibility is doubtful—a clear example of epistemic caution in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one area of your life where you have assumed a middle ground exists between two opposing things (good/bad, true/false, halal/haram) without evidence? Are you willing to revisit that assumption today?
?
This lesson says that even the possibility of an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness is doubtful. Is there a belief you hold that you have never questioned because it 'makes sense' — yet may be built on an unexamined assumption? What would it cost you to say 'I don't know'?
?
When you face uncertainty, do you rush to fill the gap with a comfortable answer? How can you practice the discipline of 'not knowing' as an act of intellectual honesty before Allah?

Definition of al-I'timad and Its Disputed Existence
Ibn Sina defines al-i'timad; mutakallimun disagree on its existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-i'timad according to Ibn Sina and the theological debate over its existence.

Core Idea
Al-i'timad is defined as the cause of resistance, but its existence is disputed.

This passage introduces the concept of al-i'timad (inclination) as defined by Ibn Sina. The philosophers consider it a cause of resistance, while theologians disagree on whether it exists independently. We will explore the definition, the alternative interpretation, and the argument from sense perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to Ibn Sina's definition and the alternative view that al-i'timad is identical to resistance.
The passage uses technical terms like al-mudafa'a and al-mayl.
2
Analyze the definition and alternative
Notice that Ibn Sina defines al-i'timad as that which necessitates resistance. Consider why some might say it is the resistance itself—this will be crucial for the debate.
This is a case of disagreement over whether al-i'timad is a cause or an effect.
3
Understand the theological disagreement
Identify the two camps: those who deny al-i'timad (al-Isfara'ini) and those who affirm it as necessary (Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris). Note the sensory examples given to support affirmation.
The sensory argument claims that we directly perceive inclination, but this is contested.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الاعتماد وهو المسمى بالميل عند الحكماء كما سيأتي وفيه مباحث أحدها الاعتماد على ما ذكره ابن سينا في الحدود ما يوجب للجسم المدافعة لما يمنعه الحركة إلى جهة ما من الجهات وهذا تصريح منه بأن الاعتماد علة للمدافعة وقيل هو نفس المدافعة المذكورة وقد اختلف فيه أي في وجود الاعتماد المتكلمون فنفاه الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرائيني وأتباعه وأثبته المعتزلة وكثير من أصحابنا كالقاضي بالضرورة أي قالوا ثبوته ضروري ومنعه مكابرة للحس فإن من حمل حجراً ثقيلاً أحس منه اعتماداً وميلاً إلى جهة السفل ومن وضع يده على زق منفوخ فيه مسكن تحت الماء أحس بميله إلى جهة العلو
Concerning al-i'timad, and it is called al-mayl (inclination) by the philosophers as will come. And it includes investigations: one of them is al-i'timad according to what Ibn Sina mentioned in al-Hudud: 'That which necessitates for the body resistance (al-mudafa'a) against what prevents it from movement towards a certain direction among directions.' And this is a statement from him that al-i'timad is the cause (illa) of resistance. And it was said: it is the resistance itself. And there has been disagreement concerning it, i.e., concerning the existence of al-i'timad, among the mutakallimun: al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini and his followers denied it, while the Mu'tazila and many of our companions, such as al-Qadi, affirmed it as necessary (bi al-darura), i.e., they said its affirmation is necessary. And preventing it is obstinacy against sense perception (mukabara li al-hiss), for whoever carries a heavy stone senses from it an inclination (i'timad) and a tendency (mayl) towards the downward direction, and whoever places his hand on an inflated waterskin containing air under water senses its inclination towards the upward direction.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage opens the third investigation on al-i'timad (inclination). It first defines al-i'timad according to Ibn Sina as that which causes a body to resist what prevents its movement. This defines it as the cause of resistance. An alternative view is that al-i'timad is identical to the resistance itself. Then it notes the disagreement among theologians: some deny its existence, while others affirm it as a necessary truth. The proponents argue that its denial contradicts sensory experience, giving examples: carrying a heavy stone gives a sensation of downward inclination; placing a hand on an inflated waterskin under water gives a sensation of upward inclination. This argument is presented as evidence that al-i'timad is known by necessity.

Key Terms
الاعتماد
الميل
المدافعة
العلة
الضروري
المكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sensory argument proves that al-i'timad exists as a distinct entity separate from the sensation of resistance.
Correction: The sensory argument only proves that one experiences resistance or tendency; it does not necessarily prove that al-i'timad is a separate cause. The alternative view holds that al-i'timad is the resistance itself.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina in al-Hudud, what is al-i'timad?
A) It is the resistance itself
B) It is that which necessitates resistance
C) It is the tendency towards the center
D) It is the weight of the body

Key Takeaway
Al-i'timad is defined as the cause of resistance, and while some affirm its existence by sense, others deny it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says denying al-i'timad is 'obstinacy against sense perception.' When have you rejected a truth because it clashed with your intellect, even though your heart sensed it? Are you willing to submit your senses to the discipline of Islamic scholarship, even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
The dispute over al-i'timad distinguishes between the cause and the effect. In your spiritual life, do you focus on the outward resistance (the trials) rather than the inward inclination (the divine wisdom) that Allah has placed? How can you train yourself to seek the cause, not just the symptom?
?
Ibn Sina and others differed on something as fundamental as the nature of a physical attribute. This shows the depth of scholarly disagreement. How does this humility challenge your own certainty about theological matters you have not studied thoroughly? Will you commit to learning the precise definitions from the trustworthy scholars before forming strong opinions?

intermediate
6 min
Types of Iʿtimād and Their Opposition
Iji classifies types of iʿtimād and discusses whether they are mutually opposed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classification of iʿtimād types leads to a verbal dispute about their mutual opposition, hinging on the definition of taḍādd.

Core Idea
The dispute over iʿtimād opposition is verbal, depending on the definition of taḍādd.

The text now turns to the third aspect of iʿtimād: its types. Since iʿtimād is associated with motion, it has as many types as directions. The key question is whether these types oppose one another. The answer, al-Ījī shows, depends on a prior definition of opposition itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: classification, question, disagreement, conclusion.
Pay attention to the condition about extreme contrariety.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the two groups: those who do not require extreme contrariety and those who do. Note what each group concludes about which types are opposed.
3
Understand the verbal dispute
The author says the disagreement is 'nazāʿ lafẓī' (verbal dispute). Reflect on why changing the definition of taḍādd resolves the conflict.
This shows how definitions shape theological arguments.
4
Connect to broader kalam
Consider how this discussion of iʿtimād fits into the larger argument about atoms and accidents. Why does it matter?
This ties back to the nature of created things and their dependence on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثها : له ) أي للاعتماد ( أنواع متعددة ( بحسب أنواع الحركة فقد يكون ) الاعتماد كالحركة إلى العلو والسفل وإلى سائر الجهات وهل أنواعه كلها (متضادة ) بعضها مع بعض ؟ اختلف فيه ( بناء على أنه هل يشترط بين الضدين غاية الخلاف والبعد أم لا ) يشترط فمن لم يشترط غاية الخلاف جعل كل نوعين من أنواع الاعتماد الجهات متضادين ومن اشترطها قال : إِنَّ كل نوعين بينهما غاية التباعد فهما متضادان كالميل الصاعد والهابط وما ليس كذلك فلا تضاد بينهما وإن كانا ممتنعي الاجتماع كالميل الصاعد والميل المقتضي للحركة يمنة أو يسرة ) فهو نزاع لفظي ) مبني على تفسير التضاد )
The third of them: it (iʿtimād) has multiple types according to types of motion. So iʿtimād may be like motion upward, downward, and to the other directions. Are all its types mutually opposed (mutaḍāddah)? There is disagreement on it, based on whether extreme contrariety and distance are required between two opposites or not. Those who do not require extreme contrariety consider every two types of iʿtimād in opposite directions to be opposed. Those who require it say: every two types between which there is extreme distance, like upward and downward inclination, are opposed; what is not like that, there is no opposition between them, even if they are mutually exclusive, like upward inclination and inclination requiring motion to the right or left. It is a verbal dispute based on the interpretation of opposition (taḍādd).
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a classification and discussion of opposition. The third topic of iʿtimād states that it has multiple types according to direction. The question arises: are all these types mutually opposed? The disagreement is about the definition of opposition: does it require extreme contrariety? Those who do not require it consider any two opposite-direction types as opposites. Those who require extreme contrariety say only inclinations that are at extreme ends (like upward and downward) are true opposites; other inclinations, like upward and rightward, are not opposites even if they cannot coexist. The author concludes this is a verbal dispute based on how one defines taḍādd.

Key Terms
تضاد (taḍādd)
غاية الخلاف (extreme contrariety)
ميل (mayl/inclination)
ضدان (ḍiddān/opposites)
امتناع الاجتماع (mutual exclusivity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all opposing inclinations are opposites in the same sense.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it depends on the definition of taḍādd; some require extreme contrariety, others do not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the dispute about the types of iʿtimād being mutually opposed considered a verbal dispute?
A) Because all types are clearly non-opposed.
B) Because it depends on whether extreme contrariety is required for two things to be opposites.
C) Because the author does not care about the issue.
D) Because iʿtimād is not a real thing.

Key Takeaway
The opposition of iʿtimād types is not a factual matter but depends on how one defines opposition itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in a disagreement about religious matters, do you pause to check whether you and the other person are using the same definition for key terms? What would it cost you to clarify that first before continuing?
?
The passage reveals that a dispute can be purely verbal—based on differing definitions. Are there any areas in your own understanding of aqeedah where you have held strong opinions that might actually be based on a verbal misunderstanding? Will you examine them this week?
?
If the scholars concluded this is a verbal dispute, what does that teach you about humility in theological discussions? Have you ever insisted on being 'right' when the difference was only in wording? How can you repent from that pattern?

The Six Directions According to Common People
Iji explains how common people derive the six directions from human body parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how common people derive the six directions from human anatomy and then generalize them to all bodies.

Core Idea
The six directions are not absolute but derived from the human body.

Iji begins by explaining how the common people conceptualize the six directions. They take them from the human body's own parts: front, back, right, left, above, below. This lesson unpacks that derivation and shows how it was extended to animals and then to all bodies. Understanding this helps us see the conventional nature of directional terms in early kalam discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Iji describes the human body's orientation.
2
Identify the six directions from body parts
The passage gives specific body parts for each direction: right from the stronger side, left from opposite, front facing face, etc. Make a list.
3
Understand the generalization
See how common people applied these to animals and then to all bodies, even without distinct parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنَّ الجهات ) على ما اشتهر بين الناس ( ست أخذها العامة من جهات الإنسان ) وأطرافه ( التي هي القدام والخلف واليمين والشمال والفوق والتحت). فإنَّ الإنسان يحيط به جنبان عليهما اليدان وظهر وبطن ورأس وقدم فالجانب الذي هو أقوى في الغالب ومنه ابتداء الحركة يسمى يميناً وما يقابله يساراً، وما يحاذي وجهه وإليه حركاته بالطبع وهناك حاسة الإبصار يسمى قداماً، وما يقابله خلفاً وما يلي رأسه بالطبع يسمى فوقا وما يقابله تحتا. ولما لم يكن عند العامة سوى ما ذكر وقعت أوهامهم على هذه الجهات الست واعتبروها في سائر الحيوانات أيضاً لكنهم جعلوا الفوق مايلي ظهورها بالطبع والتحت ما يقابله ثم عمموا اعتبارها في سائر الأجسام وإن لم يكن لها أجزاء متمايزة على الوجه المذكور
And know that the directions, according to what is well-known among people, are six. The common people took them from the directions of the human body and its limbs, which are front, back, right, left, above, and below. For the human is surrounded by two sides upon which are the two hands, a back, a belly, a head, and a foot. So the side that is generally stronger and from which movement begins is called right, and what faces it is left. What faces his face and to which his natural movements are directed, and where the sense of sight is located, is called front, and what faces it is back. What is above his head naturally is called above, and what faces it is below. Since the common people have only what is mentioned, their imaginations fell upon these six directions, and they also considered them in other animals, but they made above what faces their backs naturally, and below what faces it. Then they generalized their consideration to all bodies, even if they do not have distinct parts in the manner mentioned.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation. It explains that the common people derive the six directions from the human body. The human has two sides (hands), back, belly, head, foot. The stronger side where movement begins is right; opposite is left. The side facing the face, to which natural movements go and where sight is, is front; opposite is back. Above the head is up; opposite is down. Since common people only know these, they apply them to animals by analogy: for animals, above is what faces their back naturally, below is opposite. Then they generalize to all bodies, even those without distinct limbs.

قدام (front)
خلف (back)
يمين (right)
يسار (left)
فوق (above)
تحت (below)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The six directions are absolute and independent of human anatomy.
Correction: The common people derived the six directions from the human body and then generalized, so they are anthropocentric conventions.

Checkpoint

A) The direction of the rising sun.
B) The side that is generally stronger and from which movement begins.
C) The side opposite the heart.
D) The side that faces the front.

Key Takeaway
The six directions are not metaphysically real but are conventional labels based on human anatomy and then extended by analogy to all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the six directions are not absolute but are derived from your own body. How often do you unconsciously project human limitations—like direction—onto your conception of Allah? What specific thought can you hold today to remind yourself that He is beyond such categories?

Iji notes that common people generalize directions to all bodies, even those without distinct parts. Have you ever caught yourself assuming that Allah must be 'somewhere' when you pray? Read Surah Al-Ikhlas and write down one way it corrects that assumption in your heart today.
?
The passage says 'their imaginations fell upon these six directions.' Your imagination is powerful—it can either limit your understanding of Allah or elevate it. Which common imagination about Allah are you willing to challenge today, and what verse of the Qur'an will you use as your sword?

Inclination and the Threefold Division of Motion
Al-Iji defines inclination and classifies motion into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Iji defines inclination (i'timad) as tendency (mayl) and his classification of motion into natural, forced, and psychic, including the crucial condition of will for psychic motion.

Core Idea
Motion is classified by its cause: natural, forced, or psychic — and psychic motion requires will.

In this passage, Al-Iji lays down the conceptual foundation for understanding motion in kalam. He defines inclination as a tendency and then divides motion into three fundamental types based on their source. The key nuance is that psychic motion is not simply any motion accompanied by awareness—it must issue from will. This distinction is important for later discussions on divine attributes and human actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the definition of inclination and the threefold division.
2
Focus on the definition of psychic motion
Identify the two conditions: issuing from the rational soul and being voluntary. The passage gives an example of falling as a motion that is not psychic despite awareness.
3
Understand the objections and responses
The commentary addresses objections about stones moved by God's will and cases of vacuum. Notice how the author defends the classification by arguing that such motions are voluntary or metaphorical.
These objections strengthen the definition.
4
Apply the classification
Try to think of other motions and classify them as natural, forced, or psychic according to the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل لا إلى السفل فالميل الصادر عن النفس الناطقة في بدنها عند القائل بتجردها نفساني ،قسري لأنها ليست خارجة عن البدن ممتازة عنه في الإشارة. والميل المقارن للشعور إذا لم يكن صادراً عن الإرادة لا يكون نفسانياً كما إذا سقط الإنسان عن السطح ) وكذا الحركات منحصرة بهذا الدليل في الطبيعية والقسرية والنفسانية، قوله : فالميل الصادر إلخ) بيان لفائدة تفسير الخارج بالممتاز بالإشارة الحسية وفائدة التقييد بقوله : وصادر عن الإرادة وما قيل : إنه إذا تحرك الحجر بإرادة القادر المختار إلى فوق فميله قسري مع أنه لا يصدق عليه أنه بسبب ممتاز عن محل الميل في الإشارة فوهم، لأنَّ ذلك الميل إرادي كيف ولا فرق بينه وبين الميل الذي يحدثه نفوسنا في أبداننا. وكذا ما قيل في صورة امتناع الخلاء كالزراقات والقارورة المخصوصة المكبة على الماء. فإنهم قالوا : القاسر فيها امتناع الخلاء وهو ليس ذا وضع لأن القاسر فيها ملازمة سطوح الأجسام بواسطة امتناع الخلاء والنسبة إلى امتناع الخلاء على سبيل التجوز .
The third observatory - the third objective: on inclination (i'timad), which is tendency (mayl), not necessarily downward. So the tendency issuing from the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) in its body, according to the one who affirms its immateriality, is psychic (nafsānī); [it would be] forced (qasrī) if [the soul] were not exiting the body distinguished from it in indication (al-ishāra). And the tendency concomitant with awareness (al-shuʿūr) if it does not issue from will (al-irāda) is not psychic, as when a person falls from a roof. And likewise motions are confined by this proof to natural (ṭabīʿiyya), forced (qasriyya), and psychic (nafsāniyya). He said: the words 'the tendency issuing etc.' is an explanation of the benefit of interpreting 'external' (al-khārij) as 'distinguished by sensory indication' and the benefit of the qualification by his statement 'and issuing from will'. And what was said: that if a stone moves by the will of the powerful chooser upward, its inclination is forced, even though it does not hold that it is due to something distinguished from the locus of inclination in indication, is mistaken, because that inclination is voluntary (irādī); how not, for there is no difference between it and the inclination that our souls produce in our bodies. And likewise what was said concerning the case of the impossibility of a vacuum (imtināʿ al-khalāʾ), such as the syringes and the particular flask inverted on water: they said the coercer (al-qāsir) in it is the impossibility of vacuum, which is not a positional thing, because the coercer in it is the adhesion of the surfaces of bodies by means of the impossibility of vacuum, and the attribution to the impossibility of vacuum is by way of metaphor (tajawwuz).
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines inclination (i'timad) as tendency (mayl) and classifies motion into three types: natural, forced, and psychic. It first defines psychic motion as a tendency issuing from the rational soul in its body when the soul is considered immaterial; if the soul were not distinguished from the body by sensory indication, the motion would be forced. It then adds that a tendency accompanied by awareness is only psychic if it issues from will; otherwise it is not psychic, as in the case of falling from a roof. The passage then states that all motions are confined to these three types. The commentary that follows explains the benefit of interpreting 'external' as 'distinguished by sensory indication' and the qualification 'issuing from will'. It refutes an objection about a stone moving by God's will upward, arguing that such a motion is voluntary (irādī) because it resembles the inclination produced by our souls. It also discusses the case of vacuum as a metaphorical coercer. The key point is the precise definition of psychic motion: it must issue from the soul and be voluntary.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any motion accompanied by awareness is psychic (nafsānī), regardless of will.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that a tendency accompanied by awareness is only psychic if it issues from will; otherwise it is not psychic, as in falling from a roof.

Checkpoint

A) It must issue from the rational soul.
B) It must issue from will (irāda).
C) It must be downward.
D) It must be distinct from the body in sensory indication.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, motion is classified by its causal source: natural, forced, or psychic, and psychic motion requires both awareness and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you act, do you pause to check whether your action truly issues from your soul with will, or are you often moved by external pressures or unconscious habits? How would embracing only voluntary, aware actions change your daily life?

?

Three Opinions on the Motion of the Pulse
Classification and defense of three opinions on pulse motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three opinions on the motion of the pulse and why the composite view does not undermine the threefold classification of simple motions.

Core Idea
The pulse's composite motion is no exception to the threefold division of simple motions.

The motion of the pulse has been a subject of debate among scholars, with some viewing it as purely coercive, others as composite. This passage presents three opinions and defends the composite view, showing that it fits within the established categories of motion. We'll explore each opinion and the reasoning behind the composite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the use of 'قيل' to present opinions.
2
Identify the three opinions
The passage first presents two coercive views (spirit-driven and heart-driven) and then a composite view. Identify each and note the differences.
The two coercive views share the idea that the motion is forced, but differ on the coercer.
3
Understand the defense of the composite view
The passage argues that the threefold division of motion (natural, coercive, voluntary) applies only to simple motions. The composite pulse motion, being composite, does not break that classification.
This is the key logical move that reconciles the composite view with the standard division.
4
Review the explanatory notes
The commentary clarifies specific terms like 'pure air' and 'heavy', and explains how the composite view works (contraction coercive, expansion natural). This deepens your understanding.
The reference to the author of al-Mujaz provides additional support.

And it has been said: Indeed, the motion of the pulse is coercive, and the coercer is the spirit (al-rūḥ), for it draws its nourishment which is air and pushes what is excess from it, so its vessel (the arteries) undergoes expansion due to the drawing and contraction due to the pushing. And it is said: The coercer is the heart, either by way of ebb and flow: when the heart expands, the spirit moves from the arteries to it, so it contracts; and when the heart contracts, the spirit moves to the arteries, so it expands. Or by way of being consequent, as the motion of a tree follows the motion of its branches and twigs, so their expansion occurs with the expansion of the heart and their contraction with its contraction. And it is also said: The motion of the pulse is composite, and what is confined to the three categories is simple motion, so there is no objection by its being outside them.

His saying: 'pure air' means free from impurities. His saying: 'heavy' (kall) with fatha on kaf and tashdid on lam means heavy. His saying: 'for it draws' means the spirit draws nourishment which is pure air. His saying: 'and pushes what is excess from it' means the spirit pushes what becomes excess of that nourishment, the smoky part tempered in it. His saying: 'its vessel' means the arteries. His saying: 'by drawing' means due to the drawing of nourishment. His saying: 'by pushing' means due to the pushing of wastes. His saying: 'on the way of ebb and flow' - ebb is the flow and flow is its opposite. His saying: 'the motion of the pulse is composite' according to what the author of al-Mujaz chose, for he said: The motion of contraction is coercive, and the motion of expansion is natural, meaning that the natural amount of the vein is what occurs to it in the state of expansion, and as for what occurs to it in the state of contraction, it is an amount that occurs to it coercively. The heart. His saying: 'for when the heart expands' here is a discussion because the refutation of the confinement would then return with respect to the motion of
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a classification-and-defense of opinions on the motion of the pulse (حركة النبض). It first presents two opinions that both consider the pulse motion coercive (قسرية): one attributed the coercion to the spirit (الروح) drawing air, the other to the heart via ebb-and-flow or consequent motion. A third opinion holds it is composite (مركبة), i.e., partly coercive (contraction) and partly natural (expansion). The author of the commentary concludes that the threefold division of motion (natural, coercive, voluntary) applies only to simple motions, so the composite pulse motion does not break that classification. The commentary then explains specific terms: 'pure air' (هواء صاف) means free from impurities, 'heavy' (كلا) means weighty, and clarifies that 'drawing' and 'pushing' refer to the spirit's actions. It also cites the author of al-Mujaz, who explicitly states contraction is coercive and expansion is natural, thus supporting the composite view. The final line notes a potential objection: if the heart's expansion is natural, then the confinement of simple motions might be questioned, but this is left as a subject of discussion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The composite motion of the pulse is an exception to the threefold division of simple motions.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the threefold division applies only to simple motions, so composite motion like the pulse is not an exception.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a simple motion
B) Because the threefold division applies only to composite motions
C) Because the threefold division applies only to simple motions
D) Because it is a natural motion

Key Takeaway
The composite motion of the pulse does not invalidate the threefold classification of simple motions because that classification applies only to simple motions.

Combined Natural and Forced Inclination Produces a Single Stronger Inclination
Al-Jurjani argues for the combination of natural and forced inclinations into a single stronger inclination and refutes the objection requiring an obstacle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why natural and forced inclinations combine into a single intensified inclination and why the objection requiring an obstacle is invalid.

Core Idea
Natural and forced inclinations merge into one stronger inclination, not two separate ones.

Al-Jurjani addresses a key question in physical causation: can a body simultaneously possess both a natural inclination and a forced inclination? Some deny this possibility, but the author argues that they combine into a single intensified inclination. This lesson unpacks the argument and its refutation of objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing deeply. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning. The passage is dense with technical terms.
Pay attention to the keywords: ميل, طبيعة, قاسر.
2
Identify the main claim and the objection
Find where Al-Jurjani states the claim (first two lines) and then the objection by 'some' (starting with 'وقال بعضهم'). The objection sets a condition for combination.
The objection claims combination is only possible with an obstacle; without one, it's impossible.
3
Analyze the refutation
See how Al-Jurjani refutes the objection: he points out that bodies with nature vary in size, density, etc., so nature does not necessitate a fixed degree of inclination. This undermines the objection's assumption.
The refutation begins with 'وهذا باطل' (and this is false).
4
Examine the example of the falling stone
The passage ends with a question: why is a stone falling from above faster than one from below? The answer: lack of obstruction allows continuous increase of inclination, resulting in a stronger combined effect.
This example illustrates how combination produces intensification.

المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل اجتمعا أحدثا مرتبة أشد مما يقتضيه كل واحد منهما على حده فلا يكون هناك إلا ميل واحد مستند إلى الطبيعة والقاسر معاً. وقال بعضهم إنما يجوز اجتماعهما إذا كان الجسم ممنواً بما يعاوقه كالحجر فإن الهواء يقاومه وبقدر تلك المقاومة يحصل الفتور فلا يبعد أن يحصل مع الميل الطبيعي ميل قسري وإذا لم يكن له معاوق كما إذا قدرنا المسافة خلاء كان اجتماعهما محالاً، لأنَّ الطبيعة إذا خلت عن العوائق أحدثت معلولها على أقصى ما يمكن فيكون الميل الطبيعي على ذلك التقدير بالغاً إلى نهاية الشدة فيستحيل أن يجامعه ميل غريب على أحد الوجهين، وهذا باطل بما اختلاف الجسم ذي الطبيعة في الصغر والكبر والتخلخل والتكاثف والاندماج والانتفاش فلا يرد أن الطبيعة نسبتها إلى جميع مراتب الميل على السوية فلا يقتضي مرتبة معينة كما مر بيانه . قوله : (إلا) ميل واحد مستند إلى الطبيعة والقاسر معاً في إشارة إلى أن ليس داخلاً في شيء من الأقسام الثلاثة لأنها أقسام لما يكون مستنداً إلى واحد منها . قوله : (ممنواً) في الصحاح منوته ومنيته إذا ابتليته. أعلى يكون أسرع من الذي من مكان أسفل مع تساويهما في الحجم والثقل حتى أن الأول ربما يصادم حيواناً فيقتله ولا كذلك النازل من أسفل ؟ قلت : سره اشتداد الميل في الأول وذلك لأن الطبيعة إذا لم تكن معوقة بالضد أوجدت الميل ولا يزال يزداد الميل ومعلوم أن تأثير الطبيعة وحدها أو مع ميل قليل ليس كتأثيرها مع الميول الكثيرة التي تقويها وتعضدها كما في المباحث المشرقية.
The third observation - the third purpose: On al-i'timad (dependence/leaning) which is al-mayl (inclination). When they both combine, they produce a degree stronger than what each one alone necessitates, so there is only one inclination based on both nature and the force together. And some said that their combination is only permissible if the body is afflicted with what opposes it, like a stone, for the air resists it and to the extent of that resistance weakness occurs, so it is not far that with natural inclination a forced inclination occurs. And if there is no opposer, as if we suppose the distance to be a void, then their combination is impossible, because nature when free of obstacles produces its effect to the utmost possible, so the natural inclination on that estimation reaches the extreme of intensity, and so it is impossible for it to be combined with a strange inclination in either of the two ways. And this is false due to the difference of the body having a nature in smallness and largeness, rarefaction and condensation, contraction and expansion. So it cannot be objected that nature's proportion to all degrees of inclination is equal, so it does not necessitate a specific degree, as was explained earlier. His statement: "except one inclination based on both nature and the force together" indicates that it is not included in any of the three divisions, because they are divisions of what is based on one of them. His statement: "afflicted" (mamnuwwan): in al-Sihah, manawtuhu and mayyattuhu if you tested him. Is a falling body from above faster than one from a lower place, when they are equal in size and weight, such that the former may collide with an animal and kill it, but not the one descending from below? I said: its secret is the intensification of inclination in the former, because nature, when not obstructed by an opposite, generates inclination and inclination continues to increase. And it is known that the effect of nature alone or with a little inclination is not like its effect with many inclinations that strengthen and support it, as in the Oriental Investigations.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the core argument that when natural and forced inclination combine, they produce a single, stronger inclination rather than two separate ones. It begins by stating the claim, then raises an objection: some say combination is only possible when there is an obstacle (like air resistance) because without obstacles, nature alone would produce maximum inclination, leaving no room for forced inclination. The refutation follows: this objection is false because bodies with nature vary in size, density, etc., so nature does not produce a fixed degree of inclination. A lexical note explains 'mamnuwwan'. The passage then answers a question: why does a stone falling from above move faster than one from below, even if equal in size and weight? The answer is that lack of obstruction allows nature to continuously increase the inclination, and the effect of nature with many supporting inclinations is greater than alone or with few. Thus, the combination results in a single intensified inclination.
