القاسر (al-qasir)
المعاوق (al-mu'aq)
المدافعة (al-mudafa'a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural and forced inclination cannot combine unless there is an obstacle like air resistance.
Correction: The passage shows they can combine into a single stronger inclination, and the objection about obstacles is refuted by the variability of bodies.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when natural and forced inclination combine?
A) They produce two separate inclinations.
B) They produce a single, stronger inclination.
C) They cancel each other out.
D) They produce a weaker inclination.

Key Takeaway
Natural and forced inclinations combine into a single intensified inclination, not two separate ones, and this is possible even without obstacles due to the variability of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel pulled between your natural desire for truth and the external pressures of society, do you experience them as two separate forces or as a single, intensified direction in your life? Where is the obstacle you keep using to excuse your lack of full commitment?
?
If nature, when free from obstacles, produces its effect to the utmost, what obstacles have you allowed to weaken your innate inclination towards goodness? Are you willing to remove them so that your natural and intentional efforts combine into one powerful resolve?

Definition of Hardness and Softness
Al-Jurjani defines hardness and softness, excludes celestial bodies, and presents al-Razi's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defines hardness and softness, why celestial bodies are excluded, the debate over softness as privation or positive quality, and al-Razi's view that they are not among tangible qualities.

Core Idea
Hardness is a quality before resistance, and softness is not just absence but may be a positive opposite.

This passage establishes the definitions of two fundamental qualities: hardness and softness. Al-Jurjani carefully distinguishes hardness from the act of resistance and introduces an exception for celestial bodies. He also presents a debate about whether softness is a privation or a positive opposite, followed by al-Razi's critical insight that these qualities are not part of the tangible qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions and exception
Read the first part where hardness is defined as a quality of resistance to the presser, softness as the absence of hardness in something that normally has that property, and the exception of celestial bodies.
Notice that hardness is a quality, not just the act of resisting.
2
Examine the debate on softness
Identify the two interpretations of softness: as a privation (ʿadam malakatī) and as a positive quality opposite to hardness.
The text says 'it is said' to introduce the alternative view.
3
Analyze al-Razi's argument
Understand why al-Razi says hardness and softness are not tangible. He identifies three things in a soft body: motion, concavity, and disposition—but the first two are not softness.
Al-Razi concludes that softness is not a simple quality.
4
Review the commentary on resistance
Read the commentary that distinguishes hardness from resistance: resistance occurs only at pressing, but hardness exists before.
This clarifies that hardness is an additional quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الرابع : الصلابة ممانعة للغامز ]
( الصلابة كيفية بها ممانعة (الغامز أي كيفية للجسم يكون بها للغامز فلا يقبل تأثيره ولا ينغمز تحته ( واللين عدم الصلابة عما من شأنه ذلك ) وإنما اعتبر هذا القيد (احترازاً عن الفلك ) فإنه لا يوصف عندهم بكونه شأنه الصلابة لأنه من وإن كان مما لا ينغمز ولا يتأثر من الغامز لكن بذاته لا بكيفية قائمة به كالجسم العصري ) فهو عدم ملكة لها وقيل: بل اللين ( كيفية بها يطيع الجسم للغامز فهو ) على هذا التفسير (ضدها ) لكونها وجودية أيضاً قال الإمام الرازي : إِنَّ الصلابة واللين ليسا من الكيفيات الملموسة وذلك أنَّ الجسم اللين هو الذي ينغمز فهناك أمور ثلاثة : الأول : الحركة الحاصلة في سطحه الثاني : شكل التقعير المقارن لحدوث تلك الحركة. الثالث : كونه مستعداً لقبول ذينك الأمرين وليس الأولان بلين لأنهما

قوله : ( كيفية بها إلخ) كونها مغايرة للمانعة بناء على أنَّ الممانعة إنما تتحقق حال الغمز، والصلابة ثابتة في الجسم الصلب قبلها وليست لذاته لكونه من شأنه قبول الغمز فتكون الكيفية زائدة .

قوله : (قال الإمام الرازي إلخ المشهور أن الكيفيات الملموسة الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة واللطافة والكثافة واللزوجة والهشاشة والجفاف والبلة والثقل والخفة والخشونة والملاسة والصلابة واللين والتحقيق أنَّ الأربعة الأخيرة ليست منها .

قوله : فهناك أمور (ثلاثة بل أربعة رابعها عدم المقاومة إلا أن يكتفى عنه بذكر الأمر الثالث كما اكتفي به عن ذكر عدم الاستعداد الشديد نحو الانفعال .
[Fourth Objective: Hardness (ṣalāba) is a resistance to the presser (ghāmiz)]
(Hardness is a quality (kayfiyya) by which there is resistance to the presser, i.e., a quality of the body by which it has [resistance] to the presser, so that it does not accept its effect and does not sink under it. (And softness (līn) is the absence of hardness in that which normally has that property. And this condition is considered (as a precaution against the celestial sphere (falak)), for it is not described in their view as having the property of hardness, because even though it does not sink and is not affected by the presser, it is by its essence, not by a quality subsisting in it, like the terrestrial body (al-jism al-‘uṣrī).) So it is a privation (al-‘adam al-malakatī) of it. And it is said: rather, softness is (a quality by which the body obeys the presser, so it is) on this interpretation (opposite (ḍidd) to it), being also existential. Al-Imām al-Rāzī said: Hardness and softness are not among the tangible qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-malmūsa); this is because the soft body is that which sinks; there are three things: first, the motion occurring in its surface; second, the shape of concavity concomitant with the occurrence of that motion; third, its being disposed to accept those two things. And the first two are not softness, because they...

His statement: 'A quality by which, etc.'—[explains] that it is different from resistance (al-mumāni‘a), based on the fact that resistance only occurs at the moment of pressing, whereas hardness is established in the solid body before that and is not due to its essence because it is liable to accept pressing; so the quality is additional.

His statement: 'Al-Imām al-Rāzī said, etc.'—The well-known [view] is that the tangible qualities are heat, cold, moisture, dryness, subtlety, density, viscosity, brittleness, dryness, wetness, heaviness, lightness, roughness, smoothness, hardness, and softness; but investigation shows that the last four are not among them.

His statement: 'So there are three things'—rather four, the fourth being absence of resistance, except that one is satisfied with mentioning the third, just as one is satisfied with not mentioning the intense disposition towards being affected.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage defines hardness (ṣalāba) and softness (līn). It begins by stating that hardness is a quality by which a body resists a presser (ghāmiz), preventing it from sinking. Softness is defined as the absence of hardness in something that normally would have that property; this qualification excludes celestial bodies (falak), which do not sink but not due to a quality—they do so by their essence, like a terrestrial body? The text then notes that on one account, softness is a privation (‘adam malakatī), but on another, it is a positive quality by which the body obeys the presser, making it opposite to hardness. Next, al-Rāzī’s view is cited: hardness and softness are not among the tangible qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-malmūsa). He explains that in a soft body, there are three factors: the motion on its surface, the concavity shape accompanying that motion, and the disposition to accept these two; the first two are not softness. The commentary then clarifies that hardness is distinct from resistance (mumāni‘a) because resistance occurs only at the moment of pressing, while hardness exists before. The well-known list of tangible qualities includes many, but investigation shows that the last four (including hardness and softness?) are not actually tangible. Finally, there is a note that the matters are three or four, the fourth being lack of resistance, but it can be dispensed with by mentioning the third.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Softness is simply the absence of hardness without further condition, and hardness is identical to resistance.
Correction: Softness is defined as the absence of hardness only in things that normally have that property, excluding celestial bodies. Hardness is a quality distinct from the act of resistance, existing before pressing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the celestial body not described as hard?
A) Because it is too far away.
B) Because it does not sink due to its essence, not a quality.
C) Because it is soft.
D) Because hardness requires being tangible.

Key Takeaway
Hardness and softness are defined by the body's response to pressing, with careful conditions and a debate over their ontological status, culminating in al-Razi's challenge to their classification as tangible qualities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage distinguishes between hardness (a quality existing before pressing) and resistance (which occurs only at the moment of pressing). Think of your own character—do you have a consistent quality of steadfastness (hardness) even when no test comes, or do you only 'resist' when pressured? What would it take for you to have that quality established in your soul before the trial?
?
Softness is defined either as the mere absence of hardness or as a positive quality of obedience. In your spiritual life, do you define yourself by what you lack (e.g., not being arrogant) or by positive virtues you actively possess? Which definition makes your character more real and enduring?
?
Al-Razi argues that hardness and softness are not directly sensed but are dispositions behind the sensations. When you judge others, do you react to their surface words and actions (the 'motion and concavity') or do you seek to understand their heart's underlying disposition? How often do your perceptions deceive you because you settle for the tangible?

Smoothness and Roughness: Position or Quality?
Defines smoothness and roughness and states the disagreement over their category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of smoothness and roughness according to the mutakallimūn and the philosophers, and their disagreement over whether they belong to the category of position or quality.

Core Idea
Smoothness and roughness are either a matter of position or a quality depending on the scholar's perspective.

This passage presents the definitions of smoothness and roughness from two major schools: the mutakallimūn and the philosophers. The mutakallimūn emphasize the relational aspect of the parts' positions, while the philosophers consider them as tangible qualities. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how different scholarly traditions classify physical properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parentheses in the Arabic indicate insertions from the commentary.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the definition of smoothness and roughness according to the mutakallimūn and the philosophers. Note the key terms used.
3
Pinpoint the categorical disagreement
The passage explicitly states that according to the mutakallimūn, smoothness and roughness are from the category of position (waḍʿ), not quality. The philosophers disagree, considering them as qualities (kayfiyyatān). Recognise this as the central point.
4
Consider the variant opinion
Note the additional opinion that these qualities subsist on the surface of the body, which is an example of an accident subsisting in another accident, a doctrine the philosophers allow.
This shows a deeper metaphysical principle.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters. It shows how different schools classify physical properties based on their ontological frameworks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الخامس : الملاسة عند المتكلمين ( الملاسة عند المتكلمين استواء وضع الأجزاء في ظاهر الجسم ( والخشونة ) عدمه ) بأن يكون بعض الأجزاء ناتئا وبعضها غائرا فهما على هذا القول من باب الوضع دون الكيف، وعند الحكماء) هما (كيفيتان) ملموستان (قائمتان بالجسم تابعتان للاستواء واللااستواء المذكورين ( وقيل :) قائمتان ( بسطح الجسم فإنَّ قيام العرض بالعرض جائز عندهم
The third observation – the fifth objective: smoothness according to the mutakallimūn: (Smoothness according to the mutakallimūn is the evenness of the position of parts on the surface of the body, and roughness is its absence) that is, some parts protrude and some are sunken. So they are, according to this statement, from the category of position (waḍʿ) not quality (kayf). And according to the philosophers: they are two tangible qualities subsisting in the body, following the evenness and unevenness mentioned. And it is said: they subsist on the surface of the body, for the subsistence of an accident by an accident is permissible according to them.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage defines smoothness and roughness and presents the disagreement between the mutakallimūn (theologians) and the philosophers (ḥukamā') regarding their ontological category. The math (original text) states the mutakallimūn's view: smoothness is the evenness of the position of parts on the surface of a body, and roughness is the absence of such evenness, with parts protruding and sunken. Thus, smoothness and roughness fall under the category of position (waḍʿ), not quality (kayfiyya). The philosophers differ: they consider smoothness and roughness as two tangible qualities (kayfiyyatān mamlūsatān) that subsist in the body and are consequent upon the evenness or unevenness of the parts. There is also a variant opinion that they subsist on the surface of the body, based on the principle that an accident may subsist in another accident, which the philosophers accept. The commentary explains that the disagreement hinges on whether the primary referent is the relation of parts (which is waḍʿ) or the resulting sensation (which is kayfiyya).

متكلمون
حكماء
استواء
انفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might mistakenly think smoothness and roughness are only qualities (kayfiyyāt).
Correction: The mutakallimūn emphasize their relational aspect as position (waḍʿ), not just qualities.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimūn, smoothness and roughness belong to which category?
A) Quality (kayfiyya)
B) Position (waḍʿ)
C) Quantity (kammiyya)
D) Substance (jawhar)

Key Takeaway
Smoothness and roughness are classified as either position (waḍʿ) by the mutakallimūn or quality (kayfiyya) by the philosophers, reflecting different approaches to physical properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you touch a smooth surface, do you instinctively attribute the sensation to a 'quality' of the object, or can you see it as a relation (waḍʿ) of its parts? Which perspective feels more true to your experience, and why might that matter for how you understand the nature of physical reality?
?
The passage shows that theologians and philosophers disagreed over how to categorize smoothness and roughness. Reflect on a disagreement you have with someone about a seemingly simple issue—could it be that you are both looking at different 'categories' of the same phenomenon? How might recognizing that change your approach?
?
If something as ordinary as smoothness can be understood as either position or quality, what does that say about your own assumptions regarding the nature of everyday experiences? Are there other aspects of your life that you might be incorrectly categorizing, perhaps confusing a relational state with an inherent quality?

The Impossibility of Defining Light and Color
Al-Sanusi states the impossibility of defining light and color, presents their definitions, and critiques them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why light and color cannot be defined, what definitions have been proposed for them, and why those definitions are considered insufficient.

Core Idea
Light and color are known directly by sensation, so any definition is merely a description.

Al-Sanusi begins by asserting that light and color are so obvious that they defy definition. Yet thinkers have proposed definitions to highlight their properties. This lesson presents the core claim and the famous definitions of light and color, along with the critique that these definitions are actually more obscure than the things defined.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the claim of impossibility to the definitions and the critique.
The passage includes both the matn and the beginning of the sharh.
2
Identify the claim of impossibility
Find the statement that light and color cannot be defined. Understand why: they are obvious and known by sensation.
This is the opening sentence.
3
Examine the definitions proposed
The passage gives two definitions for light: as a primary perfection of the transparent, and as a quality not dependent on vision. Color is defined as the opposite. List them.
Note the phrase 'أو' which introduces an alternative definition.
4
Understand the critique
The passage states that these definitions are 'definition by the more obscure' (تعريف بالأخفى). Why? Because the definitions are less clear than the things defined.
Reflect on the irony of defining obvious things with technical terms.
5
Consider the purpose
The passage suggests the purpose is to draw attention to properties and rulings, not to give real definitions. This sets up the subsequent discussion.
This connects to the next part of the course.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه لا يمكن تعريفهما أي تعريف الضوء واللون لظهورهما فإن الإحساس بجزئياتهما قد أطلعنا على ماهيتهما لا يفي به ما يمكننا من تعريفاتهما على تقدير صحتها كما مر في مباحث الحرارة. وما يقال في تعريفهما من أن الضوء كمال أول للشفاف من حيث هو شفاف وإنما اعتبر قيد الحيثية لأن الضوء ليس كمالاً للشفاف في جسميته ولا في شيء آخر بل في شفافيته والمراد بكونه كمالاً أول أنه كمال ذاتي لا عرضي. أو كيفية لا يتوقف إبصارها على إبصار شيء آخر ومن أن اللون بعكسه أي كيفية يتوقف إبصارها على إبصار شيء آخر هو الضوء فإن اللون ما لم يصر مستنيراً لا يكون مرئياً. فتعريف بالأخفى كما لا يخفى، ولعل المراد بما ذكره هو التنبيه على خواصهما وأحكامهما ليزداد امتيازهما ولما كانت رؤية اللون مشروطة برؤية الضوء أورد كلا منهما في قسم فقال: ولنجعل مباحثهما قسمين. قوله: لا يفي به ما يمكننا لأن الحاصل في الذهن بعد حذف مشخصات الجزئيات نفس ماهيتها فهو تصور بالكنه الإجمالي وما يمكننا من تعريفاتهما إنما هو الرسم لعدم الاطلاع على ذاتيات الماهية الحقيقية وهو يفيد العلم بالوجه وقد مر تفصيله. قوله: كمال أول للشفاف من حيث هو شفاف وتحقيقه ان من الأجسام ما شأنه أن لا يحجب تأثير المضيء فيما وراءه كالهواء والماء وهو الشفاف وما من شأنه الحجب فمنه ما شأنه يرى من غير احتياج إلى حضور شيء آخر بعد وجود المتوسط الشفاف وهو المضيء كالشمس ومنه ما يحتاج إليه وهو الملون فإنه يحتاج في ظهوره ورؤيته إلى الضوء، والشفاف إنما يصير شفافاً بالفعل لوجود الضوء فالضوء ما يتم به شفافيته ويصير به شفافاً بالفعل بلا توسط أمر آخر فيكون كمالاً ذاتياً له بخلاف اللون فإنه كمال للملون من حيث ملونيته ليس بكمال ذاتي له بل بواسطة الضوء ولذا فسره في الشفاء بكيفية يكمل بالضوء من شأنها أنه يصير الجسم مانعاً لفعل المضيء فيما يتوسط ذلك للجسم بينه وبين المضيء. قوله: والمراد بكونه كمالاً أول إلخ أي ليس الأول هاهنا بالقياس إلى الكمال الثاني كما في تعريف النفس والحركة بل أن لا يكون كمالاً بواسطة أمر آخر ومن هذا ظهر أن تبديل لفظ بذاتها على ما في الشفاء والمباحث من أنه كيفية هو كمال بذاتها للشفاف بقوله: أول تبديل مخل. قوله: يتوقف إبصارها أي بذاتها فلا يرد الكيفيات المبصرة بتبع اللون. قوله: أي كيفية يتوقف إبصارها أي إبصارها بالذات وبه يخرج الشكل فإنه كيفية يتوقف إبصارها على إبصار الضوء واللون لكنه لا يبصر بالذات.
Know that they cannot be defined, that is, light and color cannot be defined, due to their obviousness. For sensation of their particulars has apprised us of their quiddity; what we can offer of their definitions, even if they were correct, does not suffice, as was stated in the discussion of heat. And what is said in their definition, that light is a primary perfection of the transparent insofar as it is transparent, and the qualification 'insofar as' was considered because light is not a perfection of the transparent in its corporeality nor in anything else, but in its transparency; and the intended meaning of its being a primary perfection is that it is an essential perfection, not accidental. Or it is a quality whose vision does not depend on the vision of something else. And that color is the opposite of it, that is, a quality whose vision depends on the vision of something else, which is light; for color, unless it becomes illuminated, is not visible. So it is a definition by the more obscure, as is not hidden. Perhaps the intended meaning of what he mentioned is to draw attention to their properties and rulings, so that their distinction increases. And since the vision of color is conditioned by the vision of light, he mentioned each of them in a section and said: let us make their discussions two sections. His statement: 'what we can offer does not suffice' because what is obtained in the mind after removing the individuators of particulars is their very quiddity, so it is a conception by overall essence, and what we can offer of their definitions is only a description (al-rasm) due to lack of knowledge of the essentialia of the true quiddity, and it gives knowledge by aspect, and its detail has been given. His statement: 'primary perfection of the transparent insofar as it is transparent' – its verification is that among bodies, there are those whose property is not to obstruct the effect of the luminous thing beyond it, like air and water, and that is the transparent. And what has the property of obstruction includes that whose property is to be seen without need for the presence of something else after the existence of the transparent medium, and that is the luminous, like the sun, and what needs that, and that is the colored, for it needs light for its appearance and vision. The transparent only becomes actually transparent by the existence of light; so light is that by which its transparency is perfected and by which it becomes actually transparent without the mediation of another thing, so it is an essential perfection for it. Unlike color, for it is a perfection of the colored insofar as it is colored, not an essential perfection for it, but by mediation of light. For this reason, it was explained in the Shifa as a quality that is perfected by light, whose property is that the body becomes an obstruction to the action of the luminous thing regarding what mediates that body between it and the luminous thing. His statement: 'and the intended meaning of its being a primary perfection, etc.' – that is, 'primary' here is not in relation to the second perfection as in the definition of the soul and motion, but rather that it is not a perfection by mediation of another thing. From this it is apparent that replacing the wording 'by itself' as in the Shifa and the Mabahith, that it is a quality that is a perfection by itself of the transparent, with his saying 'primary' is a replacement that corrupts. His statement: 'its vision depends' – that is, vision of it by essence, so the qualities seen by following color are not included. His statement: 'i.e., a quality whose vision depends' – that is, its vision by essence, and by this shape is excluded, for it is a quality whose vision depends on the vision of light and color, but it is not seen by essence.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definitions of light and color. It begins by stating that light and color cannot be defined because they are obvious; sensory acquaintance with their particulars reveals their quiddity, and any definition we propose is insufficient, as was stated in the discussion of heat. Then it presents two definitions: (1) light is a primary perfection (kamāl awwal) of the transparent (al-shaffāf) insofar as it is transparent, and (2) light is a quality whose vision does not depend on the vision of something else; color is the opposite, a quality whose vision depends on the vision of light. The commentary notes that these are definitions by the more obscure (taʿrīf bi-al-akhfā) and that their purpose is to highlight properties and rulings. Since the vision of color depends on light, the author decided to make the discussions of light and color into two sections. Then the commentary explains why definitions do not suffice: what we obtain in the mind is the quiddity itself after removing particulars, so it is a conceptualization by overall essence; our definitions are only descriptions (rasm) because we lack knowledge of the essentialia of the true quiddity. Then it verifies the first definition in detail: bodies are divided into transparent (like air and water, which do not obstruct the luminous) and non-transparent. Among non-transparent bodies, some are luminous (like the sun, seen without need for another thing after the transparent medium) and some are colored (needing light). The transparent becomes actually transparent by light, so light is an essential perfection for it. Color, by contrast, is a perfection of the colored by mediation of light. It references the Shifa's definition. The commentary clarifies that 'primary' here does not mean 'relative to a second perfection' (as in definitions of the soul or motion) but means 'not by mediation of another thing'. It notes that replacing 'by itself' with 'primary' as in some texts corrupts the meaning. Finally, it explains that the definition of color includes the condition that its vision is by essence, thus excluding shape, which depends on light and color for its vision but is not seen by essence.

Key Terms
الضوء
اللون
كمال أول
الشفاف
التعريف
الرسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definitions given are real definitions that capture the essence of light and color.
Correction: They are only descriptions (rasm) not definitions proper; the passage explicitly says they are 'definitions by the more obscure'.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage say that light and color cannot be defined?
A) Because they are composite and complex.
B) Because they are obvious and known directly by sensation, so any definition is insufficient and merely a description.
C) Because they are accidents and cannot be defined.
D) Because there is disagreement about their nature.

Key Takeaway
Light and color are known through sensation, and their definitions are only reminders of their properties.

Detailed Analysis of Light as Primary Perfection
Commentary explains light as primary perfection of the transparent, body classification, and corrects a textual issue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed verification of light as a primary perfection of the transparent, the classification of bodies, and the clarification of the term 'primary'.

Core Idea
Light is an essential perfection of the transparent, actualizing its transparency without mediation.

After stating the definitions, the commentator delves into the first definition of light, verifying why light is a primary perfection of the transparent. He explains the classification of bodies into transparent, luminous, and colored, and clarifies that 'primary' here means not by mediation, not relative to a second perfection. He also addresses a textual substitution found in other works and concludes by excluding shape from the definition of color.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. This passage is a detailed explanation of the earlier definitions.
It begins with 'قوله: لا يفي به ما يمكننا'
2
Understand the classification of bodies
The text divides bodies into transparent (does not obstruct), luminous (seen without need for another light), and colored (needs light). Notice how light perfects the transparent.
This classification is key to the definition.
3
Grasp why light is an essential perfection
Light makes the transparent actually transparent without mediation of another thing, so it is an essential (dhātī) perfection. Color is different because it depends on light.
Contrast light and color here.
4
Clarify the meaning of 'primary'
The commentator explains that 'primary' (awwal) here does not mean relative to a second perfection, but rather that the perfection is not by mediation of another thing.
This corrects a potential misunderstanding from other contexts.
5
Note the textual correction and exclusion of shape
The passage points out that replacing the wording 'by itself' with 'primary' in some texts corrupts the meaning. Also, shape is excluded from the definition of color because it is not seen by essence.
These are important nuances.

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say 'primary' (كمال أول) means in the definition of light?
A) It is the first perfection, followed by a second perfection.
B) It is an essential perfection not attained through mediation of another thing.
C) It is the most important perfection.
D) It is a perfection that belongs to the transparent accidentally.

Key Takeaway
Light is the actualizer of transparency, and 'primary' here means without mediation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says our definitions are only descriptions (rasm) because we lack knowledge of the true essence (haqiqa). In your own spiritual life, have you settled for a 'description' of your faith—knowing what it looks like from the outside—or have you sought its true essence? What one step can you take today to move from rasm to haqiqa in your relationship with Allah?
?
Light is described as an essential perfection that actualizes transparency without mediation. What is the 'light' that gives your soul life and transparency to the truth? Are you relying on secondary perfections—comfort, status, praise—to feel alive, or have you sought that unmediated, essential perfection? What will you do this week to seek it directly?
?
The commentator corrects a textual change that would corrupt the meaning. Have you ever allowed convenient reinterpretations of Islamic teachings to change the true meaning for your own benefit? Ask yourself honestly: In what area of faith or practice have I been careless with the original intent? How will you return to the source to correct your understanding starting today?

Whiteness Through Light Mixing: Denying Exclusive Temperamental Causality
Commentator affirms mixing illuminated air with transparent parts causes whiteness, denying need for temperament.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's position that whiteness can be caused by the mixing of illuminated air with transparent parts, independent of temperamental change, and how this is defended through various arguments.

Core Idea
Whiteness can come from light mixing, not just temperamental color change.

The commentator defends the view that whiteness in foam and similar examples is real, arising from light interacting with transparent parts. This counters a narrower view that only temperamental change produces color. The passage unpacks the base text's affirmations and responds to potential objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Get a sense of the commentator's line of reasoning.
Note the repeated use of 'قوله' to introduce explanations of the base text.
2
Identify the core claim
Find where the commentator affirms that mixing illuminated air with transparent parts is a cause of whiteness even without temperament. This is the central thesis.
The phrase 'أحد أسباب حدوث البياض' is key.
3
Analyze the supporting sub-arguments
Examine each 'قوله' section: transformation, accusation, clarification, denial of sophistry, and pulverization analogy. Understand how each supports the main claim.
These are not separate points but reinforcements of the same idea.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this discussion fits into Islamic cosmology and causality debates. The commentator is rejecting a purely temperamental theory of color.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلذلك قال صاحب الكتاب : ( والحق منعه ) أي منع أن لا بياض فيما ذكروه من الأمثلة ) والقول بأنَّ ذلك ) أي اختلاط الهواء المضيء بالأجزاء الشفافة ( أحد أسباب حدوث البياض وإن لم يكن هناك مزاج يتبعه حدوث اللون . قوله : ( أفادهما مزاجاً إلخ) فيكون حدوث البياض بطريق الاستحالة . قوله: (وارتفاع الأمان إلخ) لأنه حكم بوجود البياض في الأمثلة المذكورة ولا بياض في الحقيقة فيكون متهما ولا شهادة لمتهم . قوله : ( وهو أنه قد صرح فيما نقلناه إلخ) من قوله : وكان أصل البياض هو الضوء الذي استحال ببعض الوجوه ومن قوله : أن البياض بالحقيقة في الأشياء ليس بضوء فإنه كالتصريح بأن البياض في الأمثلة المذكورة ضوء مستحيل . وقيل : المراد أنه صرح فيما نقلناه وإن لم يكن ذلك المصرح مذكوراً هاهنا ولا يخفى بعده. قوله : ( وجعله بياضاً حادثاً) حيث قال : لا أعلم حدوث البياض بطريق آخر، وقال أيضاً بحث المزاج: إنَّ كثيراً من الأعراض يعرضه أيضاً بسبب مخالطة غير مزاجية وذكر الأمثلة المذكورة. قوله : ( وليس في هذا سفسطة لأنه لم يقل بأنه لا بياض وأنه متخيل كما قاله القدماء بل أنه أمر موجود حدث بطريق انعكاس الضوء من الهواء على الأجزاء المشفة . قوله : ( وإلا كان السحق إلخ قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك لتفاوت التخلخلين والحق هذا فإنَّ الطبخ يكثر الحجم بخلاف السحق . قوله : ( وهو أنه قد صرح إلخ ) وإن لم يذكر المصرح به هاهنا .
Therefore the author of the book said: 'And the truth is to deny it'—that is, to deny that there is no whiteness in the examples they mentioned—'and the statement that that'—i.e., the mixing of illuminated air with transparent parts—'is one of the causes of the occurrence of whiteness, even if there is no temperament that is followed by the occurrence of color.' His statement: 'It gave them a temperament, etc.'—so the occurrence of whiteness is by way of transformation. His statement: 'And loss of trust, etc.'—because he judged the existence of whiteness in the mentioned examples while in reality there is no whiteness, so he is accused and there is no testimony for an accused. His statement: 'And that he stated clearly in what we quoted, etc.'—from his statement: 'And the origin of whiteness is the light that was transformed in some respects' and from his statement: 'That whiteness in reality in things is not light'—for it is like a clear statement that the whiteness in the mentioned examples is light that has been transformed. And it is said: It means that he stated clearly in what we quoted even if that which was clearly stated is not mentioned here, and its remoteness is not hidden. His statement: 'And he made it a whiteness occurring'—where he said: 'I do not know the occurrence of whiteness by another way' and also said in the discussion of temperament: 'Many accidents also occur due to non-temperamental mixing' and mentioned the examples mentioned. His statement: 'And there is no sophistry in this'—because he did not say that there is no whiteness and that it is imaginary as the ancients said, but rather it is an existing thing that occurred by way of reflection of light from air onto transparent parts. His statement: 'Otherwise pulverization, etc.'—it was said: Why is it not permissible that that is due to the difference in rarefactions? And the truth is this, for cooking increases volume unlike pulverization. His statement: 'And that he stated clearly, etc.'—even if the clearly stated thing is not mentioned here.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's (sahib al-kitab) statement affirming the truth: the correct position is to deny that there is no whiteness in the examples of foam etc., and instead affirm that mixing illuminated air with transparent parts is one of the causes of whiteness, even without a temperamental change. The commentator then explains each part of the base text: 'it gave them a temperament' refers to the occurrence of whiteness by transformation (istihala). 'Loss of trust' refers to the accusation against Ibn Sina if he had denied whiteness. 'He stated clearly in what we quoted' is explained by Ibn Sina's earlier words: 'the origin of whiteness is light transformed in some respects' and 'whiteness in reality is not light'—together they imply that the whiteness in the examples is transformed light. The commentator notes an alternative interpretation. Further, 'he made it a whiteness occurring' is linked to Ibn Sina's statement that he does not know another way for whiteness, and that many accidents occur through non-temperamental mixing. The commentator then restates that there is no sophistry because Ibn Sina did not deny whiteness but considered it an actual thing arising from light reflection. Finally, he addresses an objection about pulverization versus cooking, explaining that cooking increases volume while pulverization does not.

Key Terms
الحق
منع
اختلاط الهواء المضيء
الأجزاء الشفافة
المزاج
الاستحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some hold that whiteness only occurs through temperamental change (mizaj) and not through other causes like light mixing.
Correction: The commentator argues that mixing illuminated air with transparent parts is a valid cause of whiteness even without temperament, as supported by Ibn Sina's statements.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator identify as one cause of whiteness even without temperament?
A) The pulverization of transparent parts.
B) The mixing of illuminated air with transparent parts.
C) The cooking of objects.
D) The direct presence of light alone.

Key Takeaway
Whiteness can be caused by the reflection of light from air onto transparent parts, without requiring a change in temperament.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The commentator insists that whiteness in foam is real, not imaginary. Are there aspects of your faith or knowledge that you dismiss as 'mere appearances' instead of real? What would it mean to take them as genuine?
?
When you see whiteness in foam, do you trust your senses or abstract theories? Where in your life do you need to reconcile sensory experience with higher knowledge?

Black and White as Primary Colors and Their Mixtures
Enumerates how black and white as primaries yield all other colors through mixing with light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theory that black and white are the two primary colors and how other colors are derived from them through composition with light.

Core Idea
All colors are mixtures of black and white, with light variations.

This passage presents a classical theory of color, held by some scholars, that posits black and white as the two original colors. All other colors are produced by mixing them in various proportions and conditions of light. The text offers a detailed classification of how specific colors emerge from such mixtures, providing a systematic account of the visible spectrum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the theory.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice that the passage begins by asserting black and white as the two primaries, and that other colors are composites.
3
Trace the color derivations
Follow the sequence of mixtures: black+white alone gives dust-color; then with light and smoke, red, duskiness, yellow, green, etc. Note the conditions (predominance of black or light).
4
Understand the principle of analogy
The passage ends by saying to analogize the rest of the colors, indicating this is a systematic method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اعترف بوجودهما أعني وجود السواد والبياض قال : أي بعضهم هما الأصل والبواقي من الألوان تحصل بالتركيب منهما على أنحاء شتى فإنهما إذا خلطا وحدهما حصلت الغبرة وإذا خلطا لا وحدهما بل مع ضوء كفيء الغمام الذي أشرقت عليه الشمس والدخان الذي خالطه النار حصلت الحمرة إن غلب السواد على الضوء في الجملة وإن اشتدت غلبته عليه فالقتمة ومع غلبة الضوء على السواد حصلت الصفرة وإن خالطها أي الصفرة سواد مشرق فالخضرة والخضرة إذا خلطت مع بياض حصلت الزنجارية التي هي الكهبة وإذا خلطت الخضرة مع سواد حصلت الكراثية الشديدة والكراثية إن خلط بها سواد مع قليل حمرة حصلت النيلية . ثم النيلية إن خلط بها حمرة حصلت الأرجوانية، وعلى هذا فقس حال سائر الألوان.
And among those who affirm their existence—i.e., the existence of black and white—some said: they are the two primaries, and the remaining colors are obtained by composition from them in various ways. For if they are mixed alone, dust-color (ghubra) results. And if they are mixed not alone but with the light of the shadow of a cloud on which the sun has shone and with smoke mixed with fire, redness results—if black predominates over light in general; and if its predominance intensifies, then duskiness (qutma); and with predominance of light over black, yellowness results. And if yellowness is mixed with bright black, then greenness results. And if greenness is mixed with whiteness, then azure (zanjariyya) which is dun-color (kuhba) results. And if greenness is mixed with black, then intense leek-color (kurrāthiyya) results. And if leek-color is mixed with black with a little redness, then indigo (nīliyya) results. Then if indigo is mixed with redness, then purple (arjuwāniyya) results. And on this, analogize the case of the rest of the colors.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and classification of colors according to a theory that black and white are the two primary colors, and all other colors are composites of them, sometimes with the addition of light. The commentator reports that some scholars who affirm the existence of black and white hold that they are the origin (asl). The passage then provides a detailed breakdown of how specific colors are produced: mixing black and white alone gives dust-color (ghubra). Mixing them with light from a cloud shadow and with fire-tinged smoke yields red if black predominates, and duskiness (qutma) if black predominates more strongly. If light predominates, yellow results. Yellow mixed with bright black gives green. Green mixed with white gives azure (zanjariyya), also called dun-color. Green mixed with black gives intense leek-color. Leek-color mixed with black and a little red gives indigo. Indigo mixed with red gives purple. The commentator then says to apply this analogy to all other colors, implying that all colors can be derived from these two primaries with variations of mixing and light conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many colors are simple and not composed of other colors.
Correction: This theory holds that all colors are composites of black and white, with light variations.

Checkpoint

A) Redness
B) Yellowness
C) Dust-color (ghubra)
D) Greenness

Key Takeaway
According to this theory, black and white are the two primary colors, and all other colors are produced by mixing them under varying light conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you pause today to actually see His wisdom in a single color, or will you remain blind to it?
?
The theory reduces all colors to just two primaries: black and white. This simplicity from complexity is a sign of the Creator's oneness (tawheed). When you look at a rainbow or a sunset, is your heart moved to proclaim 'Allahu Akbar'? If not, what is preventing you from seeing His signs? Commit to one specific time today to look for His hand in the colors around you.

Darkness as Privation of Light
Al-Iji defines darkness as privation of light with cave proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of darkness as the privation of light and the rational proof that establishes its non-existence as a positive entity.

Core Idea
Darkness has no positive reality; it is merely the absence of light.

This lesson examines a classic kalam proposition: that darkness is not a thing in itself but simply the lack of light. Al-Iji offers a clever proof from asymmetrical vision that demonstrates why darkness cannot be an independent entity. Let's work through the definition and the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: definition, type of opposition, proof, and clarifications.
Pay attention to the parenthetical comments by the commentator.
2
Understand the definition and its qualification
Darkness is defined as absence of light from that which is normally luminous. This excludes transparent bodies. Identify why this qualification is necessary.
Transparent objects, like air, lack light but are not considered dark because they are not meant to be luminous.
3
Analyze the cave proof
Work through the cave example step by step. Why does the asymmetrical vision prove that darkness is not a real obstacle? What would happen if darkness were a positive entity?
The proof relies on the difference in visibility between the insider and outsider.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : الظلمة عدم الضوء ( الظلمة عدم الضوء عما من شأنه أن يكون مضيئاً ) فالتقابل بينهما تقابل العدم والملكة ( والدليل على أنه أمر عدمي رؤية الجالس في الغار المظلم (الخارج) عنه إذا وقع على الخارج ضوء ولا) (عكس) أي لا يرى الخارج الجالس ( وما هو ) أي ليس الحال المذكور من الجانبين ( إلا لأنه ليس الظلام ( أمراً حقيقياً قائماً بالهواء مانعاً الإبصار) إذ لو كان كذلك لم ير أحدهما الآخر أصلاً لوجود العائق عن الرؤية بينهما فتعين أنها عدم الضوء وحينئذ ينتفي شرط كون الجالس في الغار مرئياً فلا يرى دون شرط كون الخارج مرئياً فيرى فلذلك اختلف حالهما. قوله : ( عما من شأنه إلخ ) احتراز عن الشفافية فإنه يصدق عليه عدم الضوء لكن ليس عما شأنه الضوء إذ الشفافية ليس من شأنها الضوء كما صرح به الشيخ في الشفاء. وقال : الجسم إما شفاف أو ملون أو مضيء. قوله : ( أي ليس الحال المذكور إلخ) أشار بذلك إلى أنَّ الاستدلال بالاختلاف المستفاد من مجموع قوله : رؤية الجالس فإنه يدل على عدم كون الظلمة عائقة لا على عدمها، وكذا قوله : ولا عكس لا يدل على شيء منهما بل على عدم الرؤية فقط.
The Third Proposition: Darkness is the absence of light. (Darkness is the absence of light from that which is normally luminous.) So the opposition between them is the opposition of privation and possession. (The proof that it is a privation is the sight of the one sitting in a dark cave (the outside) when light falls on the outside, and (the converse) i.e., the outside does not see the one sitting. (And what is it) i.e., the mentioned state from both sides is not (except because darkness is not a real entity subsisting in the air preventing sight) because if it were so, neither would see the other at all due to the existence of an obstacle to vision between them. So it is established that it is the absence of light, and then the condition for the one in the cave being visible is negated, so he is not seen, while the condition for the outside being visible is present, so he is seen. Therefore their states differ.) His words: ('from that which is normally luminous' etc.) is a precaution against transparency, for absence of light applies to it but not from what is normally luminous, since transparency is not normally luminous, as the Shaykh stated in al-Shifa'. He said: Body is either transparent, colored, or luminous. His words: ('i.e., the mentioned state' etc.) He indicated by that that the inference from the difference derived from the totality of his words 'the sight of the one sitting' indicates that darkness is not an obstacle, not that it is non-existent. Likewise, his words 'nor the converse' do not indicate either of them but only the absence of sight.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage defines darkness and provides a proof. It begins by stating the proposition: darkness is the absence of light from that which is normally luminous. This excludes transparency, which also lacks light but is not normally luminous. The opposition between light and darkness is that of privation and possession. The proof is from the cave example: a person inside a dark cave can see someone outside when light falls on the latter, but the outsider cannot see the insider. If darkness were a real entity subsisting in the air and preventing sight, then neither would see the other because the obstacle would block both directions. Since vision is asymmetrical, darkness cannot be a real obstacle; it is merely the absence of light. The commentator explains that the proof relies on the difference in vision (one sees, the other does not) rather than on the mere fact of non-vision, which could be due to other causes. The clarification about 'from that which is normally luminous' ensures that transparent bodies are not considered dark, as they are not in the category of things that can be illuminated.

Key Terms
الظلمة
الضوء
العدم
التقابل
العدم والملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Darkness is a positive entity like a color or a substance that fills a space.
Correction: The cave proof shows that darkness cannot be a real entity, otherwise it would block vision in both directions. It is merely the absence of light.

Checkpoint
What is the proof that darkness is the absence of light?
A) The Quranic verse "He created darkness and light"
B) The cave example showing asymmetrical vision
C) The branch about stars not being seen in daylight
D) The objection about light surrounding the object

Key Takeaway
Darkness is not a thing; it is the absence of light from what should be luminous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a 'dark' situation in your life—confusion, difficulty, or spiritual emptiness—do you treat it as a real, unchangeable barrier, or as a lack of something that you can actively bring in (like knowledge, patience, or faith)? How does this lesson's definition of darkness as mere absence change your approach to such challenges?
?
If darkness is just the absence of light, then what is your personal responsibility to 'bring light' into your own life and the lives of others? Where have you accepted darkness as a given instead of seeking to fill the absence?
?
The proof in this lesson shows that the failure to see is not due to a positive obstacle but to a missing condition. Where in your relationship with Allah have you assumed a positive barrier (e.g., 'I cannot change because of my past') when in reality it is only the absence of something you could seek (e.g., sincere repentance)? How will you seek that presence today?

The Philosophers' Definition of Light as Color's Appearance
Sanusi presents the philosophical definition of light and shade.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that light is not a separate quality but the color itself at its maximal appearance.

Core Idea
Light is just color fully appearing, not an added quality.

The philosophers put forward a striking redefinition of light. They deny that light is a quality that attaches to color. Instead, they argue that light is simply the color itself when it becomes fully apparent. This lesson examines this core definition and the related concepts of darkness and shade.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on light and shade
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the philosophers define absolute light, absolute darkness, and the intermediate shade.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا كيفية موجودة زائدة عليه'.
2
Identify the central definition
Find the sentence that explicitly states what light is according to the philosophers. It says light is the color appearing in varying degrees.
3
Understand the rejection of a superadded quality
The passage denies that light is a quality (كيفية) added to color. Instead, it is the color itself appearing more completely.
4
Reflect on the perceptual example
The passage mentions how the sense, when accustomed to one level, mistakenly perceives brilliance when encountering a higher level. This illustrates the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضوء والخفاء المطلق هو الظلمة والمتوسط بينهما هو الظل وتختلف مراتبه بحسب القرب والبعد من الطرفين فإذا ألف الحس مرتبة من تلك المراتب ثم شاهد ما هو أكثر ظهوراً من الأول يحسب أنَّ هناك بريقاً ولمعاناً وليس الأمر كذلك بل ليس هناك كيفية زائدة على اللون الذي ظهر ظهوراً أتم فالضوء هو اللون الظاهر على مراتب مختلفة لا كيفية موجودة زائدة عليه فإن أورد عليه أنا ندرك التفرقة بين اللون المستنير وبين اللون المظلم . قالوا : إِنَّ ذلك بسبب أنَّ أحدهما خفي والآخر ظاهر لا بسبب كيفية أخرى موجودة مع المستنير وقد بالغ بعضهم في ذلك حتى قال : إِنَّ ضوء الشمس ليس إلا الظهور التام للونه ولما اشتد ظهوره ومبلغ الغاية في ذلك بهر الأبصار حتى خفي اللون لا لخفائه في نفسه بل لعجز البصر عن إدراك ما هو جلي في الغاية هذا تقرير مذهبهم
Light and absolute obscurity is darkness, and what is intermediate between them is shade, and its levels vary according to proximity and distance from the two extremes. When the sense becomes accustomed to one of those levels, then observes what is more apparent than the first, it thinks there is brilliance and glitter, but it is not so. Rather, there is no additional quality beyond the color that has appeared more completely. So light is the color that appears in varying degrees, not an existing quality superadded to it. If it is objected: we perceive a distinction between an illuminated color and a dark color. They said: that is because one is hidden and the other is apparent, not because of another quality existing with the illuminated. Some of them went so far as to say that sunlight is nothing but the complete appearance of its color, and when its appearance intensifies and reaches the utmost degree, it dazzles the eyes so that the color is hidden—not because it is hidden in itself, but because of the inability of the sight to perceive what is extremely evident. This is the formulation of their school.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage defines the philosophers' view on light. It begins by explaining absolute light and darkness, then describes levels of light and shade. The core claim: light is not an additional quality but the color itself when it appears more fully. An objection is raised: we perceive a difference between illuminated and dark colors. The philosophers respond by attributing that to one being hidden and the other apparent, not to a separate quality. They support this by arguing that sunlight, when at its peak, overwhelms the eyes so that the color itself is not seen due to the observer's weakness, not due to any additional quality. The passage concludes by stating this is their doctrine.

Key Terms
الضوء
الخفاء المطلق
الظل
الكيفية الزائدة
الحس
اللون الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light is a separate quality added to color.
Correction: The philosophers argue that light is the color itself in its state of maximal appearance; there is no additional quality.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is light?
A) A quality superadded to color
B) The appearance of the color itself in varying degrees
C) A separate substance emanating from the sun
D) The absence of darkness

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define light as the color in its state of full appearance, not as a separate quality added to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a situation where your awareness of a past sin intensifies, do you assume that you have become worse, or could it be that the truth about yourself is simply appearing more fully? What would change if you saw it as greater clarity rather than added guilt?
?
The philosopher says that our sense can be fooled into thinking there is 'brilliance and glitter' when there is only fuller appearance. Where in your spiritual life have you mistaken an emotional rush or a new experience for something extraordinary, when in fact it was just the same reality becoming more apparent?
?
If the 'brilliance' you perceive is not an added quality but just a more complete appearance, then what might you be avoiding because it feels like a burden, when it is actually the truth becoming clearer? Will you embrace it as clarity rather than reject it as foreign?

Response to the Objection: No Separate Quality Needed
Sanusi presents the objection and the philosophers' response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' response to the objection that we perceive a difference between illuminated and dark colors.

Core Idea
The difference is due to one being apparent and the other hidden, not a separate quality.

The philosophers' definition of light might seem challenged by our everyday experience: we clearly see a difference between a color in light and the same color in shadow. This lesson presents their defense, arguing that the perceived difference is solely one of appearance, and uses the extreme case of sunlight to underline their point.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage starting from 'فإن أورد عليه' through the end. The objection states that we perceive a difference between an illuminated color and a dark one.
Notice the structure: objection, then 'قالوا' for the response.
2
Understand the philosophers' reply
They attribute the difference to one color being hidden (خفي) and the other apparent (ظاهر), not to an extra quality.
3
Analyze the example of bright sunlight
Some philosophers go further: sunlight is just the complete appearance of its color. When it is extremely intense, it dazzles the eyes, causing the color to seem hidden—not because it is hidden, but because sight cannot perceive it.
This reinforces that no extra quality is present.
4
Draw the final conclusion
The passage ends by stating 'هذا تقرير مذهبهم' – this is the formulation of their school. The entire argument denies any superadded quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Light and absolute obscurity is darkness, and what is intermediate between them is shade, and its levels vary according to proximity and distance from the two extremes. When the sense becomes accustomed to one of those levels, then observes what is more apparent than the first, it thinks there is brilliance and glitter, but it is not so. Rather, there is no additional quality beyond the color that has appeared more completely. So light is the color that appears in varying degrees, not an existing quality superadded to it. Some of them went so far as to say that sunlight is nothing but the complete appearance of its color, and when its appearance intensifies and reaches the utmost degree, it dazzles the eyes so that the color is hidden—not because it is hidden in itself, but because of the inability of the sight to perceive what is extremely evident. This is the formulation of their school.

Commentary
This passage defines the philosophers' view on light. It begins by explaining absolute light and darkness, then describes levels of light and shade. The core claim: light is not an additional quality but the color itself when it appears more fully. An objection is raised: we perceive a difference between illuminated and dark colors. The philosophers respond by attributing that to one being hidden and the other apparent, not to a separate quality. They support this by arguing that sunlight, when at its peak, overwhelms the eyes so that the color itself is not seen due to the observer's weakness, not due to any additional quality. The passage concludes by stating this is their doctrine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That light is a separate entity that causes colors to become visible.
Correction: The philosophers maintain that the difference between illuminated and dark colors is solely a difference in the degree of appearance of the color itself, not due to an added quality.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' response, why do we perceive a difference between an illuminated color and a dark color?
A) Because there is a quality of light present in one and absent in the other
B) Because one is hidden and the other is apparent
C) Because illuminated colors are made of a different substance
D) Because the sense of sight is deceived

Key Takeaway
The philosophers defend their definition by explaining that perceived differences between illuminated and dark colors are due to degrees of appearance, not a separate quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a spiritual 'darkness' in your life (e.g., doubt, lack of connection), do you automatically assume it is because something is missing, or could it be that a truth is so evident it has become hidden from your heart? What would it take for you to stop and allow that truth to appear again?
?
The philosophers say sunlight is so apparent it becomes unseen. In your daily life, what 'sunlight' of Allah's signs or mercy has become so familiar that you no longer perceive it? Will you commit today to notice one such thing with fresh eyes?
?
If the difference between light and dark is not a separate quality but a degree of appearance, then perhaps your spiritual struggle is not about acquiring something new but about unveiling what is already there. What hidden appearance of faith might you be ignoring in yourself, and what step will you take to let it fully appear?

Light and Illumination: The Two Types of Luminosity
Classification of light into self-luminous and borrowed illumination, with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical distinction between light (ضوء) as an inherent quality of self-luminous bodies and illumination (نور) as a borrowed quality in bodies illuminated by another.

Core Idea
Self-luminous bodies have light; illuminated bodies have borrowed illumination.

The commentator begins by classifying the degrees of light. He distinguishes between two types: the light inherent in a self-luminous body, such as the sun, and the illumination that arises in a body from another luminous source, like the moon or the earth's surface lit by the sun. This distinction is grounded in the Quranic verse. In this lesson, we will examine this classification and the evidence for it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the definitions of light (ضوء) and illumination (نور). Notice that light is for self-luminous bodies like the sun, while illumination is for bodies that derive light from another, like the moon.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Read the verse from Surah Yunus and consider why the commentator uses it to support the distinction.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this technical distinction is important for understanding physical and metaphysical light in Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : مراتب الضوء في مراتبه ) أي مراتب الضوء مطلقاً ( القائم بالمضيء لذاته هو الضوء ) أي قد يخص هذا الاسم بالكيفية الحاصلة للجسم المضيء في ذاته بعد إطلاقه على ما يعمها وغيرها ( كما في الشمس) وما عدا القمر من الكواكب فإنها مستضيئة لذواتها غير مستفيدة ضوءها من مضيء آخر ( و ) القائم ( بالمضيء لغيره نور ) إذا كان ذلك مضيئاً لذاته ( كما في القمر ووجه الأرض المستضيء بضوء الشمس فإذا قوبل الغير و الضوء بالنور أريد بهما هذان المعنيان ( قال : ( الله تعالى هو الذي جعل الشمس . ضياء والقمر نوراً ﴾ [ يونس : ٥] ، والحاصل في الجسم من مقابلة المضيء لغيره هو الظل، كالحاصل على وجه الأرض حال الإسفار وعقيب الغروب فإنه مستفاد من الهواء المضيء بالشمس وكالحاصل على وجه الأرض من مقابلة القمر المستنير بالشمس. قوله : ( فإنه مستفاد من الهواء المضيء بالشمس لكن لا بطريق الانعكاس كما صرح به في الملخص واستدل عليه . ثم إِنَّ فيما ذكر إشارة إلى اندفاع الاعتراض المشهور على أن المضيء يضيء، إلا المقابل وهو أنا نرى وجه الأرض عند الإسفار مضيئا وهذه الاستضاءة من الشمس التي هي غير مقابلة إياه حينئذ، ووجه الدفع تقرر كون الاستضاءة لا بطريق الانعكاس أن تلك الاستضاءة من الهواء المستضيء بالشمس المقابل للأرض .
The second objective: The degrees of light. (In its degrees) i.e., the degrees of light absolutely. (What subsists in the self-luminous is light) i.e., this name may be particularized to the quality subsisting in the self-luminous body after its application to what is common to it and other things (as in the sun). And excluding the moon, the planets are self-luminous, not deriving their light from another luminous source. (And) what subsists (in the luminous by another is illumination) if that is self-luminous (as in the moon and the face of the earth illuminated by sunlight. So when the general and the specific are compared and light and illumination are meant by them, these two meanings are intended. He said: God Almighty is He who made the sun a light and the moon an illumination. (Yunus 10:5). And what results in the body from the interposition of the luminous by another is shade, like what results on the face of the earth at dawn and after sunset, for it is derived from the air illuminated by the sun, and like what results on the face of the earth from the interposition of the moon illuminated by the sun. His saying: (For it is derived from the air illuminated by the sun, but not by way of reflection, as he stated in al-Mulakhkhas and argued for it. Then in what was mentioned there is an indication of the repulsion of the famous objection that the luminous illuminates only what is opposite it, while we see the face of the earth at dawn luminous, and this illumination is from the sun which is not facing it at that time. The repelling point is that the illumination is not by way of reflection, because that illumination is from the air that is illuminated by the sun and facing the earth.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the degrees of light. It distinguishes between ضوء (light) as a quality inherent in self-luminous bodies like the sun (and according to the commentator, all planets except the moon), and نور (illumination) as a quality in a body that shines by borrowing light from a self-luminous source, like the moon and the earth's surface illuminated by sunlight. The Quranic verse (Yunus 10:5) is cited as evidence for this terminological distinction. Then it defines الظل (shade) as the result of the interposition of an opaque body between a luminous source and a surface; examples are the earth's face at dawn and after sunset, where the shade comes from the air illuminated by the sun, not directly. Finally, a commentary note clarifies that this shade is not by reflection, and it addresses the objection that the sun should only illuminate what it directly faces—the answer is that the earth at dawn is illuminated indirectly via the air that is illuminated and faces the earth.

ضوء (light)
نور (illumination)
مضيء لذاته (self-luminous)
مضيء لغيره (luminous by another)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light and illumination are synonymous terms for any luminous phenomenon.
Correction: The passage shows that light (ضوء) is specific to self-luminous bodies, while illumination (نور) is for bodies that derive light from another source.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of 'illumination' (نور)?
A) The sun
B) The moon
C) A self-luminous star
D) Fire

Key Takeaway
Light is inherent to self-luminous bodies, while illumination is borrowed from another source.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself trying to shine with borrowed light—seeking validation, knowledge, or spirituality from others—rather than seeking the Self-Luminous Source directly? What would change if you turned to God first?
?
If your faith is like the moon's light, dependent on external sources (teachers, books, routine), what happens when those sources are obscured? How can you cultivate a self-sustaining light from the Qur'an and sunnah?
?
The sun gives light without diminishing; God gives without losing. Are you generous with what you have, or do you hoard, fearing scarcity? How can you reflect God's attribute of giving starting today?

Shade: Definition and the Refutation of an Objection
Definition of shade and refutation of the objection regarding indirect illumination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of shade (الظل) as resulting from the interposition of a luminous body, and the argument that shade can occur without direct opposition, as seen at dawn.

Core Idea
Shade arises from interposition of a luminous body, even indirectly through the air.

After defining light and illumination, the commentator turns to shade (الظل). Shade is not merely the absence of light but a positive quality resulting from the interposition of an opaque body. He provides examples from the earth at dawn and after sunset, and clarifies that this shade comes from the illuminated air, not by direct reflection. This leads to a refutation of a common objection that a luminous source only illuminates what is directly opposite it. In this lesson, we will explore this nuanced definition and the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on shade and the objection
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the definition and the argument.
2
Analyze the definition of shade
Note that shade is defined as what results in a body from the interposition of a luminous by another.
3
Examine the examples
Focus on the earth at dawn and after sunset. How does the commentator explain the source of shade?
4
Understand the objection and refutation
Identify the objection that the sun should only illuminate what is opposite it. See how the refutation uses the idea of illuminated air as an intermediary.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument preserves the principle that illumination requires a line of sight, but via an intermediate medium.

?

The Glittering Thing: Intrinsic or Derived
Definition and classification of a glittering thing distinct from light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between شعاع and بريق and how they relate to light and illumination.

Core Idea
Glittering things on bodies can be either intrinsic or derived.

Sanusi introduces a fourth objective: a glittering phenomenon distinct from light. This passage defines that phenomenon and classifies it into two types, using an analogy to the well-known distinction between light and illumination. Understanding this helps clarify the physics of light as understood in classical kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi defines the glittering thing before classifying it.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two types
Find where the passage distinguishes شعاع from بريق. Notice the condition (by itself vs. from another) and the examples (sun vs. mirror).
The examples are key to remembering the distinction.
3
Understand the analogy
Sanusi compares the pair (شعاع/بريق) to (ضوء/نور). Reflect on why this analogy is used and what it reveals about intrinsic vs. derived properties.
The analogy reinforces the classification.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you can distinguish the two types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : أن ثمة شيئاً غير الضوء يترقرق ] ( أنَّ ثمة شيئاً غير الضوء يترقرق ) أي يتلألأ ويلمع ( على ) بعض ( الأجسام ) المستنيرة ( كأنه شيء يفيض منها ) أي من تلك الأجسام ( ويكاد يستر لونها وهو ) أعني ذلك الشيء المترقرق (له ) أي للجسم (إما لذاته ويسمى ) حينئذ ( شعاعاً ) كما للشمس من التلألؤ واللمعان الذاتي ( وإما عن غيره ويسمى ) حينئذ (بريقاً) كما للمرآة التي حاذت الشمس ) ونسبة البريق إلى الشعاع نسبة النور إلى الضوء ) في أنَّ الشعاع والضوء ذاتيان للجسم والبريق والنور مستفادان من غيره.
The Fourth Objective: That there is a glittering thing other than light. (That there is a glittering thing other than light), i.e., it glitters and shines (upon) some (bodies) that are illuminated (as if it is something emanating from them), i.e., from those bodies, (and it almost covers their color. It,) meaning that glittering thing, (is, for it) i.e., for the body, (either by itself and is then called) ray (شعاع), as is the sun's intrinsic glitter and shine, (or from another and is then called) glitter (بريق), as is the mirror that faces the sun. And the relation of glitter to ray is like the relation of illumination (نور) to light (ضوء) in that ray and light are intrinsic to the body, while glitter and illumination are derived from another.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies a phenomenon distinct from light: a glittering or shimmering thing that appears on some illuminated bodies. The author explains that this thing glitters and shines on bodies as if it flows from them and almost obscures their color. He then divides it into two types: (1) intrinsic (شعاع), like the sun's own radiance, and (2) derived (بريق), like the sparkle of a mirror facing the sun. Finally, he draws an analogy: the relationship of بريق to شعاع is like that of نور (illumination) to ضوء (light) — شعاع and ضوء are intrinsic to the body, while بريق and نور are received from another source.

شعاع
بريق
نور
ضوء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The glittering thing is the same as light.
Correction: The passage distinguishes it from light and classifies it into intrinsic (شعاع) and derived (بريق) types.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The glittering thing on bodies is either intrinsic (شعاع) or derived (بريق), analogous to the relation between light and illumination.

?
The analogy of نور (illumination) to ضوء (light) shows that effects can be traced to a source. In your life, what are the 'glittering' things you value? Are they rooted in an intrinsic light of faith, or are they momentary glimpses from worldly sources? How would your priorities change if you sought intrinsic نور rather than borrowed glitter?
