Sound's Self-Evident Essence and Refutation of False Definitions
Al-Jurjani defines sound and refutes false definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani argues that sound's essence is self-evident and how he refutes mistaken definitions that confuse the cause with the essence.

Core Idea
Sound's essence is self-evident; wave, strike, and tear are only causes, not the thing itself.

Al-Jurjani begins the discussion of audible things by tackling a common confusion: people often mistake the cause of sound for its essence. He presents three incorrect definitions and carefully shows why each fails. This passage teaches us not only about sound but also a key methodological point: do not confuse a thing's cause with its quiddity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation to get a full sense of al-Jurjani's argument.
Notice how he classifies audible things and then focuses on sound.
2
Identify the three definitions
Find where al-Jurjani lists the three proposed definitions: wave (tammawuj), strike (qar'), and tear (qal'). Distinguish between proximate and remote causes.
The wave is the proximate cause; strike and tear are remote causes.
3
Understand the refutation
For each definition, note al-Jurjani's argument: the wave is tangible and motion, sound is neither; strike is contact, tear is separation, sound is neither; all three are visible, sound is not.
These arguments show the definitions do not match sound's properties.
4
Grasp the positive claim
Al-Jurjani asserts that sound's essence is self-evident (badīhī) and needs no definition. It is independent of its causes.
This is a key epistemological point in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : المسموعات ] من المحسوسات المسموعات وهي الأصوات والحروف) التي هي كيفيات عارضة للأصوات . ( ومباحثه ) أي مباحث النوع الثالث ( قسمان ) : [ القسم الأول : في الصوت ] ( في الصوت ) قدمه على الحرف لكونه معروضاً له متقدماً عليه بالطبع ( وفيه مقاصد ) . المقصد الأول : الصوت هو التموج ] إنَّ الصوت وإن كان بديهي التصور كسائر المحسوسات إلا أنه (قد اشتبهت عند بعضهم ماهيته بسببه ( القريب أو البعيد . ( فقيل : ( الصوت ( هو التموج ) أي تموج الهواء وهو سببه القريب . ( وقيل : ( الصوت ( هو القرع أو القلع ) مع أن هذين سببان له بعيدان ) والحق ) كما أشرنا إليه ( أنَّ ماهيته بديهية ) مستغنية عن التعريف ومغايرة لما توهموه فإن التموج محسوس باللمس ألا أن یری الصوت الشديد ربما ضرب الصماخ بتموجه فأفسده وأنه قد يعرض من الرعد أن يدك الجبال، وكثيراً ما يستعان على هدم الحصون العالية بأصوات البوقات . والصوت ليس ملموساً في نفسه وأيضاً التموج حركة والصوت ليس كذلك والقرع مماسة والقلع تفريق، والصوت ليس شيئاً منهما، وأيضاً كل منهما مبصر بتوسط اللون ولا شيء من الأصوات بمبصر
The third type: audible things. Among perceived things are audible things, namely sounds and letters, which are qualities accidental to sounds. Its investigations (i.e., investigations of the third type) are two divisions: The first division: on sound. (On sound) he gave it precedence over the letter because it is a substrate for it, naturally prior to it. (And in it are purposes). The first purpose: sound is the wave. Indeed, sound, although its conception is self-evident like other perceived things, yet its essence has been confused for some of them by its proximate or remote cause. For it was said: sound is the wave, i.e., the waving of air, which is its proximate cause. And it was said: sound is the strike or the tear, although these are two remote causes for it. And the truth, as we pointed out, is that its essence is self-evident, independent of definition and different from what they imagined. For the wave is perceptible by touch. Do you not see that intense sound may strike the eardrum with its wave and corrupt it, and that it may occur from thunder that it demolishes mountains, and often the destruction of high fortresses is aided by the sounds of trumpets. But sound is not itself tangible. Also, the wave is motion and sound is not so; the strike is contact and the tear is separation, and sound is neither of them. Also, each of them is visible by means of color, and nothing of sounds is visible.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. Al-Jurjani begins by classifying the third type of perceived things as audible things, which include sounds and letters (letters being qualities accidental to sounds). He then divides the investigation into two parts, the first being on sound. Within this, the first purpose is that sound is commonly identified with the wave, but he clarifies that sound's essence is self-evident. The confusion arises because people mistake its cause (proximate or remote) for its essence. He lists three proposed definitions: sound as the wave of air (proximate cause), sound as the strike, and sound as the tear (both remote causes). The correct position is that sound's essence is intuitive and does not need definition. He then refutes the wave definition by arguing that waves are perceptible by touch and can even damage the ear, but sound itself is not tangible. Moreover, the wave is motion, sound is not; the strike is contact, the tear is separation, and sound is neither. Finally, each of the three is visible through color, whereas sound is not visible. Thus, none of these can be the essence of sound.

Key Terms
صوت
تموج
قرع
قلع
بديهي
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sound is identical to the wave of air (tammawuj).
Correction: The wave is a motion and is tangible; sound is neither motion nor tangible. The wave is a cause, not the essence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, why is it incorrect to say that sound is the wave (tammawuj) of air?
A) Because the wave is motion and sound is not motion.
B) Because the wave is audible and sound is not.
C) Because the wave is visible and sound is not.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
Sound's essence is self-evident and cannot be defined by its causes—wave, strike, or tear—as each of these differs fundamentally from sound itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jurjani shows that sound's essence is self-evident yet easily confused with its causes. In your own life, what is a self-evident truth about your relationship with Allah (e.g., 'I am His servant') that you sometimes obscure by focusing on secondary causes like rituals or circumstances? How will you re-center on that essence today?
?
The lesson argues that sound is not motion, contact, or separation—it is something else. Similarly, the essence of your faith is not found in outward actions alone. What is the 'self-evident' core of your belief? Write one concrete step you will take to live from that core rather than from the 'wave' of external deeds.

Definition of Sound as Vibration and Its Properties
Definition of sound as vibration and refutation of alternative views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of sound as vibration and how it differs from motion, tangibility, and visibility.

Core Idea
Sound is vibration, not motion, not tangible, and not visible.

This lesson introduces the classical definition of sound as vibration (التموج) and clarifies what sound is not. The commentator provides examples of sound's effects and argues that sound is neither tangible, nor motion, nor the contact of striking, nor the separation of tearing, nor visible. Understanding these distinctions is foundational for later discussions on the nature of speech (kalam).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the definition and the list of properties.
Note the heading 'النوع الثالث - القسم الأول : المقصد الأول'.
2
Identify the definition
Find the statement 'الصوت هو التموج'. What does 'التموج' mean? It means vibration or undulation.
This is the core definition.
3
Analyze the arguments
Break down the reasoning: sound is not tangible, not motion, not striking/tearing, and not visible. How does the commentator prove each?
These arguments refute alternative views.

النوع الثالث - القسم الأول : المقصد الأول : الصوت هو التموج أصلا، ضرب الصماخ بتموجه فأفسده وأنه قد يعرض من الرعد أن يدك الجبال، وكثيراً ما يستعان على هدم الحصون العالية بأصوات البوقات . والصوت ليس ملموساً في نفسه وأيضاً التموج حركة والصوت ليس كذلك والقرع مماسة والقلع تفريق، والصوت ليس شيئاً منهما، وأيضاً كل منهما مبصر بتوسط اللون ولا شيء من الأصوات بمبصر
The third type - the first section - the first objective: Sound is vibration originally. Striking the eardrum with its vibration corrupts it, and it may occur that thunder demolishes mountains, and often high fortresses are demolished by the sounds of trumpets. Sound is not tangible in itself, and also vibration is motion but sound is not, and striking is contact and tearing is separation, and sound is neither of them. Also, each of them is visible through color, but none of the sounds are visible.

Commentary
This passage defines sound (الصوت) as vibration (التموج). It begins by stating the heading 'النوع الثالث - القسم الأول : المقصد الأول' and then gives the core definition: 'الصوت هو التموج'. The commentator then provides examples: sound can harm the ear, thunder can destroy mountains, and trumpet sounds can demolish forts. Next, he argues that sound is not tangible (ملموس) by noting that vibration is motion but sound is not motion, nor is it the contact of striking or the separation of tearing. Additionally, both striking and tearing are visible through color, while sound is not visible. This refutes alternative views that consider sound to be a body or a tangible quality.

Defining the Letter: Quality, Sound, or Both?
Three definitions of 'letter' as sound quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions on the definition of the linguistic 'letter' (harf) as a quality of sound, the sound itself, or their combination.

Core Idea
The letter is not just a written shape; it is a sound with a distinguishing quality.

Al-Jurjani’s commentary presents a nuanced discussion on what exactly constitutes a 'letter' in the context of speech. The passage explores whether the letter is the inherent quality of sound, the sound that bears that quality, or a combination of both. By examining these three views, we gain insight into the precision of classical theological-linguistic analysis.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three definitions
Read the passage and identify the three distinct views on what the letter is: (1) the quality itself, (2) the sound itself, (3) the combination. Underline each position.
The text uses 'the Sheikh' and 'a group of verifiers' to label the first two views.
2
Understand the purpose of the gloss
Notice that the commentator begins by glossing 'quality' to include length and shortness of sound, which is necessary to exclude those from the definition of 'letter'.
This shows the precision required in definitions.
3
Consider the commentator's own assessment
The text says 'the closest to the truth is that it is the aggregate of the accident and the substance'. Reflect on why this might be the most comprehensive view.
The commentator promises to return to this later, so this is a preview.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أي هيئة وصفة) فسر الكيفية بها ليتناول طول الصوت وقصره حتى يصح الاحتراز عنهما بقوله: تميزاً في المسموع واعلم أنَّ كون الحرف عبارة عن تلك الكيفية العارضة للصوت . إنما هو عند الشيخ وعند جمع من المحققين الحرف هو الصوت المعروض للكيفية المذكورة والأشبه بالحق أنها مجموع العارض والمعروض كما صرح به البعض وسيشير إليه الشارح فيما سياتي .
His saying: (i.e., form and description) he interpreted 'quality' (al-kayfiyya) by it to include the length and shortness of sound so that the exclusion of them by his saying 'distinction in the heard' is valid. And know that the letter (al-harf) being that quality that accidentally occurs to the sound is only according to the Sheikh. And according to a group of verifiers, the letter is the sound that is the subject of the mentioned quality. And the closest to the truth is that it is the aggregate of the accident and the substance, as some have stated, and the commentator will refer to it later.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a footnote explaining the definition of 'letter' (حرف) in the context of sound. It begins by glossing 'quality' as 'form and description' to include pitch variation. Then it presents three views: (1) The Sheikh (al-Razi?) holds that the letter is the quality that inheres in the sound. (2) A group of verifiers holds that the letter is the sound itself that bears that quality. (3) The most likely truth, according to some, is that the letter is the combination of both the quality and the sound that bears it. The commentator promises to return to this later.

عارض

Correction: In this context, the letter denotes the speech sound and its distinguishing quality, not the written form.

B) It is the sound itself.
C) It is the aggregate of both the accidental quality and the sound.
D) It is the written character.

Key Takeaway
The letter in theological linguistics is not merely a written symbol but a sound that possesses a distinguishing quality, with scholars debating whether it is the quality, the sound, or their combination.

Ibn Sina's Definition of the Letter
Ibn Sina defines the letter and explains the intent of 'occurs to sound'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of the letter and the significance of the phrase 'occurs to sound' for including instantaneous letters.

Core Idea
A letter is a quality that makes a sound distinct.

Ibn Sina defines the letter as a quality that occurs to sound, enabling distinction from another similar sound. This lesson explains the definition's first component and its scope, including how it accounts for instantaneous letters.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp Ibn Sina's definition. Note the key terms: كيفية, تعرض, الصوت, تميز.
The definition is concise; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the three components
Find the three main parts: (1) it is a quality, (2) it occurs to sound, (3) by it the sound is distinguished from another similar sound.
3
Understand 'occurs to sound'
Notice that this phrase is intended to include letters at the extremes of sound, like instantaneous consonants. Reflect on the analogy of 'now' to time.
This prepares for the objection in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ القسم الثاني : في الحروف ]
[ المقصد الأول : تعريف ابن سينا للحروف ]
عرفه أي الحرف ابن سينا بأنه كيفية أي هيئة وصفة تعرض للصوت بها أي بتلك الكيفية يمتاز الصوت عن صوت آخر مثله في الحدة والثقل تميزاً في المسموع هذا تعريفه وأما الكشف عن مفهومه فهو أن نقول : قوله : تعرض للصوت أراد به ما يتناول عروضها له في طرفه عروض الآن للزمان ليتناول الحروف الآنية وهذا إشارة إلى ما ذكره الإمام الرازي: من أنَّ التعريف المذكور لا يتناول الحروف الصوامت كالتاء والطاء والدال فإنها لا توجد إلا في الآن الذي هو بداية زمان الصوت أو نهايته فلا تكون عارضة له حقيقة لأن العارض يجب أن يكون موجوداً مع المعروض، وهذه الحروف الآنية لا توجد مع الصوت الذي هو زماني. قال : ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنها عارضة للصوت عروض الآن للزمان والنقطة للخط يعني أن عروض الشيء للشيء قد يكون بحيث يجتمعان في الزمان وقد لا يكون وحينئذ يجوز أن يكون كل واحد من الحروف الآنية طرفاً للصوت عارضاً له عروض الآن للزمان فيندفع الإشكال وقوله : مثله في الحدة والثقل ليخرج من التعريف الحدة أي الزيرية والثقل أي اليمبة فإنهما وإن كانتا صفتين مسموعتين عارضتين للصوت فيمتاز بهما ذلك الصوت عما يخالفه في تلك الصفة العارضة، إلا أنه لا يمتاز بالحدة صوت عن صوت آخر يماثله في الحدة ولا بالثقل صوت عما يشاركه فيه وقوله : تميزا في المسموع ليخرج الغنة التي تظهر من تسريب الهواء بعضاً إلى جانب الأنف، وبعضاً إلى الفم مع انطباق الشفتين والبحوحة التي قوله : عن صوت آخر مثله في الحدة والثقل المراد من الصوت الآخر هو الذي لا يكون مكيفاً بالكيفية التي في الصوت الأول وإلا فالحرف الواحد يمكن أن يتلفظ به مرتين بحيث لا يختلف الحدة والثقل فيهما فلا يصح أن يراد الامتياز عن جميع الأصوات التي تشاركه في الحدة والثقل كما قيل .
[The second section: on letters] [The first intent: Ibn Sina's definition of letters] He defined it, i.e., the letter, Ibn Sina, as a quality (kayfiyya), i.e., a form and attribute, that occurs to sound (sawt), by which, i.e., by that quality, the sound is distinguished from another sound similar to it in pitch (hidda) and weight (thiqal), with a distinction in the auditory (masmu'). This is his definition. As for the uncovering of its concept, we say: His statement: 'that occurs to sound' – he intended by it to include its occurrence to it at its extreme, like the occurrence of 'now' to time, so as to include instantaneous letters (al-huruf al-aniyya). This refers to what Imam al-Razi mentioned: that the aforementioned definition does not include the silent letters like tā', ṭā', dāl, for they exist only in the instant that is the beginning or end of the time of the sound, so they are not truly accidents of it, because the accident must exist with the subject, and these instantaneous letters do not exist with the sound that is temporal. He said: It can be answered that they are accidents of sound like the occurrence of 'now' to time and the point to the line, meaning that the occurrence of something to something may be such that they coexist in time or may not, and then it is permissible that each of the instantaneous letters be an extreme of the sound, occurring to it like the occurrence of 'now' to time, so the objection is repelled. And His statement: 'similar to it in pitch and weight' – to exclude from the definition pitch (hidda), i.e., sharpness, and weight (thiqal), i.e., heaviness, for although they are two audible qualities that occur to sound, by which that sound is distinguished from what differs from it in that accidental quality, yet a sound is not distinguished by pitch from another sound that resembles it in pitch, nor by weight from a sound that shares it. And His statement: 'a distinction in the auditory' – to exclude nasalization (ghunna) which appears by sending some air to the side of the nose and some to the mouth with the closure of the lips, and hoarseness (bahuha) which... His statement: 'from another sound similar to it in pitch and weight' – the meaning of 'another sound' is that which is not qualified by the quality that is in the first sound; otherwise, a single letter could be uttered twice such that pitch and weight do not differ between them, so it would not be correct to intend distinction from all sounds that share it in pitch and weight, as has been said.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines the letter (al-harf) according to Ibn Sina and explains each part of the definition, including an objection and its answer. It begins by stating Ibn Sina's definition: a letter is a quality that occurs to sound, by which that sound is distinguished from another sound similar in pitch and weight, with a distinction in the auditory. Then it elaborates on the phrase 'occurs to sound' (taʿruḍ li-l-ṣawt): this is meant to include instantaneous letters (al-ḥurūf al-āniyya), which are consonants like tāʾ, ṭāʾ, dāl that exist only at the beginning or end of a sound—like the instant (al-ān) occurring at the boundary of time. The passage mentions an objection from al-Razi: these instantaneous letters are not truly accidents (aʿrāḍ) because they do not coexist with the sound (which is temporal); the accident must accompany the subject. The answer is that something can be an accident of another even if they do not coexist in time—like the instant to time or the point to a line—so the instantaneous letters can be ends of the sound. Next, 'similar to it in pitch and weight' excludes pitch and weight themselves, because although they are audible qualities that distinguish sounds, they do not distinguish a sound from another that shares the same pitch or weight. Then 'a distinction in the auditory' excludes nasalization (ghunna) and hoarseness (baḥūḥa), which are heard but are not letters. Finally, 'from another sound similar to it in pitch and weight' is explained: the other sound is one that is not qualified by the same letter-quality; otherwise a single letter could be uttered twice without differing in pitch and weight, so the distinction is not from all sounds of the same pitch/weight but only from those that are not letters.

Key Terms
الحرف
كيفية
الصوت
الحروف الآنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A sound
B) A quality that occurs to sound
C) A pitch
D) A duration

Key Takeaway
The letter is a quality that occurs to sound and distinguishes it from similar sounds.

The Objection of Instantaneous Letters and Its Answer
Al-Razi's objection and the answer based on the occurrence of 'now' to time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised by al-Razi about instantaneous letters not fitting the definition, and the answer that resolves it.

Core Idea
Even instantaneous letters can be considered accidents of sound.

Al-Razi objected that Ibn Sina's definition fails to include consonants that only exist at an instant. This lesson presents the objection and its resolution through an analogy with time and points.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage that contains al-Razi's objection. Note why instantaneous letters seem problematic: they don't coexist with the temporal sound.
2
Identify the core issue
The objection states that an accident must exist with its subject. Since these letters are only at boundaries, they might not be true accidents.
3
Study the answer
The answer uses the analogy of 'now' to time and point to line. Understand how this shows that occurrence at an extremity is valid.
This resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ القسم الثاني : في الحروف ]
[ المقصد الأول : تعريف ابن سينا للحروف ]
عرفه أي الحرف ابن سينا بأنه كيفية أي هيئة وصفة تعرض للصوت بها أي بتلك الكيفية يمتاز الصوت عن صوت آخر مثله في الحدة والثقل تميزاً في المسموع هذا تعريفه وأما الكشف عن مفهومه فهو أن نقول : قوله : تعرض للصوت أراد به ما يتناول عروضها له في طرفه عروض الآن للزمان ليتناول الحروف الآنية وهذا إشارة إلى ما ذكره الإمام الرازي: من أنَّ التعريف المذكور لا يتناول الحروف الصوامت كالتاء والطاء والدال فإنها لا توجد إلا في الآن الذي هو بداية زمان الصوت أو نهايته فلا تكون عارضة له حقيقة لأن العارض يجب أن يكون موجوداً مع المعروض، وهذه الحروف الآنية لا توجد مع الصوت الذي هو زماني. قال : ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنها عارضة للصوت عروض الآن للزمان والنقطة للخط يعني أن عروض الشيء للشيء قد يكون بحيث يجتمعان في الزمان وقد لا يكون وحينئذ يجوز أن يكون كل واحد من الحروف الآنية طرفاً للصوت عارضاً له عروض الآن للزمان فيندفع الإشكال وقوله : مثله في الحدة والثقل ليخرج من التعريف الحدة أي الزيرية والثقل أي اليمبة فإنهما وإن كانتا صفتين مسموعتين عارضتين للصوت فيمتاز بهما ذلك الصوت عما يخالفه في تلك الصفة العارضة، إلا أنه لا يمتاز بالحدة صوت عن صوت آخر يماثله في الحدة ولا بالثقل صوت عما يشاركه فيه وقوله : تميزا في المسموع ليخرج الغنة التي تظهر من تسريب الهواء بعضاً إلى جانب الأنف، وبعضاً إلى الفم مع انطباق الشفتين والبحوحة التي قوله : عن صوت آخر مثله في الحدة والثقل المراد من الصوت الآخر هو الذي لا يكون مكيفاً بالكيفية التي في الصوت الأول وإلا فالحرف الواحد يمكن أن يتلفظ به مرتين بحيث لا يختلف الحدة والثقل فيهما فلا يصح أن يراد الامتياز عن جميع الأصوات التي تشاركه في الحدة والثقل كما قيل .
[The second section: on letters] [The first intent: Ibn Sina's definition of letters] He defined it, i.e., the letter, Ibn Sina, as a quality (kayfiyya), i.e., a form and attribute, that occurs to sound (sawt), by which, i.e., by that quality, the sound is distinguished from another sound similar to it in pitch (hidda) and weight (thiqal), with a distinction in the auditory (masmu'). This is his definition. As for the uncovering of its concept, we say: His statement: 'that occurs to sound' – he intended by it to include its occurrence to it at its extreme, like the occurrence of 'now' to time, so as to include instantaneous letters (al-huruf al-aniyya). He said: It can be answered that they are accidents of sound like the occurrence of 'now' to time and the point to the line, meaning that the occurrence of something to something may be such that they coexist in time or may not, and then it is permissible that each of the instantaneous letters be an extreme of the sound, occurring to it like the occurrence of 'now' to time, so the objection is repelled. And His statement: 'similar to it in pitch and weight' – to exclude from the definition pitch (hidda), i.e., sharpness, and weight (thiqal), i.e., heaviness, for although they are two audible qualities that occur to sound, by which that sound is distinguished from what differs from it in that accidental quality, yet a sound is not distinguished by pitch from another sound that resembles it in pitch, nor by weight from a sound that shares it. And His statement: 'a distinction in the auditory' – to exclude nasalization (ghunna) which appears by sending some air to the side of the nose and some to the mouth with the closure of the lips, and hoarseness (bahuha) which... His statement: 'from another sound similar to it in pitch and weight' – the meaning of 'another sound' is that which is not qualified by the quality that is in the first sound; otherwise, a single letter could be uttered twice such that pitch and weight do not differ between them, so it would not be correct to intend distinction from all sounds that share it in pitch and weight, as has been said.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines the letter (al-harf) according to Ibn Sina and explains each part of the definition, including an objection and its answer. It begins by stating Ibn Sina's definition: a letter is a quality that occurs to sound, by which that sound is distinguished from another sound similar in pitch and weight, with a distinction in the auditory. Then it elaborates on the phrase 'occurs to sound' (taʿruḍ li-l-ṣawt): this is meant to include instantaneous letters (al-ḥurūf al-āniyya), which are consonants like tāʾ, ṭāʾ, dāl that exist only at the beginning or end of a sound—like the instant (al-ān) occurring at the boundary of time. The passage mentions an objection from al-Razi: these instantaneous letters are not truly accidents (aʿrāḍ) because they do not coexist with the sound (which is temporal); the accident must accompany the subject. The answer is that something can be an accident of another even if they do not coexist in time—like the instant to time or the point to a line—so the instantaneous letters can be ends of the sound. Next, 'similar to it in pitch and weight' excludes pitch and weight themselves, because although they are audible qualities that distinguish sounds, they do not distinguish a sound from another that shares the same pitch or weight. Then 'a distinction in the auditory' excludes nasalization (ghunna) and hoarseness (baḥūḥa), which are heard but are not letters. Finally, 'from another sound similar to it in pitch and weight' is explained: the other sound is one that is not qualified by the same letter-quality; otherwise a single letter could be uttered twice without differing in pitch and weight, so the distinction is not from all sounds of the same pitch/weight but only from those that are not letters.

Key Terms
الحروف الآنية
الصوامت
العارض
الآن
الزمان
النقطة
الخط

Checkpoint
What analogy is used to answer the objection about instantaneous letters?
A) Water and fire
B) The occurrence of 'now' to time and point to line
C) Light and sound
D) Pitch and weight

Key Takeaway
Instantaneous letters are valid as they occur to sound at its extremes, like an instant to time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is a continuous sound, but the moments that define your eternity—your last breath, a flash of faith, a sin—are instantaneous. Are you aware that these 'nows' are as real as any long habit? How will you ensure your final 'now' finds you in submission?
?
When you face an objection to your faith that seems logical, like al-Razi's, do you immediately reject it or seek the deeper answer? What current doubt in your heart are you refusing to examine because it doesn't 'fit'? Will you bring it to Allah's revelation today?
?
The scholars used analogies of the point and line to defend truth. You have been given intellect—are you using it to defend truth or to justify your own desires? What 'edge' of your life have you dismissed as insignificant, even though it defines you before Allah?

What the Definition Excludes
Ibn Sina explains what his definition excludes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pitch and weight are not letters, and why nasalization and hoarseness are excluded from the definition.

Core Idea
Letters aren't distinguished by pitch or weight.

Ibn Sina's definition excludes qualities like pitch, weight, nasalization, and hoarseness because they do not distinguish a sound from another that shares them. This lesson clarifies these exclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the exclusion clauses
Read the Arabic passage that explains the exclusions. Note the phrases 'مثله في الحدة والثقل' and 'تميزا في المسموع'.
2
Understand why pitch and weight are excluded
Pitch and weight are audible qualities, but they do not distinguish a sound from another that shares the same pitch or weight. Letters provide such distinction.
3
Understand why nasalization and hoarseness are excluded
These are audible but are not letters; they are excluded by the phrase 'distinction in the auditory' because they are not the kind of distinction that defines letters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ القسم الثاني : في الحروف ]
[ المقصد الأول : تعريف ابن سينا للحروف ]
عرفه أي الحرف ابن سينا بأنه كيفية أي هيئة وصفة تعرض للصوت بها أي بتلك الكيفية يمتاز الصوت عن صوت آخر مثله في الحدة والثقل تميزاً في المسموع هذا تعريفه وأما الكشف عن مفهومه فهو أن نقول : قوله : تعرض للصوت أراد به ما يتناول عروضها له في طرفه عروض الآن للزمان ليتناول الحروف الآنية وهذا إشارة إلى ما ذكره الإمام الرازي: من أنَّ التعريف المذكور لا يتناول الحروف الصوامت كالتاء والطاء والدال فإنها لا توجد إلا في الآن الذي هو بداية زمان الصوت أو نهايته فلا تكون عارضة له حقيقة لأن العارض يجب أن يكون موجوداً مع المعروض، وهذه الحروف الآنية لا توجد مع الصوت الذي هو زماني. قال : ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنها عارضة للصوت عروض الآن للزمان والنقطة للخط يعني أن عروض الشيء للشيء قد يكون بحيث يجتمعان في الزمان وقد لا يكون وحينئذ يجوز أن يكون كل واحد من الحروف الآنية طرفاً للصوت عارضاً له عروض الآن للزمان فيندفع الإشكال وقوله : مثله في الحدة والثقل ليخرج من التعريف الحدة أي الزيرية والثقل أي اليمبة فإنهما وإن كانتا صفتين مسموعتين عارضتين للصوت فيمتاز بهما ذلك الصوت عما يخالفه في تلك الصفة العارضة، إلا أنه لا يمتاز بالحدة صوت عن صوت آخر يماثله في الحدة ولا بالثقل صوت عما يشاركه فيه وقوله : تميزا في المسموع ليخرج الغنة التي تظهر من تسريب الهواء بعضاً إلى جانب الأنف، وبعضاً إلى الفم مع انطباق الشفتين والبحوحة التي قوله : عن صوت آخر مثله في الحدة والثقل المراد من الصوت الآخر هو الذي لا يكون مكيفاً بالكيفية التي في الصوت الأول وإلا فالحرف الواحد يمكن أن يتلفظ به مرتين بحيث لا يختلف الحدة والثقل فيهما فلا يصح أن يراد الامتياز عن جميع الأصوات التي تشاركه في الحدة والثقل كما قيل .
[The second section: on letters] [The first intent: Ibn Sina's definition of letters] He defined it, i.e., the letter, Ibn Sina, as a quality (kayfiyya), i.e., a form and attribute, that occurs to sound (sawt), by which, i.e., by that quality, the sound is distinguished from another sound similar to it in pitch (hidda) and weight (thiqal), with a distinction in the auditory (masmu'). Ibn Sina's Definition of the Letter
Ibn Sina defines the letter and addresses the objection about instantaneous consonants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's definition of the letter (ḥarf) and how it accommodates instantaneous letters like voiceless consonants.

Core Idea
Letters are qualities that occur to sound, even if only at the sound's instant.

Ibn Sina defines the letter as a quality that occurs to sound, distinguishing it from other sounds. But this raises a problem: how can instantaneous letters like 't' exist only at the sound's beginning? This lesson unpacks the definition and shows how the objection is resolved by analogy to the 'now' of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that Ibn Sina defines the letter as a quality (kayfiyya) that occurs to sound.
The definition is precise; note the phrase 'occurrence to sound'.
2
Understand the problem with instantaneous letters
Al-Razi objects that letters like tā' exist only at the beginning or end of a sound, not throughout, so they cannot truly 'occur' to sound. See how this challenges the definition.
These letters are called 'instantaneous letters' (ḥurūf āniyya).
3
See the answer using analogy
Ibn Sina's defense is that occurrence can be like the 'now' to time: they occur at the extremity. This resolves the objection and saves the definition.
This analogy also applies to the point and the line.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عرفه) أي الحرف ) ابن سينا بأنه كيفية ) أي هيئة وصفة ) تعرض للصوت بها ) أي بتلك الكيفية ) يمتاز ( الصوت (عن) صوت آخر (مثله في الحدة والثقل تميزاً في المسموع) هذا تعريفه ( و ) أما الكشف عن مفهومه فهو أن نقول : ( قوله : تعرض للصوت أراد به ما يتناول عروضها له في طرفه عروض الآن للزمان ليتناول الحروف الآنية) وهذا إشارة إلى ما ذكره الإمام الرازي: من أنَّ التعريف المذكور لا يتناول الحروف الصوامت كالتاء والطاء والدال فإنها لا توجد إلا في الآن الذي هو بداية زمان الصوت أو نهايته فلا تكون عارضة له ،حقيقة لأن العارض يجب أن يكون موجوداً مع المعروض، وهذه الحروف الآنية لا توجد مع الصوت الذي هو زماني. قال : ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنها عارضة للصوت عروض الآن للزمان والنقطة للخط يعني أن عروض الشيء للشيء قد يكون بحيث يجتمعان في الزمان وقد لا يكون وحينئذ يجوز أن يكون كل واحد من الحروف الآنية طرفاً للصوت عارضاً له عروض الآن للزمان فيندفع الإشكال
He (Ibn Sina) defined it (the letter) as a quality (that is, a state and attribute) that occurs to sound, by which (that quality) the sound is distinguished from another sound similar to it in pitch and weight, a distinction in the audible. This is its definition. As for disclosing its concept, it is that we say: His saying 'occurs to sound' intends what encompasses its occurrence to it at its extremity, like the occurrence of the 'now' to time, so as to include instantaneous letters. This refers to what Imam al-Razi mentioned: that the mentioned definition does not include the voiceless consonants like tā', ṭā', and dāl, for they exist only in the now that is the beginning or end of the time of the sound, so they are not truly occurring to it, because the thing that occurs must exist together with the thing it occurs to, and these instantaneous letters do not exist with the sound which is temporal. He said: It can be answered that they occur to the sound like the occurrence of the 'now' to time and the point to the line, meaning that the occurrence of a thing to another may be such that they coexist in time or may not, and in that case it is permissible that each of the instantaneous letters is an extremity of the sound occurring to it like the occurrence of the 'now' to time, and thus the problem is resolved.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's definition of the letter (ḥarf) as a quality that occurs to sound, by which the sound is distinguished from another sound similar in pitch and weight, a distinction in the audible. The author then explains the phrase 'occurs to sound'. He notes that this phrase is intended to include instantaneous letters (al-ḥurūf al-āniyya), such as the voiceless consonants tā', ṭā', and dāl, which exist only at the very beginning or end of a sound and not throughout its duration. This is in response to an objection by Imam al-Razi that such letters do not truly occur to sound because they are not simultaneous with the sound. The passage then provides an answer: just as the 'now' occurs to time and the point occurs to the line without coexisting throughout, so too instantaneous letters can be regarded as extremities of sound occurring to it at its endpoint. Thus the objection is resolved, and the definition covers all letters.

Key Terms
حرف (ḥarf)
كيفية (kayfiyya)
عروض (ʿurūḍ)
حروف آنية (ḥurūf āniyya)
صوامت (ṣawāmit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Letters must be continuous qualities of sound to be considered occurring to it.
Correction: Ibn Sina's definition allows instantaneous letters as extremities, like the 'now' to time.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's definition, how are instantaneous letters (like tā') understood to 'occur' to sound?
A) They occur as a continuous quality throughout the sound.
B) They occur at the extremity of the sound, like the 'now' to time.
C) They do not occur to sound at all.
D) They occur as a result of nasal resonance.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's definition of the letter as a quality that occurs to sound includes instantaneous letters by analogy to the 'now' of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the recitation of the Quran, do you perceive each letter as a distinct quality that distinguishes it from others, as Ibn Sina defined? Or do you hear a blur of sound? This lesson calls you to see precision in the very fabric of speech. Will you commit to listening with such attention in your next prayer?
?
The objection of al-Razi shows that even a great definition can be questioned. In your own understanding of your faith, are you open to such objections that refine your beliefs, or do you hold your definitions as final? The response to the objection did not discard the definition but expanded it. Are you willing to let your concepts be expanded by challenges?
?
The analogy of the point to the line and the now to time illustrates that something can exist at the boundary without being part of the continuum. Reflect on the boundaries in your life—the moments of transition, the beginnings and endings. Do you treat them as real and meaningful, or do you dismiss them as negligible? How might viewing them as 'occurrences' change your awareness of them?

Excluding Pitch and Weight from Ibn Sina's Definition
The author explains why pitch and weight are excluded from Ibn Sina's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Sina's definition of a single sound (ḥarf) includes the phrase 'similar in pitch and weight' and how it excludes pitch and weight as distinguishing features.

Core Idea
Pitch and weight cannot distinguish a letter from another with the same pitch or weight.

In defining a letter (ḥarf), Ibn Sina includes a crucial phrase: 'similar in pitch and weight.' This phrase is not just a detail; it serves to exclude pitch (highness) and weight (lowness) from being the defining qualities of a letter. Why? Because a sound is not distinguished by its pitch from another sound that has the exact same pitch. This passage from the commentary clarifies this logical point and ensures the definition remains precise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author is explaining a clause in Ibn Sina's definition.
2
Identify the key terms for pitch and weight
Locate the Arabic terms for pitch (ḥidda / zīriyya) and weight (thiqal / yamaba). Understand that these are audible qualities, but not distinctive between sounds that share them.
These terms are important in classical sound theory.
3
Understand why pitch and weight are excluded
The passage states that although pitch and weight are qualities of sound, a sound is not distinguished by pitch from another sound that is identical in pitch, nor by weight from a sound that shares that weight. This exclusion refines the definition.
4
Clarify the meaning of 'another sound'
The 'other sound' in the definition is one that does not have the same quality as the first sound. If it had the same quality, then the same letter uttered twice would be considered different, which is incorrect.
This prevents the definition from being overly broad.
5
Reflect on the purpose of precise definitions
Consider why defining letters requires such careful exclusion. The goal is to capture what truly distinguishes one letter from another, not just any quality that may vary.
This connects to the broader subject of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) قوله : ( مثله في الحدة والثقل ليخرج ) عن التعريف (الحدة) أي الزيرية ( والثقل) أي اليمبة فإنهما وإن كانتا صفتين مسموعتين عارضتين للصوت فيمتاز بهما ذلك الصوت عما يخالفه في تلك الصفة العارضة، إلا أنه لا يمتاز بالحدة صوت عن صوت آخر يماثله في الحدة ولا بالثقل صوت عما يشاركه فيه (و) قوله : ( عن صوت آخر مثله في الحدة والثقل) المراد من الصوت الآخر هو الذي لا يكون مكيفاً بالكيفية التي في الصوت الأول وإلا فالحرف الواحد يمكن أن يتلفظ به مرتين بحيث لا يختلف الحدة والثقل فيهما فلا يصح أن يراد الامتياز عن جميع الأصوات التي تشاركه في الحدة والثقل كما قيل.
And his saying: 'similar in pitch and weight' is to exclude from the definition pitch (i.e., highness) and weight (i.e., lowness). For even though they are both audible qualities occurring to sound, by which the sound is distinguished from what differs from it in that occurring quality, yet a sound is not distinguished by pitch from another sound that is identical to it in pitch, nor by weight from a sound that shares it. And his saying: 'from another sound similar in pitch and weight' means that the other sound is that which is not qualified by the quality that is in the first sound; otherwise, a single letter could be uttered twice without any difference in pitch and weight, so it would not be correct to intend distinction from all sounds that share it in pitch and weight, as has been said.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'similar in pitch and weight' in Ibn Sina's definition. The author states that this phrase is intended to exclude pitch (ḥidda, i.e., highness) and weight (thiqal, i.e., lowness) from being the distinguishing quality. Although pitch and weight are audible qualities occurring to sound, a sound is not distinguished by pitch from another sound that has the same pitch, nor by weight from a sound that shares the same weight. The passage further clarifies that the 'other sound' referred to in the definition is one that does not have the same quality as the first sound. If it had the same quality, then uttering the same letter twice would have identical pitch and weight, and the definition would incorrectly require distinction from all sounds with the same pitch and weight, which is not intended.

Key Terms
حدة (ḥidda)
ثقل (thiqal)
زيرية (zīriyya)
يمبة (yamaba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That pitch and weight are part of the definition or are the distinguishing features of letters.
Correction: The passage explains that pitch and weight are excluded because they do not distinguish sounds that share the same pitch or weight; they are not the essential basis for differentiating letters.

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Sina's definition include the phrase 'similar in pitch and weight'?
A) To include pitch and weight as distinguishing features.
B) To exclude pitch and weight because they are not distinguishing between sounds of the same pitch/weight.
C) To emphasize that all letters differ in pitch and weight.
D) To define pitch and weight as the essence of letters.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'similar in pitch and weight' in Ibn Sina's definition excludes pitch and weight as distinguishing features, because a letter is not distinguished by pitch from another with the same pitch.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson teaches that pitch and weight are not what truly distinguish a letter, because two utterances of the same letter can share them. In your own life, are there 'pitch and weight' qualities—superficial similarities or differences—that you mistakenly use to judge people, actions, or beliefs? Think of one example. How can you instead focus on the essential, defining features that truly matter?
?
If you were to define what makes you a true believer, what outward similarities might you be tempted to rely on—like appearances, rituals, or habits—that could be identical in someone who is not sincere? How does the principle of excluding non-distinctive qualities challenge you to examine the deeper, unique core of your faith?

intermediate

Excluding Non-Audible Qualities: Ghunna and Baḥḥa
The author explains the exclusion of ghunna, baḥḥa, length, shortness, and pleasantness from the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ghunna (nasalization) and baḥḥa (hoarseness) are excluded from the definition of kalam, along with length, shortness, and pleasantness, because they are not audible qualities.

Core Idea
The letter is defined by audible distinction; non-audible qualities are excluded.

Ibn Sina's definition of the letter includes the phrase 'a distinction in the audible.' This lesson examines what this phrase excludes: qualities that may affect sound but are not themselves heard. Understanding these exclusions clarifies the precise scope of the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the excluded qualities
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. List the qualities that the author says are excluded: ghunna, baḥḥa, length, shortness, pleasantness, unpleasantness.
Notice that the author begins by saying 'to exclude' (li-yukhrija).
2
Understand why ghunna and baḥḥa are not audible
The author explains that ghunna involves air diverted to the nose, and baḥḥa is thickness of sound from the throat. They are qualities occurring to sound, but not themselves perceived by hearing. Grasp the distinction between the quality and the sound it modifies.
The key point: the distinction must be 'in the audible' — the distinguishing feature must be heard.
3
Extend the reasoning to length, shortness, and pleasantness
Length and shortness are quantities, not directly audible; they involve comparing sounds over time. Pleasantness is felt internally (wijdān), not heard. See how the author classifies these as 'sensed' (maṭbūʿān) rather than audible.
The author uses the term 'pure quantities' (kammiyyāt maḥḍa) to categorize length and shortness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) قوله : ( تميزا في المسموع ليخرج الغنة التي تظهر من تسريب الهواء بعضاً إلى جانب الأنف، وبعضاً إلى الفم مع انطباق الشفتين ( والبحوحة ) التي هي غلظ الصوت الخارج من الحلق فإن الغنة والبحوحة سواء كانتا ملذتين أو غير ملذتين صفتان عارضتان للصوت يمتاز بهما عما يشاركه في الحدة والثقل، لكنهما ليستا مسموعتين، فلا يكون التميز الحاصل منهما تميزا في المسموع من حيث هو مسموع ( ونحوهما ) ، كطول الصوت وقصره وكونه طيباً وغير طيب فإنّ هذه الأمور لیست مسموعة أيضاً أما الطول والقصر فلأنهما من الكميات المحضة أو المأخوذة مع إضافة ولا شيء منهما بمسموع وإن كان يتضمن هاهنا المسموع، فإن الطول إنما يحصل من اعتبار مجموع صوتين صوت حاصل في ذلك الوقت، وهو مسموع وصوت حاصل قبل ذلك الوقت وليس بمسموع. وأما كون الصوت طيباً أي ملائماً للطبع أو غير طيب فأمر يدرك بالوجدان دون السمع، فهما مطبوعان لا مسموعان ( إذ قوله: (من حيث هو مسموع) إشارة إلى دفع اعتراض السيد السمرقندي الذي أشار إليه صاحب المقاصد أيضاً وهو أنه لا دلالة لقولنا : تميزاً في المسموع على أن يكون فئة التميز لو قيل : تميزاً بالمسموع لصح ما ذكر، ووجه الدفع أن تميز المسموع من حيث هو مسموع، إنما هو بأن يكون ما به الامتياز مسموعاً ، كما سيظهر من قوله : ( فلأنهما من الكميات المحضة أو المأخوذة مع إضافة قد نقل الشارح في مباحث الكم المتصل على المباحث المشرقية معنى كونهما من أحدهما، لكن إنما يستقيم إذا جعل معروضهما موجوداً في الخارج وأما إذا جعل الموجود من الصوت في الخارج أمراً بسيطاً غير منقسم على قياس ما قيل في الحركة والزمان فلا بل يكون الطول والقصر القائمان بالصوت الممتد على معناهما المصدري ولا يكونان من الكميات الموجودة في الخارج عند هذا المعرف وغيره من الفلاسفة. قوله : ( وإن كان يتضمن هاهنا المسموع فإنَّ الطول الخ الظاهر من سياق كلامه أن ضمير يتضمن إذا كان على صيغة المعلوم راجع إلى الطول ويحتمل أن يرجع إلى كل من الكميات المحضة والمأخوذة مع الإضافة وأراد بقوله : هاهنا حيث كان معروضهما الصوت. فإن الكلام فيه ويؤيده قول الأبهري. نعم كل منهما متضمن للصوت الذي هو مسموع . لكن المفهوم من قوله : فإِنَّ الطول إلخ. إن تضمنها المسموع إنما هو في صورة الطول وأنت خبير بأن التضمن هاهنا ليس على معناه المتعارف في المنطق إذ ليس المسموع هاهنا جزء مدلول الطول بل معروضه وأنَّ أيضاً يتضمن هاهنا المسموع، وإن كان المراد تضمن معروضه تحقق في القصر أيضاً، لكون الصوت زمانياً البتة فلا وجه وجيهاً للتخصيص. اللهم إلا أن بينى الكلام على تبادر ذلك من الطول دون القصر، وهذا التبادر مما لا ينكر. قوله : (فهما مطبوعان ذكر في حاشية التجريد أنَّ ملائمة الصوت وعدمها مدركان بالقوة
And his saying: 'a distinction in the audible' is to exclude the ghunna (nasalization) which appears from diverting some air to the side of the nose and some to the mouth with closure of the lips, and the baḥḥa (hoarseness) which is the thickness of sound emerging from the throat. For ghunna and baḥḥa, whether pleasant or not, are both qualities occurring to sound by which it is distinguished from what shares it in pitch and weight, but they are not audible, so the resulting distinction is not a distinction in the audible insofar as it is audible. And similar to these are length and shortness of sound and its being pleasant or unpleasant, for these things are also not audible. As for length and shortness, they belong to the pure quantities or those taken with addition, and neither of them is audible, although it here includes the audible, for length arises only from considering a sum of two sounds: a sound occurring at that time, which is audible, and a sound occurring before that time, which is not audible. As for the sound being pleasant (i.e., agreeable to nature) or unpleasant, it is perceived by sensation (wijdān) rather than hearing, so they are both sensed (maṭbūʿān), not audible. His saying: 'insofar as it is audible' refers to refuting the objection of al-Sayyid al-Samarqandī, which the author of al-Maqāṣid also noted, that our saying 'a distinction in the audible' does not indicate that the means of distinction is audible; if it were said 'a distinction by the audible' it would be correct. The answer is that the distinction of the audible insofar as it is audible is only that the means of distinction is audible, as will appear from his saying: 'because they are from the pure quantities...' [then a long commentary about length and shortness and quantity]. The apparent from the context is that the pronoun 'it' (yatadamman) if in the active voice refers to length, and it is possible it refers to each of the pure quantities and those taken with addition. He meant by 'here' where their subject is sound. The speech is about it, and this is supported by al-Abharī's statement. Yes, each of them includes the sound which is audible. But what is understood from his saying 'for length' etc. is that its inclusion of the audible is only in the case of length. And you are aware that inclusion here is not in its conventional logical meaning, for the audible here is not a part of the meaning of length but its subject. And also includes here the audible, even if the meaning is that its subject is realized in shortness as well, because sound is definitely temporal, so there is no good reason for specification. Unless the speech is based on the fact that length is more quickly understood than shortness, and this quick understanding is undeniable. His saying: 'they are both sensed'—it is mentioned in the gloss of al-Tajrīd that the pleasantness of sound and its opposite are perceived by the power...
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage explains the second qualifying phrase in Ibn Sina's definition: 'a distinction in the audible'. The author states that this phrase excludes qualities like ghunna (nasalization) and baḥḥa (hoarseness) because, although they are qualities occurring to sound that can distinguish it from other sounds, they are not themselves audible; they are perceived by other senses. Therefore, the resulting distinction is not a distinction in the audible qua audible. The passage then extends the exclusion to similar qualities such as length/shortness and pleasantness/unpleasantness. Length and shortness are argued to be quantities, not directly audible; length involves a comparison of two sounds, one of which is past and not audible. Pleasantness is perceived by internal sensation (wijdān), not hearing. The author then addresses an objection by al-Sayyid al-Samarqandī, who argued that the phrase 'in the audible' does not imply that the means of distinction is audible; one would need 'by the audible'. The answer is that 'distinction in the audible' means the audible qua audible, and that requires the distinguishing quality itself to be audible, otherwise the distinction would not be in the audible. The passage then delves into a technical discussion about whether length and shortness are quantities that include the audible in their definition. The author cites al-Abharī and discusses the nature of inclusion (taḍammun) in logic, concluding that the audible is not a part of the meaning of length but its subject. Finally, he notes a gloss from al-Tajrīd stating that pleasantness is perceived by a sensory power (quwwa).

Key Terms
غنة (ghunna)
بحوحة (baḥḥa)
تميز في المسموع (tamyīz fī al-masmūʿ)
كميات محضة (kammiyyāt maḥḍa)
وجدان (wijdān)
مطبوعان (maṭbūʿān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghunna and baḥḥa are audible qualities.
Correction: They are qualities of sound but not perceived by hearing; they are sensed by other means.

Checkpoint
Why are ghunna and baḥḥa excluded from Ibn Sina's definition of the letter?
A) Because they are not qualities of sound.
B) Because they are not audible qualities.
C) Because they are always unpleasant.
D) Because they involve nasal and throat resonances only.

Key Takeaway
The letter is defined by audible distinction; qualities that are not heard, such as ghunna, baḥḥa, length, shortness, and pleasantness, do not qualify.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you listen to the Qur'an recited with a beautiful voice, do you find yourself focusing on the melody and emotional effect (pleasantness) more than on the distinct letters being pronounced? How does this lesson challenge you to reorient your hearing toward what is truly audible and essential?
?
The lesson separates length (quantity) from the letter itself. When you prolong a vowel in recitation, are you aware that the length is not part of the letter but a quantity added by comparison? Would your understanding of the Qur'an's preservation change if you realized that the letters themselves are defined only by their audible distinctions, not by their temporal extension?
?
Have you ever judged a recitation's quality based on its pleasantness or emotional impact, forgetting that the core of the letter is its distinct sound? What would it mean for your spiritual practice to listen to the Qur'an with the intention of recognizing only the letters as they are heard, stripping away all non-audible associations?

What Is Kalam?

8 min
Addressing the Objection: The Means of Distinction Must Be Audible
The author refutes al-Samarqandī's objection regarding the phrasing 'distinction in the audible'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Samarqandī's objection to Ibn Sina's definition and how the author defends it by clarifying that the distinction must be by an audible quality.

Core Idea
Distinction in the audible requires the distinguishing feature itself to be audible.

The previous lesson excluded non-audible qualities. But a subtle objection arises: does the phrase 'in the audible' require the distinguishing quality itself to be audible? This lesson unpacks the objection and its resolution, delving into the nature of quantity and inclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation. al-Samarqandī objects that 'distinction in the audible' does not imply the distinguishing feature is audible; one would need 'distinction by the audible'.
The author mentions that al-Maqāṣid also noted this objection.
2
Follow the author's answer and discussion of quantities
The author argues that 'insofar as it is audible' means the distinction is based on an audible property. He then delves into length and shortness as quantities, discussing whether they include the audible in their meaning or not. Pay attention to the concept of 'inclusion' (taḍammun) and the distinction between subject and part.
The author cites al-Abharī to support his point.

Commentary

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Length and shortness are directly perceived by hearing.
Correction: They are quantities that involve the audible but are not themselves heard; they require comparison over time.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the phrase 'distinction in the audible' imply that the distinguishing quality must be audible?
A) Because the audible is the only sense we trust.
B) Because the distinction is of the audible insofar as it is audible.
C) Because the author prefers 'by the audible'.
D) Because al-Samarqandī's objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
For a distinction to be 'in the audible,' the distinguishing quality itself must be audible; otherwise, the definition fails.

What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
Ibn Sina's Definition of Letters and Non-Audible Attributes
Critique of Ibn Sina's definition using non-audible attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Sina's definition of letters as roughness of sound is problematic because certain attributes like nasality and hoarseness are not audible, and that length, shortness, and pleasantness of sound are also not directly perceived by hearing.

Core Idea
Not all qualities of sound are audible; some are quantities or inner feelings.

In this passage, the author critiques Ibn Sina's definition of letters (ḥurūf) as roughness of sound from the throat. He argues that attributes like ghunna (nasality) and baḥḥa (hoarseness) are not audible, so they cannot serve to distinguish sound in its audible aspect. He then extends this to length, shortness, and pleasantness, explaining why they too fall outside the realm of hearing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the technical terms like غنة and بحوحة.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that ghunna and baḥḥa are not audible. Find the supporting reason.
3
Examine the examples
Look at length/shortness and pleasantness as further examples of non-audible attributes. Understand why each is considered not directly audible.
Length/shortness are quantities; pleasantness is perceived by inner feeling.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does it matter that these attributes are not audible? The author uses this to challenge Ibn Sina's definition.
If the distinguishing feature is not audible, it cannot define letters as sounds.

فلأنهما من الكميات المحضة أو المأخوذة مع إضافة ولا شيء منهما بمسموع وإن كان يتضمن هاهنا المسموع، فإن الطول إنما يحصل من اعتبار مجموع صوتين صوت حاصل في ذلك الوقت، وهو مسموع وصوت حاصل قبل ذلك الوقت وليس بمسموع. وأما كون الصوت طيباً أي ملائماً للطبع أو غير طيب فأمر يدرك بالوجدان دون السمع، فهما مطبوعان لا مسموعان ( إذ قوله: (من حيث هو مسموع) إشارة إلى دفع اعتراض السيد السمرقندي الذي أشار إليه صاحب المقاصد أيضاً وهو أنه لا دلالة لقولنا : تميزاً في المسموع على أن يكون فئة التميز لو قيل : تميزاً بالمسموع لصح ما ذكر، ووجه الدفع أن تميز المسموع من حيث هو مسموع، إنما هو بأن يكون ما به الامتياز مسموعاً ، كما سيظهر من قوله : ( فلأنهما من الكميات المحضة أو المأخوذة مع إضافة قد نقل الشارح في مباحث الكم المتصل على المباحث المشرقية معنى كونهما من أحدهما، لكن إنما يستقيم إذا جعل معروضهما موجوداً في الخارج وأما إذا جعل الموجود من الصوت في الخارج أمراً بسيطاً غير منقسم على قياس ما قيل في الحركة والزمان فلا بل يكون الطول والقصر القائمان بالصوت الممتد على معناهما المصدري ولا يكونان من الكميات الموجودة في الخارج عند هذا المعرف وغيره من الفلاسفة. قوله : ( وإن كان يتضمن هاهنا المسموع فإنَّ الطول الخ الظاهر من سياق كلامه أن ضمير يتضمن إذا كان على صيغة المعلوم راجع إلى الطول ويحتمل أن يرجع إلى كل من الكميات المحضة والمأخوذة مع الإضافة وأراد بقوله : هاهنا حيث كان معروضهما الصوت. فإن الكلام فيه ويؤيده قول الأبهري. نعم كل منهما متضمن للصوت الذي هو مسموع . لكن المفهوم من قوله : فإِنَّ الطول إلخ. إن تضمنها المسموع إنما هو في صورة الطول وأنت خبير بأن التضمن هاهنا ليس على معناه المتعارف في المنطق إذ ليس المسموع هاهنا جزء مدلول الطول بل معروضه وأنَّ أيضاً يتضمن هاهنا المسموع، وإن كان المراد تضمن معروضه تحقق في القصر أيضاً، لكون الصوت زمانياً البتة فلا وجه وجيهاً للتخصيص. اللهم إلا أن بينى الكلام على تبادر ذلك من الطول دون القصر، وهذا التبادر مما لا ينكر. قوله : (فهما مطبوعان ذكر في حاشية التجريد أنَّ ملائمة الصوت وعدمها مدركان بالقوة الواهمة لأنهما من المعاني الجزئية المتعلقة بالمحسوسات وكأن الشارح إنما عدهما هاهنا مطبوعين نظراً إلى أن إدراك الواهمة لهما بمدخل من الطبع . قوله : ( وهذا أنسب بمباحث العربية قال رحمه الله : لأن أصحاب العلوم العربية يقولون : ) الكلمة مركبة من الحرف، ويقولون للكلم : أنه صوت كذا فلو لم يكن الحرف عندهم مجموع العارض والمعروض بل عارض الصوت فقط لما صح منهم ذلك، والحاصل أن إطلاق الصوت على الكلمة المركبة من الحرف على تقدير كون الحرف نفس الهيئة العارضة للصوت مجاز تسمية للعارض باسم المعروض وعلى تقدير كون الحرف عبارة عن المجموع تسمية للكل باسم الجزء ومن البين أن الثاني أنسب . ومما ذكرنا يظهر أن كون الحرف عبارة عن نفس المعروض أنسب بذلك القول من المذهبين الأخيرين، إذ لا مجاز في ذلك الإطلاق على هذا التقدير أصلاً.
The third type—the second section: first objective: Ibn Sina's definition of letters: the roughness of the sound exiting from the throat. For ghunna (nasality) and baḥḥa (hoarseness), whether they are pleasant or unpleasant, are two attributes accidental to sound by which it is distinguished from what shares with it in sharpness and heaviness, but they are not audible. Thus the distinction resulting from them is not a distinction in the audible insofar as it is audible (and similar [attributes]), such as length and shortness of sound and its being pleasant or unpleasant, for these matters are also not audible. As for length and shortness, because they are either pure quantities or taken with relation, and nothing of them is audible, although they entail here the audible. For length only results from considering the sum of two sounds: a sound occurring at that time (which is audible) and a sound occurring before that time (which is not audible). As for sound being pleasant, i.e., agreeable to nature, or unpleasant, it is a matter perceived by innermost feeling (wijdān) not by hearing; thus they are both instinctive, not audible. (Since) His statement: 'insofar as it is audible' refers to repelling the objection of al-Sayyid al-Samarqandi, which the author of al-Maqāṣid also alluded to: that our saying 'a distinction in the audible' does not indicate that the source of distinction is the thing itself. If it were said 'a distinction by the audible', what was mentioned would be correct. The way of repelling is that distinguishing the audible insofar as it is audible is only by that through which the distinction occurs being audible, as will appear from his statement: 'Because they are pure quantities or taken with relation.' The commentator has transmitted in the investigations of continuous quantity from the Eastern investigations the meaning of their being from one of them, but it is correct only if their subject is assumed to exist externally. But if what exists of sound externally is assumed to be a simple indivisible thing, analogous to what is said of motion and time, then length and shortness subsisting in extended sound are in their verbal meaning, and they are not among the quantities existing externally according to this definer and other philosophers. His statement: 'Although it entails here the audible, for length...' The apparent from the context of his speech is that the pronoun 'it' if active refers to length, and it is possible that it refers to both pure quantities and those taken with relation. He meant by 'here' where their subject is sound. For the discussion concerns it, and it is supported by the statement of al-Abharī: 'Indeed each of them entails the sound which is audible.' But what is understood from his statement: 'for length, etc.' is that its entailment of the audible is only in the case of length. And you are aware that entailment here is not in the customary sense in logic, for the audible here is not part of the meaning of length but its subject. And also entails here the audible, even if the intended meaning is the entailment of its subject, it is also realized in shortness, because sound is necessarily temporal, so there is no proper ground for specification. Unless the speech is built on the immediate understanding of that from length rather than shortness, and this immediate understanding is undeniable. His statement: 'Thus they are both instinctive.' It is mentioned in the commentary on al-Tajrīd that the agreeability of sound and its disagreeability are perceived by the estimative faculty (al-quwwa al-wāhima) because they are among the partial meanings related to sensibles. It seems that the commentator counted them here as instinctive because the estimation (wahm) of them has an element of instinct. His statement: 'And this is more appropriate to the investigations of Arabic linguistics.' He, may God have mercy on him, said: 'Because the masters of the Arabic sciences say: the word (kalima) is composed of letters (ḥarf), and they say of the word: it is such-and-such a sound. So if the letter according to them were not the composite of the accident and the subject, but only the accident of the sound, their statement would not be valid. The result is that applying the name 'sound' to the word composed of the letter, on the assumption that the letter is the very form accidental to the sound, is a metaphor—naming the accident after the name of the subject. On the assumption that the letter is a term for the composite, it is naming the whole after the part. And it is clear that the second is more appropriate. From what we have mentioned, it appears that the letter being a term for the subject itself is more appropriate to that statement than the last two positions, for there is no metaphor in that application on this assumption at all.'
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of Ibn Sina's definition of letters (ḥurūf) as the roughness of sound exiting the throat. The author argues that certain attributes like ghunna (nasality) and baḥḥa (hoarseness) are not audible, so they cannot serve to distinguish sound in its audible aspect. The discussion extends to other non-audible attributes such as length and shortness (tūl and qaṣr) and pleasantness (ṭayyib) versus unpleasantness (ghayr ṭayyib). Length and shortness are considered pure quantities or quantities with relation, which are not audible, though they involve the audible in a composite sense. Pleasantness is perceived by inner feeling (wijdān), not hearing, and is instinctive (maṭbū‘ān). The commentary then addresses an objection by al-Samarqandi regarding the phrase 'distinction in the audible', clarifying that the distinction must be by an audible quality itself. The discussion on length includes a nuance about whether length and shortness are external quantities or merely conceptual, referencing al-Abharī. The passage also explains that the entailment of the audible in length and shortness is not the logical sense of entailment but rather the subject-accident relationship. Finally, the author argues that defining the letter as the composite of accident and subject (the ‘arḍ and ma‘rūḍ) is more appropriate for Arabic linguistic usage, as it avoids metaphor when referring to the word (kalima) as composed of letters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that length and shortness of sound are directly audible qualities like pitch.
Correction: The author argues they are quantities (pure or relational) and thus not audible in themselves; they are perceived by comparing sounds over time.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are ghunna and baḥḥa considered not audible?
A) Because they are not qualities of sound at all.
B) Because they are perceived by inner feeling, not hearing.
C) Because they are accidental attributes that do not affect the sound's audibility.
D) Because they are quantities and not sounds.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's definition of letters as roughness of sound is flawed because the distinguishing attributes (nasality, hoarseness, length, pleasantness) are not directly audible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

How can this lesson humble your confidence in your own definitions? Will you examine any of your assumptions today?
?

How does this affect your understanding of how you perceive reality? What commitment can you make to look beyond surface qualities?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
5 min
Objection to Ibn Sina and the Linguistic Appropriateness
Repelling an objection and concluding with linguistic appropriateness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised by al-Samarqandi regarding the phrase 'distinction in the audible' and how the author repels it, as well as the argument that defining the letter as the composite of accident and subject is more appropriate for Arabic linguistics.

Core Idea
A distinction in the audible must be by an audible quality; otherwise, the definition fails.

This passage continues the critique by addressing an objection from al-Samarqandi concerning the phrase 'distinction in the audible'. The author clarifies that the distinction must be by an audible quality itself. He then discusses the nature of length and shortness, referencing al-Abharī, and finally argues that defining the letter as the composite of the accidental attribute and the subject (sound) is more consistent with how Arabic linguists use the term 'letter' in composing words.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Al-Samarqandi objected that saying 'distinction in the audible' does not imply the source of distinction is audible. The author explains that it does, because the distinction must be by an audible quality.
The author uses 'from his statement' to reference earlier text.
2
Analyze the entailment discussion
The author discusses how length and shortness entail the audible, but not in the logical sense of part/whole. He quotes al-Abharī and clarifies that the entailment is about the subject-accident relationship.
This is a nuanced point about quantities and their relation to sound.
3
Examine the linguistic argument
The author concludes that defining the letter as the composite of the accidental attribute and the subject (sound) is more appropriate for Arabic linguistics, as it avoids metaphor when saying 'the word is composed of letters'.
The second position (composite) is preferred over the first (accident only) and third (subject only).

النوع الثالث - القسم الثاني : المقصد الأول : تعريف ابن سينا للحروف غلظ الصوت الخارج من الحلق فإن الغنة والبحوحة سواء كانتا ملذتين أو غير ملذتين صفتان عارضتان للصوت يمتاز بهما عما يشاركه في الحدة والثقل، لكنهما ليستا مسموعتين، فلا يكون التميز الحاصل منهما تميزا في المسموع من حيث هو مسموع ( ونحوهما ) ، كطول الصوت وقصره وكونه طيباً وغير طيب فإنّ هذه الأمور لیست مسموعة أيضاً أما الطول والقصر فلأنهما من الكميات المحضة أو المأخوذة مع إضافة ولا شيء منهما بمسموع وإن كان يتضمن هاهنا المسموع، فإن الطول إنما يحصل من اعتبار مجموع صوتين صوت حاصل في ذلك الوقت، وهو مسموع وصوت حاصل قبل ذلك الوقت وليس المقاصد أيضاً وهو أنه لا دلالة لقولنا : تميزاً في المسموع على أن يكون فئة التميز لو قيل : تميزاً بالمسموع لصح ما ذكر، ووجه الدفع أن تميز المسموع من حيث هو مسموع، إنما هو بأن يكون ما به الامتياز مسموعاً ، كما سيظهر من قوله : ( فلأنهما من الكميات المحضة أو المأخوذة مع إضافة قد نقل الشارح في مباحث الكم المتصل على المباحث المشرقية معنى كونهما من أحدهما، لكن إنما يستقيم إذا جعل معروضهما موجوداً في الخارج وأما إذا جعل الموجود من الصوت في الخارج أمراً بسيطاً غير منقسم على قياس ما قيل في الحركة والزمان فلا بل يكون الطول والقصر القائمان بالصوت الممتد على معناهما المصدري ولا يكونان من الكميات الموجودة في الخارج عند هذا المعرف وغيره من الفلاسفة. قوله : ( وإن كان يتضمن هاهنا المسموع فإنَّ الطول الخ الظاهر من سياق كلامه أن ضمير يتضمن إذا كان على صيغة المعلوم راجع إلى الطول ويحتمل أن يرجع إلى إلخ. إن تضمنها المسموع إنما هو في صورة الطول وأنت خبير بأن التضمن هاهنا ليس على معناه المتعارف في المنطق إذ ليس المسموع هاهنا جزء مدلول الطول بل معروضه وأنَّ أيضاً يتضمن هاهنا المسموع، وإن كان المراد تضمن معروضه تحقق في القصر أيضاً، لكون الصوت زمانياً البتة فلا وجه وجيهاً للتخصيص. اللهم إلا أن بينى الكلام على تبادر ذلك من الطول دون القصر، وهذا التبادر مما لا ينكر. قوله : (فهما مطبوعان ذكر في حاشية التجريد أنَّ ملائمة الصوت وعدمها مدركان بالقوة الواهمة لأنهما من المعاني الجزئية المتعلقة بالمحسوسات وكأن الشارح إنما عدهما هاهنا مطبوعين نظراً إلى أن إدراك الواهمة لهما بمدخل من الطبع . قوله : ( وهذا أنسب بمباحث العربية قال رحمه الله : لأن أصحاب العلوم العربية يقولون : ) الكلمة مركبة من الحرف، ويقولون للكلم : أنه صوت كذا فلو لم يكن الحرف عندهم مجموع العارض والمعروض بل عارض الصوت فقط لما صح منهم ذلك، والحاصل أن إطلاق الصوت على الكلمة المركبة من الحرف على تقدير كون الحرف نفس الهيئة العارضة للصوت مجاز تسمية للعارض باسم المعروض وعلى تقدير كون الحرف عبارة عن المجموع تسمية للكل باسم الجزء ومن البين أن الثاني أنسب . ومما ذكرنا يظهر أن كون الحرف عبارة عن نفس المعروض أنسب بذلك القول من المذهبين الأخيرين، إذ لا مجاز في ذلك الإطلاق على هذا التقدير أصلاً.
The third type—the second section: first objective: Ibn Sina's definition of letters: the roughness of the sound exiting from the throat. For ghunna (nasality) and baḥḥa (hoarseness), whether they are pleasant or unpleasant, are two attributes accidental to sound by which it is distinguished from what shares with it in sharpness and heaviness, but they are not audible. Thus the distinction resulting from them is not a distinction in the audible insofar as it is audible (and similar [attributes]), such as length and shortness of sound and its being pleasant or unpleasant, for these matters are also not audible. As for length and shortness, because they are either pure quantities or taken with relation, and nothing of them is audible, although they entail here the audible. For length only results from considering the sum of two sounds: a sound occurring at that time (which is audible) and a sound occurring before that time (which is not audible). As for sound being pleasant, i.e., agreeable to nature, or unpleasant, it is a matter perceived by innermost feeling (wijdān) not by hearing; thus they are both instinctive, not audible. (Since) His statement: 'insofar as it is audible' refers to repelling the objection of al-Sayyid al-Samarqandi, which the author of al-Maqāṣid also alluded to: that our saying 'a distinction in the audible' does not indicate that the source of distinction is the thing itself. If it were said 'a distinction by the audible', what was mentioned would be correct. The way of repelling is that distinguishing the audible insofar as it is audible is only by that through which the distinction occurs being audible, as will appear from his statement: 'Because they are pure quantities or taken with relation.' The commentator has transmitted in the investigations of continuous quantity from the Eastern investigations the meaning of their being from one of them, but it is correct only if their subject is assumed to exist externally. But if what exists of sound externally is assumed to be a simple indivisible thing, analogous to what is said of motion and time, then length and shortness subsisting in extended sound are in their verbal meaning, and they are not among the quantities existing externally according to this definer and other philosophers. His statement: 'Although it entails here the audible, for length...' The apparent from the context of his speech is that the pronoun 'it' if active refers to length, and it is possible that it refers to both pure quantities and those taken with relation. He meant by 'here' where their subject is sound. For the discussion concerns it, and it is supported by the statement of al-Abharī: 'Indeed each of them entails the sound which is audible.' But what is understood from his statement: 'for length, etc.' is that its entailment of the audible is only in the case of length. And you are aware that entailment here is not in the customary sense in logic, for the audible here is not part of the meaning of length but its subject. And also entails here the audible, even if the intended meaning is the entailment of its subject, it is also realized in shortness, because sound is necessarily temporal, so there is no proper ground for specification. Unless the speech is built on the immediate understanding of that from length rather than shortness, and this immediate understanding is undeniable. His statement: 'Thus they are both instinctive.' It is mentioned in the commentary on al-Tajrīd that the agreeability of sound and its disagreeability are perceived by the estimative faculty (al-quwwa al-wāhima) because they are among the partial meanings related to sensibles. It seems that the commentator counted them here as instinctive because the estimation (wahm) of them has an element of instinct. His statement: 'And this is more appropriate to the investigations of Arabic linguistics.' He, may God have mercy on him, said: 'Because the masters of the Arabic sciences say: the word (kalima) is composed of letters (ḥarf), and they say of the word: it is such-and-such a sound. So if the letter according to them were not the composite of the accident and the subject, but only the accident of the sound, their statement would not be valid. The result is that applying the name 'sound' to the word composed of the letter, on the assumption that the letter is the very form accidental to the sound, is a metaphor—naming the accident after the name of the subject. On the assumption that the letter is a term for the composite, it is naming the whole after the part. And it is clear that the second is more appropriate. From what we have mentioned, it appears that the letter being a term for the subject itself is more appropriate to that statement than the last two positions, for there is no metaphor in that application on this assumption at all.'
