Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of Ibn Sina's definition of letters (ḥurūf) as the roughness of sound exiting the throat. The author argues that certain attributes like ghunna (nasality) and baḥḥa (hoarseness) are not audible, so they cannot serve to distinguish sound in its audible aspect. The discussion extends to other non-audible attributes such as length and shortness (tūl and qaṣr) and pleasantness (ṭayyib) versus unpleasantness (ghayr ṭayyib). Length and shortness are considered pure quantities or quantities with relation, which are not audible, though they involve the audible in a composite sense. Pleasantness is perceived by inner feeling (wijdān), not hearing, and is instinctive (maṭbū‘ān). The commentary then addresses an objection by al-Samarqandi regarding the phrase 'distinction in the audible', clarifying that the distinction must be by an audible quality itself. The discussion on length includes a nuance about whether length and shortness are external quantities or merely conceptual, referencing al-Abharī. The passage also explains that the entailment of the audible in length and shortness is not the logical sense of entailment but rather the subject-accident relationship. Finally, the author argues that defining the letter as the composite of accident and subject (the ‘arḍ and ma‘rūḍ) is more appropriate for Arabic linguistic usage, as it avoids metaphor when referring to the word (kalima) as composed of letters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that length and shortness are directly audible because they affect the sound's duration.
Correction: The passage clarifies that length and shortness are quantities that involve a comparison over time and are not directly perceived by hearing alone; they are conceptual.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which definition of the letter (ḥarf) is most appropriate for Arabic linguistic usage?
A) The letter is the accidental attribute (like roughness) alone.
B) The letter is the composite of the accidental attribute and the subject (sound).
C) The letter is the subject (sound) itself.
D) The letter is the word (kalima) itself.

Key Takeaway
The objection to Ibn Sina is resolved by requiring the distinguishing feature to be audible, and the linguistic analysis favors defining the letter as the subject (sound) or at least the composite of accident and subject.

?
The author insists that distinguishing the audible requires an audible quality. In your journey to know your Lord, are you seeking signs that are of the same nature as the divine (e.g., spiritual insights) or are you content with material proofs? What does your answer reveal about the depth of your faith?
?
If the letter were merely an accident, then saying 'word is composed of letters' would be metaphorical. How metaphorical is your own understanding of the Quran? Do you treat it as literally the speech of Allah, or do you reduce it to metaphors in your heart?

The Nature of Letters: Audible and Non-Audible Accidents
Explanation of the two types of accidents and the definition of the letter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between audible and non-audible accidents of sound and how the term 'letter' can refer to either the accidental form or the composite of sound and accident.

Core Idea
Letters are defined by audible accidents, not by non-audible ones.

Before classifying letters into vowels and consonants, the author clarifies what a 'letter' is. He distinguishes between audible and non-audible attributes like nasality or hoarseness, which do not change the letter's identity. This sets the foundation for understanding the division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the author's argument.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'audible accidents' and 'non-audible accidents'.
2
Distinguish between the two kinds of accidents
Identify the non-audible accidents (ghunna, baḥḥa, etc.) and understand why they do not affect the audible composite.
The author explicitly states they are 'not in themselves audible'.
3
Understand the two meanings of 'letter'
Note that 'ḥarf' can refer to the accidental form (hay'a ārida) or the composite of subject and accident.
This is important for the later divisions.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters for Arabic linguistic investigations.
The author indicates that the composite meaning is 'more appropriate' (anbas) for Arabic studies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : أقسام الحروف قد تختلف هذه الأمور أعني الغنة والبحوحة ونحوهما. ( والمسموع واحد وقد تتحد والمسموع مختلف ) وذلك لأن هذه الأمور وإن كانت عارضة للصوت المسموع إلا أنها في أنفسها ليست مسموعة فلا يكون اختلافها مقتضياً لاختلاف المسموع ولا اتحادها مقتضياً لاتحاده بخلاف العوارض المسموعة فإن اختلافها يقتضي اختلاف المسموع الذي هو مجموع الصوت وعارضه واتحادها يقتضي اتحاد المسموع لا مطلقاً بل باعتبار ذلك العارض المسموع فتأمل. واعلم أن الحكم بأنَّ الغنة والبحوحة والجهارة والخفاية ليست مسموعة منظور فيه وأنَّ الحرف قد يطلق على الهيئة المذكورة العارضة للصوت، وعلى مجموع المعروض والعارض وهذا أنسب بمباحث العربية. قال المصنف : ( وبالجملة فماهية الحرف أوضح من ذلك الذي ذكر في تعريفها لما أن الإحساس بالجزئيات أقوى في إفادة المعرفة بماهيات المحسوسات من تعريفاتها بالأقوال الشارحة إذ لا يمكن لنا أن نعرفها إلا بإضافات واعتبارات لازمة لها، لا يفيد شيء منها معرفة حقائقها وكأن المقصود مما ذكر في تعريفاتها التنبيه على خواصها وصفاتها. المقصد الثاني : أقسام الحروف ] ( . ( : ( الحروف تنقسم من وجوه الأول : ( أنَّ الحروف (إما مصوتة وهي التي تسمى في العربية حروف المد واللين وهي الألف والواو والياء إذا كانت ساكنة متولدة من ما يقابلها قوله : (إما مصوتة (الخ إنما سميت مصوتة لاقتضائها امتداد الصوت، وسمي صامتاً لعدم اقتضائه ذلك . قوله : (إذا كانت ساكنة (متولدة إن اعتبر هذا الشرط : بالنسبة إلى الثلاثة فالمراد بالألف
The second objective: divisions of letters. These matters—meaning ghunna (nasality), baḥḥa (hoarseness), and the like—may differ while the audible is one, and may be one while the audible differs. This is because these matters, although they are accidental to the audible sound, are in themselves not audible, so their difference does not necessitate difference in the audible, nor their unity necessitate its unity, unlike the audible accidents; for their difference necessitates difference in the audible which is the composite of sound and its accident, and their unity necessitates unity of the audible—not absolutely, but with respect to that audible accident; so reflect. And know that the judgment that ghunna, baḥḥa, loudness (jahāra) and softness (khafā’) are not audible is open to scrutiny. And that the letter (ḥarf) may be applied to the mentioned form accidental to sound, and to the composite of subject and accident, and this is more appropriate to Arabic linguistic investigations. The author said: 'In summary, the quiddity (māhiyya) of the letter is clearer than what was mentioned in its definition, because sensation of particulars is stronger in leading to knowledge of the quiddities of sensibles than their definitions by explicative statements, for we cannot know them except by additions and necessary considerations, none of which benefit knowledge of their realities. It is as if the purpose of what was mentioned in their definitions is to alert to their properties and attributes.' The second objective: divisions of letters. Letters are divided from aspects: the first: that letters are either vowels (muṣawwita), which are called in Arabic 'letters of prolongation and softness' (ḥurūf al-madd wa al-līn), namely alif, wāw, and yā’ when they are quiescent (sākina) generated from ... what corresponds to them. His statement: 'either vowels'—they are called vowels because they entail extension of sound, and the consonant is called such because it does not entail that. His statement: 'when they are quiescent, generated'—if this condition is considered with respect to the three, then what is meant by the alif ...
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage introduces the second objective (al-maqṣad al-thānī) of the third type, second section: the divisions of letters (aqsām al-ḥurūf). The author first reiterates that attributes like ghunna and baḥḥa are not audible, so their variation or unity does not affect the audible sound itself, unlike audible accidents which do affect the composite. He then notes that the judgment that these attributes are not audible is open to scrutiny. The passage discusses the term 'ḥarf' (letter), which may refer to the accidental form (al-hay’a al-‘āriḍa) or to the composite of subject and accident, with the latter being more appropriate for Arabic linguistics. The author (Iji) then states that the quiddity (māhiyya) of the letter is clearer than definitions via explicative statements, because sensation of particulars is stronger for understanding sensibles. The second section formally begins with the first division: letters are either vowels (muṣawwita), which in Arabic are the letters of prolongation (alif, wāw, yā’ when quiescent), or consonants (ṣāmita). Vowels are so called because they entail extension of sound; consonants do not. The condition 'when they are quiescent' is considered for the three vowel letters, and the explanation begins for the alif but is cut off.

البحوحة
الجهارة
الخفاية
الهيئة العارضة
المعروض
العارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mistaking non-audible accidents as part of the letter's identity
Correction: They are not part of the audible composite and do not define the letter; only audible accidents affect the letter's identity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do non-audible accidents like ghunna not necessitate a difference in the audible composite?
A) Because they are not part of the sound at all.
B) Because they are not in themselves audible.
C) Because they are always the same.
D) Because they are accidental to the sound.

Key Takeaway
The letter is defined by its audible accidental properties, not by non-audible ones like nasality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you consciously distinguish between the nasalization (ghunna) that you produce and the actual audible qualities that define each letter? Will you now strive to ensure that your pronunciation relies on the correct audible accidents, not just habitual non-audible ones?
?
This lesson teaches that some attributes of your voice are not part of the letter itself. Are there aspects of your recitation that you have considered 'part of the letter' but are actually non-audible accidents? How will you adjust your practice to focus on what truly defines each letter?

The Quiddity of Letters: Sensation vs. Definition
Argument that sensation of letters surpasses definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that sensation of particular instances gives better knowledge of the letter's quiddity than formal definitions.

Core Idea
You know a letter better by hearing it than by reading its definition.

The author Iji makes a philosophical point about knowledge: for sensible objects like letters, direct sensation is more informative than explanatory definitions. This lesson presents that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's claim
Read the passage and identify the main claim: the quiddity of the letter is clearer through sensation than through definitions.
The author uses the phrase 'أوضح من ذلك الذي ذكر في تعريفها'.
2
Compare with earlier definitions
Recall that earlier definitions attempted to explain letters, but the author says they only alert to properties.
This is a meta-critique of the definitions given previously.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that definitions rely on 'additions and necessary considerations' (iḍāfāt wa iʿtibārāt lāzima) which do not reveal the true reality.
Sensation of particulars is 'stronger in leading to knowledge' (aqwā fī ifādat al-maʿrifa).
4
Reflect on the purpose of definitions
The purpose of definitions is not to give full knowledge but to 'alert to properties and attributes' (al-tanbīh ʿalā khawāṣṣihā wa ṣifātihā).
This is a pragmatic view of definitions.

The First Division: Vowels vs. Consonants
Enumeration of the first division of letters into vowels and consonants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of letters into vowels (letters of madd) and consonants, and the reason for their names.

Core Idea
Vowels extend sound; consonants do not.

Having established the nature of letters, the author now presents the first division: letters are either vowels, which are the letters of prolongation (alif, wāw, yā’ when quiescent), or consonants. This lesson explains that classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three vowel letters
The passage names alif, wāw, and yā’ as the vowel letters under a specific condition.
They are called 'ḥurūf al-madd wa al-līn' in Arabic.
2
Understand the condition: quiescent (sākina)
These letters are vowels only when they have no vowel mark (sukūn) themselves, and they are generated from the corresponding short vowel.
The text mentions 'إذا كانت ساكنة متولدة من ما يقابلها'.
3
Learn the reason for naming
They are called 'muṣawwita' (vowels) because they entail extension of sound (imtidād al-ṣawt).
Consonants (ṣāmita) are named because they do not entail extension.
4
Practice extraction
From a sample word like 'qāla', identify the vowel letter (alif) and note its condition.

The Three Tastes: Sweetness, Oiliness, and Insipidity
Sanusi defines sweetness, oiliness, and insipidity based on the receptive body's density.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the density or fineness of a receptive body determines the taste produced when acted upon by a moderate agent.

Core Idea
Taste arises from the interaction between the receptive body and the agent, with density shaping the outcome.

This lesson explores the physical basis of three fundamental tastes according to classical natural philosophy. By examining the role of the receptive body's consistency—dense, fine, or moderate—we see how sweetness, oiliness, and insipidity emerge from the interaction with a moderate active principle. The passage from Sanusi's commentary lays out this causal framework clearly.

Guided Path
1
Examine sweetness (dense receptive)
Read the definition of sweetness: it arises from intense resistance between a dense receptive and a moderate agent, producing a perfect suitable quality.
Notice that sweetness is considered the most agreeable taste.
2
Examine oiliness (fine receptive)
Read the definition of oiliness: it arises from weak resistance, allowing the agent to penetrate and produce a weak suitable quality.
Oiliness is sensed because the fine bearer penetrates the taste organ.
3
Examine insipidity (moderate receptive)
Read the definition of insipidity: it is an intermediate quality that is weaker than sweetness and stronger than oiliness, but not sensed due to its bearer's moderate density.
This is the key point—insipidity is imperceptible not because it doesn't exist, but because the body doesn't affect the taste organ.
4
Contrast the three cases
Compare the three receptives: dense produces strong taste (sweetness), fine produces weak taste (oiliness) that is sensed, moderate produces intermediate taste (insipidity) that is not sensed.
5
Reflect on the principle
Understand that the density of the receptive body directly influences the intensity and perceptibility of the taste.
This principle explains why some substances appear tasteless even though they have a latent taste.

(و) يقال أيضاً : ( لكون الجسم بحيث لا يحس بطعمه لكثافة أجزائه فلا يتحلل منه ) أي ذلك الجسم (ما يخالط الرطوبة ) اللعابية ( العذبة ) أي الخالية في نفسها عن الطعوم كلها ( التي هي آلة للإدراك بالقوة الذائقة كالصفر) ونحوه من الحديد وغيره . The fourth type - First objective: Tastes and their principles. The dense receptive (body) (for sweetness) due to the intensity of resistance between the dense receptive and the moderate agent, so the parts of the agent come together and produce a perfect, very suitable effect that is between extreme rarefaction and densification, so there arises a quality that is in utmost suitability, namely sweetness, which is the most suitable of tastes for moderate temperaments and the most delicious and appetizing to the tasting powers. And it (sweetness) is (in the fine receptive, oiliness due to little resistance) between the fine receptive and the moderate agent, so the parts of the agent penetrate into it and produce a weak suitable act (so one senses) from it a weak suitable quality, which is oiliness. And it (oiliness) is (in the moderate receptive, insipidity), because the moderate power must have its effect in the moderate receptive less than its effect in the dense and more than its effect in the fine, so there must arise a suitable quality weaker than sweetness and stronger than oiliness—yet this quality does not affect the taste due to its weakness, and the body bearing it does not penetrate into it because of its intermediateness between fineness and density, so this quality is not sensed due to lack of effect (i.e., the effect of the moderate receptive on the tasting power) neither by its matter nor by its quality (i.e., its taste), so no sensation occurs by it (i.e., that taste). Unlike oiliness, for although it is weak, its bearer is fine and penetrates into the taste and affects it by its matter, even if it does not affect it by its quality, so oiliness is sensed but not insipidity. From here it appears that insipidity is a taste above oiliness and below sweetness, except that it is not sensed in a distinct manner. (And it is said: insipidity is absence of taste) as in simple bodies. (And this is called real insipidity) and what is described with this insipidity is called insipid and tasteless. (And) it is also said: (because the body is such that its taste is not sensed due to the density of its parts, so nothing of it dissolves into the sweet moisture (saliva) that is free in itself from all tastes, which is the instrument of perception for the tasting power, like brass and the like of iron and others. (Then if one contrives to dissolve it, a strong sharp taste is sensed from it) as one makes brass into verdigris, i.e., makes it into small parts. (And this is called unreal insipidity and sensory insipidity.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage defines three tastes: sweetness, oiliness, and insipidity, based on the interaction between a receptive body (qābil) and an active agent (fāʿil). It begins with sweetness: when the receptive body is dense and the agent is moderate, the resistance is intense, causing the agent's parts to unite and produce a perfect, suitable effect — sweetness, which is the most agreeable taste. For a fine receptive body, resistance is low, so the agent penetrates and produces a weak suitable quality — oiliness. For a moderate receptive body, the effect is intermediate, yielding a quality weaker than sweetness but stronger than oiliness; however, this quality is not perceived because the body itself is moderate (neither dense nor fine) and does not affect the tasting organ. The passage then distinguishes two kinds of insipidity: real insipidity, which is the absence of taste (as in simple bodies), and unreal insipidity, where the taste is not sensed due to density but can be extracted (like brass turned into verdigris). The explanation follows the order of the original text, clarifying each step of the physical process.

Key Terms
القابل
الفاعل
الحلاوة
الدسومة
التفاهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is oiliness sensed even though its quality is weak?
A) Because oiliness is actually strong
B) Because its fine bearer penetrates the taste organ
C) Because oiliness is intermediate between sweetness and insipidity
D) Because the agent is moderate

Key Takeaway
Sweetness arises from intense resistance, oiliness from weak resistance, and insipidity from intermediate resistance, with the latter being imperceptible due to its moderate bearer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed a spiritual truth was absent simply because you did not feel its impact? Could your own 'density' of heart be blocking the sweetness of faith? What will you do today to soften your resistance?
?
The world's oiliness is easy to sense but lacks real substance. Are you chasing what is superficial and thin, while neglecting the deep sweetness that requires resistance? What concrete step can you take to pursue what is truly nourishing?
?
The moderate body has a taste but it is not perceived. Are you so comfortably balanced that you no longer feel the urgency of your spiritual state? This very indifference might be your greatest danger. How will you break your complacency and seek the penetrating truth?

Real vs. Apparent Insipidity
Sanusi distinguishes real insipidity (no taste) from apparent insipidity (hidden taste due to density).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of insipidity: real absence of taste and apparent absence due to density.

Core Idea
Insipidity can be either the complete lack of taste or a taste too weak to be sensed without extraction.

The previous lesson introduced insipidity as an imperceptible taste. Here, Sanusi clarifies that insipidity has two distinct meanings: one where the body genuinely lacks taste, and another where the taste is hidden due to density but can be released. This distinction is important for understanding how taste works in different substances.

Guided Path
1
Recognize insipidity as an intermediate taste
Recall from the previous lesson that insipidity is a taste above oiliness and below sweetness, but not sensed due to its bearer's moderate density.
This sets the stage for the distinction.
2
Learn the first definition: real insipidity
Read the definition of real insipidity: absence of taste, as in simple bodies (elements).
This is the straightforward case of no taste at all.
3
Learn the second definition: apparent insipidity
Read the definition of apparent insipidity: the body has a taste but it is not sensed due to density; only by dissolving or breaking it down (e.g., brass into verdigris) does the taste become apparent.
This explains why some seemingly tasteless substances actually have strong flavors.
4
Understand the example of brass and verdigris
The passage uses the example of brass (ṣufr) which when converted to verdigris (zanjār) releases a sharp taste, illustrating apparent insipidity.
The density of brass prevents its taste from being sensed until it is finely divided.
5
Summarize the two types
Contrast real insipidity (no taste) with apparent insipidity (taste present but imperceptible due to density).
This distinction is crucial for understanding how physical properties affect perception.

Three Meanings of Insipidity and Which Are Refuted
Commentator distinguishes three meanings of tafahah and shows effect of chicory objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct meanings of insipidity (tafahah) in kalam and which of them are refuted by the chicory example.

Core Idea
The chicory example refutes two meanings of insipidity but not the first.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies an objection regarding the taste properties of chicory. He identifies three possible meanings of 'insipidity' as a taste category. The objection shows that insipidity in the sense of absence of any taste cannot coexist with bitterness, but insipidity as a non-real taste can. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the commentator's argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three meanings
From the commentary, list the three distinct meanings of tafahah: (1) absence of any taste, (2) a taste not perceived due to deficiency, (3) a taste not perceived due to excess.
These are not explicitly enumerated in the passage but are inferred from the discussion.
3
Analyze the refutation
The commentator argues that the combination of bitterness and insipidity in chicory refutes the third meaning and apparently the second, but not the first. Understand why each is or is not refuted.
The first meaning (absence of all tastes) cannot coexist with bitterness, but the commentator says it is not refuted because it is not the common meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (من اجتماع المرارة قال رحمه الله تعالى : يعني أن الهندباء قد وجد فيه التفاهة من غير احتيال، فلو كان المراد بالتفاهة المعدودة فيها هو التفاهة الغير الحقيقية التي تكون في مثل الصفر لما أطلقوها على التفاهة التي في الهندباء. وهاهنا بحث وهو أنَّ المفهوم من كلام الشارح أن ما ذكروه من اجتماع الحرارة والتفاهة في الهندباء يبطل القول : بأن المعدود من الطعوم هو التفاهة بالمعنى الثالث، ولا يبطل القول : بأن التفاهة بالمعنى الثاني . والظاهر أنه يبطله أيضاً. بل القول بأنه المعنى الأول أيضاً، إذ لا يجامع وجود طعم مخصوص انتفاء الطعوم بأسرها، ولا إحساس طعم مخصوص انتفاء إحساس طعم ما فالتفاهة المعدودة من الطعوم على أي معنى حمل من الأخيرين. يبطله هذا الذي ذكروه من الاجتماع. نعم لو حمل على المعنى الأول لم يرد ذلك لكنه معنى ينفرد المصنف بإيراده وذكره وليس بمذكور في كتب القوم كما نقل من الشارح أيضاً فتأمل. ويمكن أن يقال : على تقدير أن لا يكون وجه الإشكال الذي نقل الشارح ثابتاً عن عنه رحمه الله تعالى : أن ضمير عليه في قوله : ورد عليه راجع إلى كل واحد من المتوهم والمذكور.
His statement: (From the combination of bitterness...) He said, may God have mercy on him: meaning that chicory has insipidity in it without artifice. If what was meant by the insipidity counted among them was the non-real insipidity that occurs in something like [؟], they would not have applied it to the insipidity in chicory. And here is a discussion: that what is understood from the commentator's speech is that what they mentioned of the combination of heat and insipidity in chicory refutes the statement that the counted among the tastes is insipidity in the third sense, and it does not refute the statement that it is insipidity in the second sense. And apparently it also refutes it. Rather, it also refutes the statement that it is the first sense, because the existence of a specific taste does not coexist with the negation of all tastes, nor does the perception of a specific taste coexist with the negation of any taste perception. So insipidity counted among the tastes, in whichever of the latter two senses it is taken, is refuted by this combination they mentioned. Yes, if taken in the first sense, that does not refute it, but that is a sense that the author alone introduced and mentioned, and it is not mentioned in the books of the people, as also transmitted from the commentator. So reflect. And it can be said: assuming that the aspect of the objection that the commentator transmitted is not established from him, may God have mercy on him, that the pronoun in his statement 'it was objected' refers to each one of the one who imagines and the one mentioned.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's analysis of the objection. He explains that the objection about chicory shows that the non-real insipidity is present, but the real insipidity (absence of taste) is not. He distinguishes three meanings: first, absence of any taste; second, a taste that is not perceived due to deficiency; third, a taste that is not perceived due to excess. The commentator argues that the objection refutes the third sense and apparently the second, but not the first. However, the first sense is not commonly used. He then discusses a subtle point about the pronoun.

صفر (ṣifr - possibly a plant)
معنى أول/ثاني/ثالث (first/second/third meaning)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the chicory objection refutes all meanings of insipidity.
Correction: The commentator shows it only refutes the second and third meanings, not the first.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, the objection from chicory refutes which meanings of insipidity?
A) Only the first
B) Only the second
C) The second and third
D) All three

Key Takeaway
The chicory objection shows that insipidity in the sense of absence of all tastes is not refuted, but the other two senses are.

?

?

Psychic Qualities: Definition and Scope
Defines psychic qualities and presents two opinions on their scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic philosophers defined psychic qualities and the debate over whether they apply only to animal souls or also to vegetative souls and even to God.

Core Idea
Psychic qualities are specific to souls, but their scope is debated.

This passage opens the second chapter on psychic qualities. The author first locates these qualities within the broader classification of qualities, then presents two competing views on which souls possess them. As you read, notice how the author handles the potential objection that divine attributes would then be psychic qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's flow come through before you analyze.
Note the structure: definition, first opinion, objection, second opinion.
2
Identify the two opinions
The passage presents two interpretations of 'souls' in the definition. Find each and note how the author argues for and against them.
The first opinion restricts to animal souls; the second includes vegetative souls.
3
Understand the objection and resolution
Notice the objection against the first opinion: divine attributes. How does the author resolve it?
The resolution is that divine attributes are not considered qualities (kayf) or accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني : في الكيفيات النفسانية من الفصول الأربعة التي هي في أقسام الكيفيات في الكيفيات النفسانية أي المختصة بذوات الأنفس من الأجسام العنصرية فقيل : المراد الأنفس الحيوانية ومعنى الاختصاص بها أن تلك الكيفيات توجد في الحيوان دون النبات والجماد . وعلى هذا فلا يتجه أن بعض هذه الكيفيات كالحياة والعلم والقدرة والإرادة ثابتة للواجب والمجردات فلا تكون مختصة بالحيوانات على أن القائل بثبوتها للواجب وغيره من المجردات لم يجعلها مندرجة في جنس الكيف، ولا في الأعراض. وقيل المراد : ما يتناول النفوس الحيوانية والنباتية أيضاً. فإنّ الصحة وما يقابلها من هذه الكيفيات يوجدان في النبات بحسب قوة التغذية والتنمية كما سيرد ذلك عليك في مباحثهما.
The second chapter: On psychic qualities from the four chapters that are in the divisions of qualities. On psychic qualities, i.e., those specific to souls of elemental bodies. It is said: what is meant is animal souls, and the meaning of their being specific is that these qualities exist in animals, not in plants and minerals. On this basis, it is not directed that some of these qualities, such as life, knowledge, power, and will, are established for the Necessary Being and separate intellects, so they would not be specific to animals, given that the one who affirms them for the Necessary Being and other separate intellects does not include them in the genus of quality, nor in accidents. And it is said: what is meant is that which includes animal and also vegetative souls. For health and its opposite among these qualities exist in plants in terms of the power of nutrition and growth, as will come to you in their discussions.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage defines the subject of the second chapter: psychic qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya). It begins by stating that these qualities are specific to souls of elemental bodies. Then it presents two opinions on what is meant by 'souls'. The first opinion restricts them to animal souls, explaining that psychic qualities are found only in animals, not in plants or minerals. The passage then notes a problem with this view: some psychic qualities like life, knowledge, power, and will are also attributed to God and separate intellects, which would contradict their being exclusive to animals. However, the one who affirms these attributes for God does not consider them as falling under the category of quality (kayf) or accidents, so the difficulty is resolved. The second opinion expands the scope to include vegetative souls as well, giving the example of health and its opposite being found in plants due to nutrition and growth. The passage thus clarifies the range of beings that possess psychic qualities.

Misconception: One might think that divine attributes like life and knowledge are included in the category of psychic qualities, making the definition too broad.
Correction: The text explicitly states that those who affirm such attributes for God do not place them under the genus of quality or accidents, so they are not counterexamples.

Checkpoint

A) Because divine attributes are not considered qualities (kayf) or accidents.
B) Because God has no psychic qualities.
C) Because the definition excludes all souls except animal.
D) Because the author rejects divine attributes altogether.

Key Takeaway
Psychic qualities are primarily associated with animal souls, but a broader interpretation includes vegetative souls, while divine attributes are excluded from this category.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The text distinguishes between created psychic qualities (like your knowledge and will) and divine attributes. When you reflect on your own knowing and willing, do you sense their limitation and dependency? How does this drive you to rely on Allah's perfect knowledge and will?
?
Consider the broadening of psychic qualities to include vegetative souls—your own body's growth and nutrition are signs of a soul's operation. How does recognizing the soul's presence in even these mundane functions deepen your gratitude to Allah for breathing His spirit into you?
?
The passage notes that divine attributes are not included in the category of quality or accident. Does this distinction challenge any assumptions you had about Allah's attributes being 'like' yours? How can this truth reshape your worship and humility before Him?

What Is Kalam?

Malaka and Hal: Rooted vs. Fleeting Psychic Qualities
Defines malaka and hal and states their accidental difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how psychic qualities are classified as malaka (disposition) or hal (state) based on their rootedness in the subject, and why the difference between them is accidental.

Core Idea
A psychic quality that sticks is a disposition; one that slips is a state.

In this passage, Sanusi defines two key terms for classifying psychic qualities: malaka and hal. The distinction rests not on the quality itself but on how firmly it is established in the subject—whether it is deeply rooted or easily changeable. Understanding this distinction is essential for analyzing human psychology in Islamic theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'if it is firmly established... it is called malaka, otherwise hal.'
The passage also includes a prefatory remark about abstract entities—this sets the context.
2
Identify the two definitions
Locate the definitions of malaka (firmly rooted, difficult to remove) and hal (not firmly rooted, easily changeable). Note that both are types of psychic qualities (kayfiyya nafsāniyya).
3
Examine the example of writing
The passage uses writing as an example: initially a hal, then through practice becomes a malaka. Reflect on how this illustrates the accidental nature of the difference.
The example of a child becoming a man reinforces that change occurs in the same subject.
4
Understand the accidental difference
The passage explicitly states the difference between malaka and hal is by an accidental cause (al-‘āriḍ), not by a specific difference (fasl). That means they are not different species but the same quality with different degrees of rootedness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجردات لم يجعلها مندرجة في جنس الكيف، ولا في الأعراض. وقيل المراد : ما يتناول النفوس الحيوانية والنباتية أيضاً. فإنّ الصحة وما يقابلها من هذه الكيفيات يوجدان في النبات بحسب قوة التغذية والتنمية كما سيرد ذلك عليك في مباحثهما . ( فإن كانت ( الكيفية النفسانية ( راسخة) في موضوعها أي مستحكمة فيه بحيث لا تزول عنه أصلاً أو يعسر زوالها ( سميت ملكة (وإلا أي وإن لم تكن راسخة فيه سميت (حالاً لقبولها التغير والزوال بسهولة والاختلاف بينهما بعارض) مفارق لا بفصل ( فإن الحال بعينها تصير ملكة بالتدريج ( ألا ترى أن الكيفية النفسانية الواحدة بالشخص كالكتابة مثلاً تكون في ابتداء حصولها حالاً، وإذا ثبتت زماناً واستحكم صارت هي بعينها ملكة كما أن الشخص الواحد قد كان صبياً ثم يصير رجلاً .
The abstract entities were not made by him included in the genus of quality, nor in the accidents. And it was said: the intended meaning is what encompasses animal and plant souls as well. For health and its opposite among these qualities exist in plants according to the power of nutrition and growth, as will come to you in their discussions. (So if) the psychic quality (is firmly established) in its subject, i.e., deeply rooted in it such that it does not depart from it at all or its departure is difficult, (it is called malaka (disposition), and if not, i.e., if it is not firmly established in it, it is called hal (state) because it accepts change and departure easily, and the difference between them is by an accidental (cause) that is separate, not by a specific difference. (For the same hal becomes malaka gradually. Do you not see that the same psychic quality in person, like writing for example, is at the beginning of its acquisition a hal, and when it becomes established over time and becomes strong, it itself becomes a malaka, just as the same person was a child and then becomes a man.)
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms in the psychology of qualities: malaka (disposition) and hal (state). It begins with a prefatory remark that abstract entities (al-mujarradat) are not placed under the genus of quality or accidents, and that psychic qualities can also apply to animal and plant souls. Then the core definition: if a psychic quality is firmly rooted in its subject such that it is difficult or impossible to remove, it is called malaka; otherwise it is called hal. The difference between them is due to an accidental cause (the rootedness or its absence), not a specific difference (fasl). Importantly, the same quality in the same individual can change from hal to malaka gradually over time, illustrated by the example of writing: initially a state, then through practice becomes a disposition. The comparison to a child becoming a man reinforces that this is a change in the same subject.

Key Terms
كيفية نفسانية
ملكة
حال
عارض
فصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think malaka and hal are different species of qualities.
Correction: They differ only by an accidental attribute (rootedness), as explicitly stated in the passage: 'the difference between them is by an accidental cause, not by a specific difference.'

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the difference between a malaka and a hal?
A) They differ by a specific difference (fasl).
B) They differ by an accidental cause (al-‘āriḍ) of rootedness or not.
C) Malaka is only for humans, hal for animals.
D) They are synonymous terms.

Key Takeaway
The same psychic quality can be a hal at first and become a malaka over time, the difference being only accidental rootedness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a good habit you have tried to develop. Is it still a hal (easily lost) or has it become a malaka? What concrete steps can you take today to help it become more rooted?
?
The lesson says the same quality can be a hal first and then become a malaka over time. Is there a spiritual quality (like patience, gratitude, or humility) that you have been working on but feel is still fragile? What would it take for you to practice it until it becomes a malaka?
?
Imagine you are the person giving the example of writing: you started with struggle, now it's effortless. What does that journey teach you about your own character development? Where are you still in the 'hal' stage, and are you willing to endure the gradual process to turn it into a malaka?

Defining Life as a Power Following Equilibrium
Defines life as a power consequent upon the specific equilibrium.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific definition of life in Islamic philosophy as a power dependent on the equilibrium of the species.

Core Idea
Life is a power contingent upon each species' specific temperament.

This passage launches a detailed discussion of life, which is considered the root of all other psychic powers. The author defines life not as a simple attribute but as a power that arises from the specific equilibrium (temperament) of each species. Understanding this definition is crucial for later discussions on the soul and its faculties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
The passage is the first aim under the first type (life) in a larger work. Notice that life is prioritized because it is foundational to other psychic qualities.
The author argues life gives precedence over other types.
2
Read the definition carefully
The matn states: 'Life is a power that follows that power the equilibrium of the species.' Understand that 'that power' refers to a power associated with equilibrium. The commentary explains that each species has a specific temperament (mizaj).
The definition is compact; the commentary expands it.
3
Understand the dependence on equilibrium
Life in every animal species is dependent on the specific temperament that suits the species' effects and properties. If the temperament leaves, the species ceases.
This highlights the contingency of life on physical composition.
4
Note the commentary clarifications
The commentary addresses a potential objection about innate dispositions (like infallibility) being called 'malaka' but not being 'hal'. It clarifies that the text's statement about 'every malaka was previously a hal' applies to acquired dispositions, not innate ones.
Be aware that the text here is incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الأول - المقصد الأول : تعريف الحياة ] ( في الحياة ) قدمها على سائر الأنواع، لأنها أصل لها ومستتبعة إياها ( وفيها ) أي في الحياة ( مقاصد ) ثلاثة : [ المقصد الأول : تعريف الحياة ] في تعريفها (الحياة قوة تتبع تلك القوة اعتدال) النوع . ومعنى ذلك أن كل نوع من أنواع المركبات العنصرية له مزاج مخصوص يناسب الآثار والخواص المطلوبة منه، حتى إذا خرج من ذلك المزاج لم يبق ذلك النوع كما سيأتي تفصيله إن شاء الله تعالى، فالحياة في كل نوع من أنواع الحيوانات تابعة لذلك المزاج المسمى قوله: (وكل ملكة إلخ) أي مكتسبة على ما في الشفاء فلا يرد أن الملكات الخلقية كعصمة الأنبياء عليهم السلام ملكات ولم تكن حالاً . قوله : ( كانت حالاً) إما بشخصه أو بنوعه .
Type One - First Aim: Definition of Life. (Concerning life) he gave it precedence over the other types, because it is an origin for them and they follow from it. (And in it) i.e., in life, (there are three) aims: [First Aim: Definition of Life]. In its definition: (Life is a power that follows that power the equilibrium) of the species. And its meaning is that every species of elemental compounds has a specific temperament that suits the effects and properties required from it, such that if it departs from that temperament, that species does not remain, as will come in detail, God willing. So life in every species of animals is dependent on that temperament called... His words: (And every malaka etc.) i.e., acquired, according to what is in al-Shifa', so it is not objected that congenital dispositions like the infallibility of the prophets (peace be upon them) are dispositions and were not states. His words: (was a hal) either in person or in type.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage introduces a new major section on life. It is the first aim under the first type (life). The author gives life precedence over other psychic qualities because it is their origin. Life is defined as a 'power that follows the equilibrium of the species'—meaning each species has a particular temperament (mizaj) that determines its life. If that temperament is lost, the species ceases. Then commentary clarifies that the statement 'every malaka was previously a hal' refers to acquired dispositions, not innate ones like prophethood. It also explains that 'was a hal' can mean either in person or in type. The text is incomplete as the OCR cuts off.

Definition of Life: Power Following Equilibrium
Defines life as the first psychological quality with its metaphysical basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of life as a power that follows the specific equilibrium of the species and how it serves as the source of all other animal powers.

Core Idea
Life is the root power that flows from the species' equilibrium, not the powers themselves.

This passage from al-Mawaqif introduces the first of the psychological qualities: life. The author provides a precise definition that distinguishes life from its effects, emphasizing its dependence on the specific equilibrium (i'tidal naw'i) of each species. We will unpack this definition and see how life is the source from which all other animal faculties emanate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Pay attention to the three parts: priority, definition, and explanation.
Notice the author begins by stating life is the first type of psychological quality.
2
Identify the definition and its components
Find the exact definition of life. Then understand the concept of 'specific equilibrium' (i'tidal naw'i) and how it relates to each species.
The definition is: 'Life is a power which follows that power the equilibrium of the species.'
3
Understand the causal chain
Trace the causal chain: specific equilibrium → life → other animal powers (sensation, movement, nutrition). See how life is both dependent on equilibrium and the source of other powers.
All this happens by God's decree, as the passage concludes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الأول : أصل الحياة وقدمها ( في الحياة ) قدمها على سائر الأنواع، لأنها أصل لها ومستتبعة إياها ( وفيها ) أي في الحياة ( مقاصد ) ثلاثة : [ المقصد الأول : تعريف الحياة ] في تعريفها الحياة قوة تتبع تلك القوة اعتدال النوع. ومعنى ذلك أن كل نوع من أنواع المركبات العنصرية له مزاج مخصوص يناسب الآثار والخواص المطلوبة منه، حتى إذا خرج من ذلك المزاج لم يبق ذلك النوع كما سيأتي تفصيله إن شاء الله تعالى، فالحياة في كل نوع من أنواع الحيوانات تابعة لذلك المزاج المسمى بالاعتدال النوعي. ويفيض منها سائر القوى الحيوانية كقوى الحس والحركة والتصرف في الأغذية. وتلخيصه أنه إذا حصل في مركب عنصري اعتدال نوعي يليق بنوع حيواني فاض عليه من المبدأ قوة الحياة ثم انبعثت منها قوى أخرى، أعني الحواس الباطنة والظاهرة والقوى المحركة إلى جلب المنافع ودفع المضار. كل ذلك بتقدير العزيز العليم فالحياة تابعة للاعتدال المذكور ومتبوعة لما عداها من القوى الموجودة في الحيوان.
The first type: The principle of life and its priority. (On life) He gave it priority over the other types because it is their principle and they follow from it. (And in it) i.e., in life, (there are) three objectives. [The first objective: Definition of life] In its definition: Life is a power which follows that power the equilibrium of the species. And the meaning of that is that every type of elemental compounds has a specific mixture (mizaj) that suits the effects and properties required from it, to the extent that if it departs from that mixture, that type does not remain—as will be detailed if God wills. So life in every type of animals is consequent upon that mixture called the specific equilibrium (i'tidal naw'i). And from it (i.e., that power) flow all the other animal powers, such as the powers of sensation, movement, and the management of nutrition. Its summary: When a specific equilibrium suitable for an animal type occurs in an elemental compound, from the Principle (al-mabda') a power of life flows upon it, then from it other powers emanate, namely the internal and external senses and the motive powers to attract benefits and repel harms. All of that is by the decree of the Mighty, the Wise. So life is dependent on the mentioned equilibrium and is followed by the other powers found in the animal.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage defines life and explains its metaphysical basis. It begins by stating that life is the first type of psychological quality because it is the foundation from which others follow. The definition given is: 'Life is a power that follows that power the equilibrium of the species.' This means that every species of elemental compounds has a specific mixture (mizaj) suited to its required effects; if this mixture is altered, the species ceases. In animals, life is consequent upon that mixture, called the specific equilibrium. From this life-power flow all other animal powers (sensation, movement, nutrition). A concise summary is provided: when the specific equilibrium appropriate for an animal type occurs in an elemental compound, a power of life flows from the Divine Principle, and from it emanate other powers—the external and internal senses, and the motive powers for seeking benefit and avoiding harm. All this is by God's decree. Life is thus subordinate to the equilibrium and is the source of the other powers in the animal.

Key Terms
حياة (hayat)
قوة (quwwa)
اعتدال نوعي (i'tidal naw'i)
مزاج (mizaj)
قوى حيوانية (quwa hayawaniyya)
مبدأ (mabda')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life is itself the power of sensation and movement.
Correction: The passage clarifies that life is a distinct power that flows from the equilibrium and is the source from which sensation, movement, and other powers emanate.

Checkpoint
According to the definition, what is life?
A) The power of sensation and movement.
B) The specific equilibrium itself.
C) A power that follows the specific equilibrium of the species.
D) The sum of all animal powers.

Key Takeaway
Life is defined as a power that depends on the species-specific equilibrium and is the source from which all other animal faculties emerge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your life is literally a power that flows from the Divine Principle only when your body maintains its specific equilibrium, how much attention do you actually pay to preserving that balance? What one habit are you indulging that disturbs the mizaj the Creator has designed for you?
?
The passage says from life emanate the powers to attract benefits and repel harms. Is your life currently oriented toward attracting what truly benefits your soul or merely what pleases your ego? Be honest: when you act, do you act from the life that God gives, or from a life you pretend to own?
?
You are alive right now only because God decrees that your body's mixture remains in equilibrium. If He withdrew that power for an instant, you would cease to be. Does this truth sit in your heart as a fact, or as a mere concept? What would it take for you to live each moment with the awareness that your very existence is a constantly renewed gift from al-‘Azīz al-‘Alīm?
